Mr. Paskin.

In handing me the papers now registered at 1 and 2, Mr. Gent instructed me to put them on the file relating to our future constitutional policy for Malaya. Since the correspondence mentions Force 136 fairly freely, I have had a special MS annex of our Constitutional file created.

I have not gone to the length of attaching the Constitutional file itself, since it is wanted in another connection. I think the present correspondence is self-explanatory, and I do not think any further action is required on it. We know from our latest meetings with him that Colonel Purcell has studied Colonel Willan's note, and that he is inclined to attach too much importance to this additional reason for creating a Malayan Union. Colonel Purcell, indeed, does not think a great deal of the coherence, size and effectiveness of the various resistance movements in Malaya.

Mr. Gent.

On Monday the 26th of January, the latest version of Col. Willan's memorandum about the future Malayan Constitution (see enclosure to No.2) was considered at a meeting in your room. The meeting was attended by yourself, Sir Harold MacMichael, Mr. Duncan, Mr. Paskin, Mr. Hastings and myself, as well as by Col. Newboult, Col. McKerron and Col. Willan on behalf of the M.P.U.

At the end of the meeting, it was agreed that Col. Willan would revise his memorandum as quickly as possible in the light of the discussions (covering also the points made in No.5 hereon), and would have 250 copies of the revised edition rolled off. He would send us copies, and we would then submit the memorandum to the Secretary of State together with a covering note, which would set out the various points which cannot be included in the memorandum itself, but which nevertheless need to be covered. These points, which concern procedure, timings etc. rather than the actual subject matter of the new proposals, were evolved in the course of our discussions.

The revised memorandum has not yet arrived, but I felt it would be as well to get along with the draft covering note as quickly as possible. I therefore submit a very tentative effort opposite. I am sending the papers straight to you in the first instance, so that you can see my effort before it goes any further. I imagine that you will want to discuss the draft at a further meeting, but perhaps this should await the arrival of the latest version of the memorandum, which is promised.

Sir G. Gater.
Secretary of State.

No. 12. In pursuance of the War Cabinet's policy directive on Malaya for the guidance of Planners, the Malayan Planning Unit (and particularly Colonel Willan their chief legal expert) and ourselves have been engaged in the considerable task of outlining a future constitution for the Malayan Union.

This Outline is Appendix A in No. 12. Annexed to it is a summary of its main features - Appendix B.

In its final stages the Outline has been discussed with Sir Harold MacMichael in view of the bearing which it has on his own task of convincing the Malay Rulers to cede jurisdiction to H.M. in their respective States. It will naturally occur to those Rulers to ask what H.M. proposes to do, when he has such jurisdiction, in the matter of a future constitution for Malaya, and Sir H. MacMichael must obviously be familiar with those plans which will need to be implemented in final and constitutional form ready to come into operation as soon as the period of military administration gives way to Civil Government.

In agreement with Sir H. MacMichael the covering memorandum has been compiled which completes

===== Page 11 =====
The picture by relating the whole project to his own initial task of securing from the Rulers the powers necessary for the effective introduction of the new constitution. The points in this covering memorandum are summarised in its last paragraph but I think I may say that the principal point is the importance of H.M.G. being firm in its resolve that H.M. should obtain jurisdiction in the Malay States so that H.M.G. may introduce a new constitutional order for the unity of Malaya. Sir H. MacMichael is convinced, and the Department agrees with him, that any appearance of hesitation or room for argument on that point will seriously risk the success of his mission to the Rulers who, like other Orientals, may be expected to probe for any opportunity for argument or delay in making up their minds. Such a firm resolve, however, will not necessarily mean that H.M.G. must commit itself to the details of the constitutional scheme now submitted, but the need for having a fresh constitution completed and ready in legal shape before the military period comes to an end, whether on the lines now submitted or on other lines which H.M.G. may prefer, is certain, and we have now no more time than is necessary for drafting all the necessary Instruments. I suggest that the Secretary of State may like to discuss this paper with the Department, Colonel Newboult and Colonel Willan of the M.P.U. (in General Hone's present absence in the Far East), and Sir Harold MacMichael, before the last named goes off in about three weeks' time to S.E.A.C. headquarters.

S. G. A.

Mr. Paskin

I spoke to Sir George Gater about this. He agreed so far as the Government of the future Malayan Unions is concerned, but thought that there was some inconclusive discussion going on on the basis of local State Governments being carried on as far as possible in the name of the Sultans. Paragraph 4 of the note requires us to give further consideration to the maintenance of a Sultan's position in public matters in his State in which he can be expected to have a personal interest and the importance of maintaining his "face" in respect of his Malay Advisory Council was stressed. We must consider whether in the more general aspects of Government a nominal, at least, form of ostensible authority can be preserved for the Sultans.

As Colonel Willan has now left, we ought to make headway with revising the note of constitutional proposals with the help of Mr. O'Connor and Mr. Hastings. Can you arrange another meeting please?

26th May, 1945

===== Page 16 =====
In Mr. Gent's unavoidable absence, Mr. Duncan, Mr. Paskin, Mr. Hastings, and myself discussed this on Wednesday of last week, assisted by Mr. O'Connor of the Malayan Planning Unit.

Almost the whole of our discussion was devoted to the position of the Sultans. The results may be tabulated as follows:

(a) Civil Lists (see paragraph 4 of No.13). The various State estimates show that in the past these have varied slightly from year to year in South States. The conclusion of our discussion was that this was a point on which generosity would be greatly to our advantage, and that the highest pre-1942 figure should be taken as a minimum in dealing with the future. That is to say, Sir Harold MacMichael should be empowered, in negotiating with the Sultans, to tell them that the highest pre-1942 figure would be allocated to them henceforward as a fixed annual grant. He should also be empowered, however, to promise a higher annual sum, at his own discretion, in the case of any individual Sultans if he regarded such a step as necessary in order to ensure the success of the negotiations.

(b) State property (see paragraph 3 of No.13). Before the meeting took place, I had already endeavoured to obtain the basis for a list of such State properties as might be excluded from the general transfer to the central government of the Union. I had met with no success. The State estimates are valueless in this respect since, for obvious reasons, they only make specific reference to such property when it becomes an individual object of expenditure. Besides consulting these estimates, I had spoken to Mr. Watherston of the Malayan Planning Unit, who had expressed the opinion that a complete list for any of the States would be quite unobtainable except on the spot. I have called for the back files dealing with the rebuilding of the Sultans' palaces in certain of the States, but I am very doubtful whether these will give me what is needed. They will no doubt contain relevant information, but this information will not be comprehensive, and it seems to me that a list of the kind desired must be comprehensive in order to be of any value at all. At the meeting I reported my failure to make any progress in this matter, and it was decided to submit to higher authority the suggestion that we should consult Sir Theodore Adams (a prominent ex-resident) and Mr. S.W. Jones (who had been Federal Secretary before becoming Colonial Secretary, Straits Settlements). It was felt that we could safely ask these gentlemen for information on the subject without disclosing our reasons; if the results were successful, we could consult other retired residents, of whom there are a number still living in this country. I now submit the suggestion as agreed at the meeting, but I must confess to a personal feeling that we may be rather wasting our time. The gentlemen whom we consult will only be able to speak from memory, and the information they give us will be at best eclectic. As I have said above, I think the lists must be comprehensive if they are to serve any real purpose. Moreover, I should be inclined to doubt the value of actual lists of the property in each State which might be excluded from transfer. What we want for our purposes (and I am wondering whether this is not what the Secretary of State had in mind) is surely a general statement of the kinds of State properties which would be suitable for exclusion. If it is decided that this is what we are really after, I think the answer can be given with little or no research. I suggest that the properties which we wish to exclude from transfer are the residences and estates of the Sultans themselves, the residences and estates of their relatives and dependants, and any buildings

===== Page 17 =====
and lands which are used for religious purposes. (We are of course only concerned with such property insofar as it is State property and not private property; in the latter event, the question of transfer does not arise).

(c) Functions (See paragraphs 4 and 5 of No.13 and immediately preceding minutes). We felt that it would be impracticable for the future administration of Malaya (either in matters affecting the central legislature or in those affecting the State Councils) to be conducted even nominally in the name of the Sultans. The fundamental objection to this is surely that it is just what happened in the past, and is just what we are trying to get away from. Even in the past the control exercised by the Sultans was purely nominal, in that they were bound to accept the advice of the Resident or the Adviser. But this nominal control, while it was powerless to do positive harm, nevertheless represented a dissipation of authority and a bar to progress; and it is precisely those disadvantages which the present proposals are designed to overcome. While we were convinced, however, that it would be impossible to preserve for the Sultans even a nominal control over the whole field of Government, we came definitely to the conclusion that in matters affecting their traditional position vis-a-vis their subjects - namely, Mohammedan religion and Malay customs - the final authority of the Sultans should be preserved not only in name but in fact. I feel sure that this view, which is in accordance with the expressed views of Colonel Newboult, and which received the strong support of Mr. O'Connor at the meeting, is correct. In the existing treaties, Mohammedan religion and Malay customs are expressly named as subjects on which the Sultans need not accept the advice of the British Resident or Adviser. The maintenance of the Sultans' final authority in these matters will in no way impair the working of the new constitution. On the other hand, it is a point on which the Sultans may be expected to insist more than on any other, since it so closely affects their personal prestige. In considering the implications of the proposals, we reached the conclusion that the suggested authority on matters of customs and religion should be exercised in their separate States by the individual Sultans in Council, and not by the Sultans as a body. I think this follows from the fact that custom varies widely from State to State. The result would be, of course, that the Advisory Councils to the Sultans, which are to be established in each State, would cease to be advisory on matters of Mohammedan religion and Malay customs, and would become sovereign legislative bodies in this limited field. On paper, this complicates the situation considerably, since there will be two separate legislative bodies in each State, neither of them owning the authority of the other. The respective spheres of responsibility will, however, be clearly demarcated, and no complications should arise in practice.

As stated at the beginning of this minute, the position of the Sultans took up the greater part of our discussion. But we also considered the possibility (see paragraph 6 of No.13) of defining more closely the constituencies which would send members to the new Legislative Council. On this point, we endorsed Mr. Gent's view, expressed in his minute of 18/5, that the demarcation of these constituencies will, to a large extent, depend on how far populations (especially Chinese miners and Indian rubber labourers) have been displaced as a result of war conditions, and that in the meanwhile further definition is not possible.

===== Page 18 =====
If the conclusions of our discussion, as recorded above, are approved, I think the first action will be to amend Colonel Willan's memorandum accordingly, and to consult Sir Theodore Adams and Mr. F.S. Jones about State property (if this is considered worthwhile). As soon as the memorandum has been revised (or even concurrently?), I think we should set about preparing the first draft of a memorandum which Sir Harold MacMichael could show to the Sultans (see paragraph 10 of 13), and by means of which he could acquaint them with our main constitutional proposals and above all reassure them on the maintenance of their personal prestige.

A. T. Bardilea

Sir G. Gater.

I was able to have a word with Mr. Gent about this today. He agrees generally with the proposals recorded in Mr. Bourdillon's minute of the 6th of June, of which those in paragraph (c) "Functions" are the most important from the political point of view. Mr. Gent agreed that we might well be able to devise a system whereby each Sultan in his "Advisory" Malay Council (which to this extent would no longer be "advisory") should be the legislative authority in each State for matters of Malay custom and religion. We should, of course, have to devise a further bit of machinery to ensure that no legislative acts of that Council infringe on the functions of the superior legislative body, but that will not be a matter of difficulty.

As to the next step, we suggest that, before these revised proposals are submitted to the Secretary of State, we should be authorised to discuss them with Sir H. MacMichael himself who will be due to arrive here in the near future.

H.T. B.
26

This was discussed further on the afternoon of Thursday, June the 28th, between Mr. Gent, Sir H. MacMichael, Sir H. Duncan, Mr. Paskin, Mr. Hastings and myself.

The major part of our discussions was taken up with the position of the Sultan in the new Malayan Constitution. In this connection, Sir H. MacMichael reverted to the suggestion that the Sultan should be given a nominal position in the whole field of State Administration (see Sir G. Gater's minute of 12/6), but only to say that he was now satisfied that this proposal was not practicable. He had

===== Page 23 =====
Apparently discussed the matter with General Hone and Colonel Willan during his recent trip to the East, and had been persuaded by them that it was an integral part of the new proposal that the Sultan should be removed even from nominal control. Colonel Willan in particular had been insistent that the retention by the Sultans of nominal control would strike at the root of the whole proposals. It was impossible to state as a matter of form that State legislation would in future require the assent of the Sultans, without such assent being required in fact. This would undermine the authority of the new central legislature, and would thus endanger the conception of the Malayan Union as a whole.

We then examined the more limited proposal (see section C in my minute of 6/6/45) that the Sultans should remain the legislative authority on matters of Mohammedan religion and Malay custom. Difficulty was at once seen in any suggestion that the Sultans, in legislating on these matters, should be subject to no control by the Governor. The suggestion recorded in my minute referred to above was indeed to this effect, but discussion revealed that this proposal would be likely to create acute problems. In this connection, the letter from Mr. Cator at No. 20 is very relevant. Mr. Cator points out that Malay custom is not a body of fixed rules but is highly elastic. Its field, in fact, is comprehensive and ill-defined. Sir H. MacMichael, from his experience of other Mohammedan countries, said the same thing about Mohammedan law. This means that the Sultans, in legislating on matters of Malay custom and Mohammedan religion, would be likely to trespass again and again into the sphere of ordinary administration, with unpredictable results, unless some control were maintained over their activities. It was felt, therefore, that the Governor must maintain ultimate control over the Sultans legislative authority. The next question was to consider what form this control should take.

Here it was felt that the Advisory Council of Sultans, which is to be set up under the new Constitution with the Governor as its President, could play a very useful role. After some discussion, it was agreed that the Governor's assent should be required before the enactments of any of the Sultans became law, and that the Governor should not merely exercise a power of disallowance (in the latter event, of course, the enactments would become law immediately upon promulgation, and would only be nullified in the event of disallowance in individual cases). Before granting or refusing his assent, the Governor would in all cases consult his Advisory Council of Sultans.

The picture thus built up is as follows. On matters exclusively concerned with Mohammedan religion and Malay custom, the Sultans would legislate in the individual States. In doing so, they

===== Page 24 =====
They would use the Advisory Councils provided in each State, which for these purposes would become more than merely advisory bodies. Legislation resulting from this system would not immediately become law but would be submitted to the Governor for his assent. The Governor would in no case grant or refuse his assent without consulting his Advisory Council of Sultans, though he would not be bound to accept their advice. At first sight, it might appear that this scheme tends to deprive the Sultans of such power as proposed in the suggestions recorded in my minute of 6/6 to leave in their hands. However, it was felt that the safeguard of ultimate control by the Governor was necessary. Moreover, this modification of the proposal to leave matters of Mohammedan religion and lay customs in the hands of the Sultans had a great positive advantage, as it gave some reality to the deliberation of the Governor's Advisory Council of Sultans, which would otherwise be little more than a façade.

Other less important matters were also discussed. Sir H. MacMichael made a pertinent point about the temporary body that is to precede the establishment of a properly constituted Legislative Council. The relevant passage in Colonel Willan's Memorandum (pages 26 and 27 of Appendix A to No.12) reads as follows:

"When Military Administration ceases and the new Civil Government is established in the Malay Peninsula, it will be impossible for that Government to set up a Legislative Council immediately. To cover the interregnum between the end of the Military Administration and the setting up of a Legislative Council, the new constitution should specifically provide for the establishment of an Advisory Council to the Governor which would temporarily take the place of a Legislative Council. This Advisory Council should consist of an unspecified number of official and unofficial members."

Sir Harold MacMichael pointed out that this passage did not make it clear whether the Advisory Council was in fact to be a Legislative body or not. He had consulted Colonel Willan on the point, and the latter, while admitting the wording was obscure, maintained that the Advisory Council should indeed be a Legislative body. Sir Harold maintained the opposite view. During the initial period, all responsibility would rest on the Governor's shoulders. In these circumstances, it was only right that he should have the power also. The meeting agreed with this view, and I was instructed to revise the wording of the passage in question. I suggest the following revision:

"When Military Administration ceases and the new Civil Government is established in the Malay Peninsula, it will be impossible for that Government to set up a Legislative Council immediately. During the interregnum between the end of the Military Administration and the setting up of a Legislative Council."

===== Page 25 =====
Council, the Governor alone will be responsible for Legislation, but the new Constitution should specifically provide for the establishment of an Advisory Council to assist him in this task. The Governor would normally consult this Advisory Council before enacting Legislation but would not be bound to accept the Council's advice. The Council should consist of an unspecified number of official and unofficial members.

Other points which we discussed arose out of Mr. Cator's letter at No. 20. These may be enumerated as follows:

(a) We agreed with Mr. Cator that the Sultan's right of Legislation in matters of Mohammedan religion and Malay customs must not extend to the collection of taxes or tithes, since this would lead to a pernicious system of double taxation.

(b) We felt that Mr. Cator's letter confirmed our previous impression that it would be impossible to draw up a neat and comprehensive list of the kinds of property to be excluded from transfer to Union ownership. The most that could be achieved would be a general statement of the kinds of State property which would be suitable for exclusion. Section of my minute of 6/6 cites the residencies and estates of the Sultans themselves, the residencies and estates of their relatives and dependants, and any buildings and land which are used for religious purposes. I think this is still about as near as we can get to the correct classification, though it was agreed at the meeting that we must be careful to limit the term "relatives and dependants" to include only those in the Sultan's immediate circle. In this connection, Sir Harold MacMichael stressed the importance of not giving any commitments to the Sultans which would prevent the Service authorities from taking control of property which they might require at some later date for strategic purposes. This point must clearly be borne in mind, but I think it was felt that its importance could be overstressed in this immediate connection. We are seeking to define the types of property which can be excluded from transfer to ownership by Malayan Union. The fact that such property would remain State property, or would remain at the disposal of the Sultans, would not mean that it would pass irrevocably out of the control of the Service authorities.

===== Page 26 =====
Position in this respect would be no worse than in the past, when all property had been State property, and when the Service authorities had not found cause for complaint.

(c) Agreement was expressed with Mr. Cator's view that in assuring the continuation of the Sultan's emoluments, these should be divided into two parts consisting first of the Sultan's personal allowance, and secondly of provision for his family. The importance of limiting the interpretation of the word "family" to the Sultan's immediate circle was stressed, and it was felt, as Mr. Cator had suggested, that the money should be held by trustees and should be carefully tied up as to capital and interest.

If the conclusions embodied in the above minute are approved, the first thing to do will be to get out a revised memorandum of Constitutional proposals. I hardly think we need revise Colonel Willan's Memorandum as such. This is rather lengthy, and the earlier pages of it are devoted to a recitation of past events and statements. I suggest that we should aim at a much shorter Memorandum (somewhat on the lines of Appendix B to No.12), which should embody the main Constitutional proposals with the latest amendments. This document could then serve as a basis for later documents - e.g. the Order-in-Council itself and the explanatory memorandum which could be handed to the Sultan (see last sentence of my minute of 6/6/45).

A. T. Boudillie
30.6.45.

===== Page 28 =====
The new policy for Malaya is now awaiting submission to Ministers for their definite confirmation.

We have in the meantime, for some months, been working on the consequent constitutional arrangements which would be required in the Malayan mainland (the "Malayan Union") and in Singapore. The memorandum now attached is an outline of the scheme we propose for the Malayan Union, and this is the territory of major political and economic importance—political because it raises the whole issue of the Malay Sultans and their future position in a progressive united Malaya.

I am sending on separately our proposals regarding Singapore which, according to the policy scheme, is for a period at any rate to be constituted a separate Colony and Government.

The Secretary of State will no doubt wish to discuss, but we have made the memorandum as concise as possible in order that he may see the outline proposed before Ministers take the policy paper.

The point of the telegram at 28 is in the words "not more than" in respect of the 21 Official Members of the proposed Legislative Council for the Malayan Union, the idea of the telegram being to suggest that while this would prohibit an increase in official membership, it would be possible to increase the unofficial membership above 21 in order to make possible an unofficial majority at some future date.

The phrasing of the final memorandum at 27 (incidentally the reference is to paragraph 5 not 3) is based on the final Willan memorandum (page 26) at 11 which in turn repeats the wording of the second draft Willan memorandum at 2. This last memorandum which was the result of discussions on the Constitution held last December, including the one of 11th December referred to in the telegram, does not use the phrase "not more than" but speaks without qualification of 21 officials. The point does not appear to have a reason before this query.

I take it that whatever the wording, the intention is to have a Legislative Council of 21 officials and 21 unofficials to begin with and that this would not preclude subsequent alterations towards setting up an unofficial majority. If that is so then the telegram at 28 is placing too precise and too final an interpretation on the memorandum.

Unless it is felt that the words "not more than" have a significance which it is important should be read into the memorandum at 27, I reply as in draft.

I am anxious to get some reply to General Hone's telegram at (31) submitted to Sir George Gater in the near future. You will recall that we had a few words before our general discussion yesterday on this particular telegram. I should myself have thought it extremely unlikely that there would be any State property of a religious character in the Malay States which was not connected with the Mohammedan religion. In these circumstances it might meet the point made by Sir Harold MacMichael if the relevant clause of the Order in Council were stated in general terms. I have not the temerity to attempt a draft myself, but I imagine that it would exclude from the transfer to Malayan Union any buildings or lands etc., now the property of the State Government and used for the purposes of religion. There would be no harm, I think, if this clause operated to retain under the authority of the State Councils property now allocated for other religions and if any difficulties arose they could presumably be dealt with by ad hoc legislation. I suggest therefore that it would be sufficient to reply to Sir Harold MacMichael on the lines of the attached draft.

I was asked a short time ago to prepare a draft White Paper on the policy for the future constitution of the Malayan Union at Singapore, with the intention, I understand, of it being published before Parliament rises.

The original draft is at 35. This has been discussed by Mr. Gent, Mr. Paskin, Col. O'Connor, and myself, and as a result, a second draft for a White Paper has been prepared and is now at (36) for consideration. The effects of these revisions have been to considerably reduce and streamline the original memorandum, which included a historical review in justification of the new policy and a fair amount of detail concerning the proposed constitutional arrangements. Additions are a reference to the future of Labuan in paragraph 3 and to the future functions of a Governor-General in the last paragraph.

The plan of the paper is as follows:

Para. 1. Present constitutional arrangements of Malaya.

Para. 2. Need for change.

Para. 3. Separate treatment of Singapore and Labuan.

Paras. 4 & 5. Need for new agreement with Rulers and the application of fresh legislation to enable the reconstitution of Malaya.

Paras. 6 - 11. The Malayan Union.

Paras. 12 - 13. The Colony of Singapore.

Para. 14. Pan-Malayan subjects.

Para. 15. The Governor-General.

This Paper is now submitted for consideration and approval.

===== Page 35 =====
I do not pretend by any means to be fully acquainted with what is intended in these constitutional changes, but the following points occur to me on the draft White Paper:

(1) The form and arrangement are such as to convey the general impression that we are thinking almost solely of the arrangements for the future Malayan Union and the position of the Colony of Singapore and of the Governor-General are very much afterthoughts. I should have thought the presentation would have been improved if we gave the necessary general account of the constitution of the new Colony of Singapore and of the position of the Governor-General immediately after paragraph 5 and before going into detail about the internal constitution of the Malayan Union.

(2) Paragraph 14 leaves a rather vague impression as to both the geographical area to be covered by the new Governor-General and as to the extent of his powers. This may be intentional, but questions are bound to be asked, e.g., whether Hong Kong is within his sphere; what will be his constitutional relationship to the Governors of the several territories; whether those Governors will communicate directly with the Secretary of State or only through the Governor-General; and so on. As those questions are bound to be asked, might it not be as well to try and anticipate by giving some answers in this paper?

(3) Presumably the municipality of Penang is to be continued in existence; if so, ought not reference be made to it or some other local authorities which will be continued in the Malayan Union? Are not questions likely to be asked, too, as to whether the same broad representative basis is to be given to the Penang municipality as is proposed for the Singapore municipality?

===== Page 36 =====
(4) Para. 10 deals with the transfer of assets and property to the new constitutional union. It suggests by implication that the new Colony of Singapore will take over all the assets of the Straits Settlements other than those relating to Penang, Malacca and Labuan. If this is so, ought it not to be explicitly stated?

(5) Para. 13 refers to particular agreements to regulate certain subjects of pan-Malayan importance, including currency. I imagine that no more is intended as regards currency than that the new constitutional union will take over the existing agreements between the Straits Settlements and the various Malay States, which govern the form of currency. If so, I think that ought to be made clear, as otherwise people may read the paragraph to mean that some new agreement is to be negotiated which may cast very undesirable doubts on the continuity of currency policy. The same may be true of other special subjects which are to be regulated by agreements.

(6) On a matter of pure form, the last sentence of para. 4 will presumably require amendment if, as I gather from Mr. Gent's minute above, the White Paper is likely to be issued after Sir Harold MacMichael has completed his negotiations, not while they are still in progress.

4.12.45.

===== Page 37 =====
I agree generally with Mr. Caine. In particular, I think that some rearrangement is most desirable, and that points expressly dealt with now only as respects the Malayan Union should equally be mentioned for Singapore and vice versa. Paragraph 3 appears to anticipate paragraphs 4 and 5 and should, I suggest, come after them. The logical arrangement appears to be to state first of all the general scheme covering the Union of Singapore and also the Cocos Islands, Labuan, and Christmas Island, then set out the proposed constitution for Singapore (including the "initial phase" to which there is only a reference by way of implication in paragraph 12), then the constitution of the Malayan Union, followed by paragraph 13 (subjects of pan-Malayan importance), paragraph 7 (Malayan citizenship), paragraph 9 (courts), paragraph 10 (property), the last sentence of paragraph 6 (transitional provisions if needed at all, which I doubt), paragraph 14 (Governor General).

It seems desirable to have marginal notes to all the paragraphs if any are to have them. As regards paragraph 5, the present second sentence requires modification in view of the actual terms of the Bill. I have made a suggestion in which I have assumed that when this paper is published the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill will in fact be before Parliament.

Paragraph 7. I assume that my alteration of sub-paragraph (b) is correct. The usual expression is "ordinarily resident." The sentence about British nationality should surely be extended to all British subjects who acquire Malayan Union citizenship. It does not apply only to those born in Penang and Malacca. In the last line but one, "local Councils" appears not to cover Legislative Councils and Executive Councils, but presumably it should.

In paragraph 8, second sentence, "approval" seems to be better than "agreement."

===== Page 38 =====
As regards paragraph 9, I suppose it has been decided to refer to the "High Court" of the Union and the "Supreme Court" of Singapore, though I am not sure why. I should have thought one court of appeal for the two territories would be sufficient. This, however, might cause complications in legislation, and as the Judges of one court will be ex officio Judges of the other, the point appears to be of no practical importance. The constitutions will not provide for appeals to the Privy Council; right of appeal will, however, exist.

Paragraphs 11 and 12. I agree with Mr. Caine that the question of municipalities should be dealt with rather more fully, and as respects the Malayan Union as well as Singapore. Incidentally, General Hone raised a question which may affect these paragraphs.

Mr. Robert Wray.

Mr. Caine's Minute of 4/12/45.

Paragraph 1. I agree.

Paragraph 2. We cannot, I understand, disclose the territories to be in the sphere of the Governor-General until further progress has been made regarding Borneo and Sarawak; and I do not think that the Governor-General's functions have yet been finally defined, though there is a paper now in circulation about them. I agree with General Hone that to call him "Governor General" (or "Governor" anything) is likely to have an unfortunate effect (in native estimation) on the position of the Governor of Singapore. I suggest that, if possible, the word "Governor" in the title should be avoided. "High Commissioner" would be unsuitable, as that is associated in the public mind with the F.M.S.

It is difficult to think of an alternative. "His Majesty's Agent General" is the best I can suggest and that is not good.

===== Page 41 =====
Your Minute dated 5/12/45.

Paragraph 1. I agree. I think "paragraph 7" in line 18 should be "paragraph 12".

Paragraph 2. I agree.

Paragraph 3. I agree. In Willan's Memorandum he recommends that membership of the Executive Council be confined to M.U. citizens. He does not mention Leg. Co., but it should be included. I think Mr. Morgan intended "Local Councils" to include In. Co. & Leg. Co., but the Ferdinand is not a good one.

Paragraph 4. Yes.

Paragraph 5. This is Willan's nomenclature. He says, on page 33 of his Memorandum, "The term High Court is used because it appears to be the custom to reserve the term 'Supreme Court' for Crown Colonies."
On the other hand, I think that "Supreme Court" is the more usual term when the Court has jurisdiction over a Colony and a Protectorate, e.g., Kenya, Gambia, Nigeria, and the Gold Coast. Penang and Malacca are no longer a Colony, but they are part of a former Colony. Previously it was the "Supreme Court" of the F.M.S. I doubt whether it matters very much, but I should like the point to be decided as it affects my draft Order in Council.

The question of a joint Court of Appeal is dealt with and rejected by Willan at page 32. As you say, the point is not of much practical importance, but I think that probably Willan is right. This is a reform which might come later. I agree that the new Constitution will not "provide for" appeals to the Privy Council. The F.M.S. Appeal Order in Council 1912 will be superseded.

I have recast the Third Draft of the White Paper, following the order of subjects suggested by Mr. Roberts Wray and covering as far as possible points of amendment suggested in the previous minutes. The paper now takes the following plan. The figures in brackets refer to the corresponding paragraphs in the Third Draft.

I. Previous Position (1)

Line 6. The geographical description of Cocos and Christmas Island is difficult, but "south of Sumatra" is rather more accurate than "south of Java".

Last two lines: "Five" should read "six" and "nine" should read "ten", as Perlis should be included.

2. Need for Reform (2)

Line 2. I have inserted a negative to read "not inappropriate" to make sense of this passage.

3. H.M.'s Jurisdiction in Malay States (4)

Line 3. "Malaya" to read "the Peninsula".

Line 12. I have altered the tense to cover whether Sir H. MacMichael has or has not concluded his mission.

4. Outline of Proposals (5)

Line 3. The definite of Malay States has been made more precise to avoid confusion with Labuan.

===== Page 43 =====
Second sentence: It should be noted that this assumes the S.S. (Repeal) Bill will be before Parliament when this paper is published.

5. Special Position of Singapore (3)

Attention should be drawn to the statement about Labuan. Is this to be disclosed?

6. The Colony of Singapore (9 and 10)

This has been brought forward in the Paper and paragraph 11 of the Third Draft put into it on the same lines as paragraph 10 for the Malayan Union. I have kept the words "Municipal Commission" and not "Municipality" as Mr. Roberts Wray has suggested in the Third Draft, since the latter term refers to the town area and the local assembly.

The major part of this paragraph assumes that we are proceeding on agreed lines and not on those now put forward by General Hone for an all-embracing Island Council, with executive Boards.

7. The Malayan Union (6)

I have omitted the last sentence about transitional provisions, as suggested by Mr. Roberts Wray.

We should consider whether the remarks about local government and State Councils require further elaboration, since Mr. Caine and Mr. Roberts Wray both suggest that equal point should be given to municipalities in the Malayan Union, that of Penang in particular, as to the Municipality in Singapore. I think, however, that the balance of elaboration is between the Municipality of Singapore and its nearest parallels, the State and Settlement Councils, and that we should not enter into fuller details of local government in the Malayan Union, particularly

===== Page 44 =====
Particularly, as the Penang Municipality and other details of local government have not yet been worked out and are to be discussed with Brigadier Willan when he arrives here shortly, I suggest, therefore, that the present passage should stand as drafted.

8. Malay Rulers (8)

Line 11: "approval" reads "for" "agreement"

9. Pan-Malayan Subjects (13)

To meet the point made by Mr. Caine, I have substituted the general phrase "matters of common arrangement" for "particular agreements".

10. Malayan Union Citizenship (7)

In view of criticisms, "local Councils" as not being sufficiently explicit, has been changed to "Central and Local Councils".

11. Courts (9)

A decision is needed to settle whether it should be the "High Court" or "Supreme Court" of the Malayan Union, Col. O'Connor accepting Brig. Willan's preference for the former and Mr. Roberts Wray favouring the latter.

12. Transfer of Property (10)

Regarding the second sentence, in spite of Mr. Caine's criticism, I have let the passage stand since Col. O'Connor says the proposal is as stated.

===== Page 45 =====
13. Governor General (14)

This paragraph, as a first disclosure of an important new appointment, will provoke questions, at a time when details have not been finally settled as to what should be the title, functions, and relationships with the Governors of the Governor General. I understand this subject was raised by Gen. Hone during his visit.

I suggest this paragraph be omitted as this would not affect the rest of the Paper.

The Fourth Draft is submitted for consideration.

The Fifth Draft was reconsidered this morning in the light of Mr. Creech Jones' comments by Mr. Gent, Mr. Lloyd, Mr. Mayle, Col. O'Connor, and myself. Note was also taken of suggestions put forward by the Sultans of Kelantan and Perak in connection with Malayan Union citizenship.

The following is a summary of the main changes effected.

Para. 2. Need for reform. This has now been substantially expanded to indicate the imperative need for changes to suit Malaya to post-war conditions and aims.

Para. 3. H.M. to possess jurisdiction in the Malay States.

This has been modified to include the successful conclusion of Sir H. MacMichael's mission. Further points can, it is felt, be elaborated either in the public announcement about the mission or in the Parliamentary debate.

Para. 5. Special position of Singapore. The last sentence has been re-worded so as not to leave future amalgamation as a matter only for agreement between the Malayan Union and Singapore.

Para. 6. Colony of Singapore. In the last sentence, "full and effective" reads for "adequate" representation.

Para. 8. Malay Rulers. This section generally indicates the new standing of the Malay Rulers and their relationship to the Malays. A new sentence covers their financial position.

Para. 9. Subjects of pan-Malayan Importance.

It was not intended that the list of subjects

===== Page 50 =====
would necessarily be inclusive. Higher Education has been included.

Para. 10. Malayan Union Citizenship.
I am circulating separately (50823/7/3) papers dealing with views submitted by the Rulers, some of whom are exercised about this subject. The substantial change is the addition of the sub-paragraph requiring that all, except those acquiring citizenship by birth, shall take an oath of allegiance to the Union.

Para. 13. Governmental

As it is difficult at this stage to be specific about Borneo affairs and as this paper is dealing with Malaya only, it is suggested that the Borneo reference should be confined to the final passage of the paragraph.

Generally it was felt that the Paper should stand as a brief statement confined to constitutional matters, and that the elaboration of these and the discussion of any other Malayan topics that may be revised in the Parliamentary debate should be dealt with orally.

If this Sixth Draft is now approved, it is necessary to consider the timetable for its approval by the Colonial Affairs Committee and the Cabinet, and for its publication. It is considered desirable that the Paper should be disclosed in Malaya simultaneously with its publication in this country. At the same time it should be laid before Parliament at the same time as the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill so introduced into the Lords at the end of the Recess, i.e.

===== Page 51 =====
22nd January. Mr. Melville informs me that this Bill has not yet been introduced in the Lords and must necessarily wait on the publication of the White Paper.

It is suggested therefore that arrangements should be made for this Paper to be considered by the Colonial Affairs Committee not later than Friday this week and to be submitted for approval by the Cabinet at a meeting on the earliest possible day next week.

Mr. Lloyd

I have suggested to Mr. Robertson that Mr. Gent and Mr. O'Connor should be warned in case Mr. Creech-Jones would like them to be present.

The note and the White Paper should be available to Cabinet Offices for distribution by Thursday evening. A copy of the draft in its latest form is attached at the top of this file. It is, of course, subject to any comments the Secretary of State may have.

1. INTERNATIONAL TRUSTEESHIP SYSTEM: FUTURE OF TERRITORIES NOW UNDER UNITED KINGDOM MANDATE

The Committee had before them a memorandum by the Secretary of State for the Colonies (C.(46) 2) regarding the future of the territories now under United Kingdom mandate.

THE SECRETARY OF STATE FOR THE COLONIES explained that there had been some discussion in the Cabinet before Christmas of the proposal that His Majesty's Government should declare their intention of placing the Mandated Territories (with the exception of Trans-Jordan) under the international trusteeship system. As a result, he had been requested to obtain the views of the Chiefs of Staff and the Dominions. This had now been done, though the views of the Australian Government had still to be received. He was anxious to resubmit the matter to the Cabinet at their meeting on the 10th of January. In the first place, however, he wished to have the views of this Committee. He apologized for the length of the paper now before them, but the matter was important and technical and required full consideration. His recommendations were summed up in paragraph 24 of the memorandum.

In discussion of these recommendations, the following points were made and decisions reached as indicated:

(i)(a) That the African mandated territories of Togoland, Cameroons, and Tanganyika should be placed under trusteeship.

It had been the assumption of all discussions in recent months that the African mandated territories should be placed under trusteeship.

Much depended on the terms of the Trusteeship Agreements. The concurrence of the United Kingdom as the mandatory power was necessary to these agreements and we should insist before giving our concurrence that they should be drawn up in terms satisfactory to us. There would be great advantage in His Majesty's Government taking the initiative in preparing a draft.

It should be possible under the United Nations Charter to make agreements which were more satisfactory than the existing mandates in two respects; first, as regards the defense arrangements and, secondly, as regards the economic provisions; it could now be ensured that these were in the best interests of the inhabitants. Under the new regime, however, there would be provision for inspection on behalf of the Trusteeship Council, an arrangement which had not existed under the mandate system.

THE COMMITTEE:

(1) Agreed that the Mandated Territories of Togoland, Cameroons, and Tanganyika should be placed under trusteeship, provided that satisfactory Trusteeship Agreements could be negotiated.

===== Page 68 =====
(i)(b) That Trans-Jordan should be given independent status.

THE FIRST LORD OF THE ADMIRALTY, on behalf of the Secretary of State for Air, expressed the wish that the advice of the Chiefs of Staff should be obtained on the effects of the course proposed.

THE SECRETARY OF STATE FOR FOREIGN AFFAIRS said that he was satisfied that better provision would be possible for our defence interests by this course than by placing Trans-Jordan under trusteeship. If Trans-Jordan were placed under trusteeship, any special requirements for defence purposes would need the concurrence of the Trusteeship Council and this might be difficult to obtain. If it were to be made independent, it should not be difficult to obtain our requirements in the new Treaty which would have to be made with Amir Abdullah.

THE SECRETARY OF STATE FOR THE COLONIES said that, if the Cabinet approved the general principle, the Chiefs of Staff would be invited to consider what provisions would be required in the new Treaty.

THE COMMITTEE:

(2) Agreed, on this understanding, to the proposal that steps should be taken to recognise the independent status of Trans-Jordan.

(i)(c) That action in regard to Palestine should be deferred pending the report of the Anglo-American Commission.

THE COMMITTEE:

(3) Agreed to this course.

(ii) That Togoland, Cameroons and Tanganyika territory need not be designated as strategic areas.

It was generally agreed that there was no need for Togoland and the Cameroons to be designated as strategic areas.

The Government of the Union of South Africa had expressed deep concern about the strategic position on the eastern side of Africa. In the light of this view, the Chiefs of Staff had considered afresh that morning the question whether Tanganyika should be designated as a strategic area. The Committee were informed that the material part of their conclusion was as follows:

"There are military arguments both for and against designating Tanganyika as a strategic area, the arguments against such a step being if anything stronger. It is appreciated that the political disadvantages of so designating the area are considerable and we therefore conclude that, having regard both to the military and political factors, Tanganyika should not be designated a strategic area."

===== Page 69 =====
THE COMMITTEE:
(4) Took the view that, having regard to the strong political arguments as set out in C.(46) 2, none of the three territories should be designated as strategic areas.

(iii) That the draft Trusteeship Agreement at Annex III to C.(46) 2 should be approved as a basis for international negotiation for the African Mandated Territories.

THE SECRETARY OF STATE FOR DOMINION AFFAIRS took the view that Dominion Governments ought to see the draft Agreement before it was circulated internationally.

THE COMMITTEE:
(5) Agreed that the draft should be accepted as a basis for negotiation and that it should be circulated to Dominion Governments forthwith.

(iv) That the draft Trusteeship Agreement at Annex III to C.(46) 2 should in the first instance be sent for concurrence to the Belgian Government in respect of Tanganyika and to the French Government in respect of Cameroons and Togoland, with copies for information to the other "Big Five" members of the Trusteeship Council and to the Secretary-General of the United Nations Organisation.

THE SECRETARY OF STATE FOR THE COLONIES pointed out that under Article 79 of the United Nations Charter the terms of trusteeship for each territory had to be agreed upon by the "states directly concerned". There was no clear indication as to the meaning of this term. It could in fact be interpreted in several different ways and the question was one which would have to be discussed internationally. Meanwhile, he proposed an interim method of procedure, as set out in paragraph 19 of C.(46) 2, which could be carried out without prejudice to the general position.

THE SECRETARY OF STATE FOR DOMINION AFFAIRS said that the Government of South Africa had expressed the desire that they should be recognised from the outset as a state directly concerned in regard to all the Mandated Territories in East and West Africa.

THE SECRETARY OF STATE FOR THE COLONIES and THE MINISTER OF STATE expressed considerable concern at the possible repercussions of such recognition in relation to Mandated Territories not only in Africa but elsewhere. For instance, a similar position might have to be accorded to Egypt in regard to Mandated Territories in Africa. It might also affect the number and identity of the states directly concerned in the future of the Mandated Territories in the Western Pacific.

It was also pointed out that, under the Committee's previous decision, the draft Agreement would in fact be circulated to all the Dominion Governments, whether or not they could be regarded as technically "states directly concerned", before it was given any international circulation.

===== Page 70 =====
On the other hand, it was argued that the Council of Foreign Ministers had agreed that South Africa was directly concerned in the peace negotiations with Italy and it was felt that the arguments in favour of recognising South Africa's claim were strong.

THE COMMITTEE:

(6) Agreed that the Union of South Africa must in fact be regarded as directly concerned in the future of our Mandated Territories in Tropical Africa. Further consideration should be given to the exact means of giving effect to this conclusion.

(v) That the Dominion Governments and the Governors of the territories concerned should be informed of our policy, and that the latter should be consulted as to the exact terms in which it should be announced.

After discussion, THE COMMITTEE:

(7) Agreed that this recommendation should be reworded as follows:

(a) that the terms of the announcement should be forwarded to Dominion Governments for their observations; and

(b) that the Secretary of State for the Colonies should undertake any necessary consultation regarding the terms of the announcement with the Governors of the territories concerned.

(vi) That an appropriate statement covering the above points should be made by the British Delegation at the earliest suitable moment at the forthcoming meeting of the General Assembly of the United Nations, without waiting for Parliament to re-assemble.

The Committee thought that there was much force in the argument that it was to our advantage to take the initiative in the practical application of the trusteeship system and for this purpose to make a statement of our intentions at the earliest possible moment during the First Session of the General Assembly of the United Nations.

It was reported to the Committee, however, that the French Cabinet were now understood to have deferred consideration of the whole principle of placing French Togoland and the Cameroons under the trusteeship system in order that they might consult further with local consultative bodies. It seemed clear, therefore, that the French Delegation would not be in a position to make any statement regarding French policy in the very near future and a statement by the British Government might be embarrassing to them. They might, indeed, ask us to slow down our tempo to match them.

===== Page 71 =====
THE SECRETARY OF STATE FOR FOREIGN AFFAIRS was invited to consider the possibility of urging the French to change their point of view, but it was generally felt that the arguments in favour of an early statement on our part regarding our intentions outweighed the disadvantage of possible embarrassment to the French.

There was considerable discussion as to the timing of any statement. It would be very useful if it could be included in the speech to be made by the Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs very soon after the opening of the Session of the General Assembly of the United Nations on 10th January. On the other hand, Parliament might consider that a statement of this importance should first be made to them and Parliament would not be sitting before the 22nd January.

It was agreed that this point could be covered if (a) the statement was to the effect that "His Majesty's Government propose to take the necessary steps" to carry out their intentions on the various matters, and (b) the statement was then repeated in the House of Commons as soon as Parliament reassembled.

It was felt that there was nothing in the proposed statement which would be inconsistent with any assurances given in Parliament. Before making the statement, however, the Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs would confirm this by reference to previous statements.

THE COMMITTEE:

(8) Agreed that, subject to the points above, an appropriate statement should be made at the earliest suitable moment at the forthcoming Session of the General Assembly of the United Nations.

(9) Agreed that the statement should subsequently be repeated in Parliament as soon as it reassembled.

(10) Invited the Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs to consider the possibility of urging the French Government to reach an early decision of policy regarding French Mandated Territories.

2. MALAYA: DRAFT STATEMENT OF POLICY FOR THE FUTURE CONSTITUTION OF THE MALAYAN UNION AND THE COLONY OF SINGAPORE

The Committee had before them a memorandum by the Secretary of State for the Colonies (C.(46) 1), covering the draft of a White Paper on the constitutional proposals for Malaya.

The SECRETARY OF STATE FOR THE COLONIES said that it was very desirable to supplement the brief statement made in Parliament on the 10th October before the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill was taken in Parliament during the next Sitting. In the last few months Sir Harold MacMichael had visited Malaya and had been very successful in reaching Agreements with the Malay Rulers which, supplementing the existing Treaties, granted full jurisdiction in each territory to His Majesty.

===== Page 72 =====
THE SECRETARY OF STATE FOR INDIA said that the proposals regarding the position of the Malay Rulers would be very carefully scrutinised by Indian Rulers. They at present had a much larger measure of autonomy than the Malay Rulers, but some alteration of their position in the near future was inevitable. Action taken in Malaya would have repercussions in India and he hoped that in working out the details of the proposals officials of the Colonial Office would keep in close touch with officials of the India Office.

THE MINISTER OF STATE suggested that in the timing of the issue of the White Paper it would be important to have regard to the progress made by the Dutch in the Netherlands East Indies with the proposals which they had discussed here with the Prime Minister. It was important to ensure that our proposals did not cause embarrassment to the Dutch, who might already have difficulty in persuading the more intransigent sections of Dutch opinion to agree to the very liberal proposals which they were about to put forward.

THE COMMITTEE:

(1) Approved the draft White Paper attached to C.(46) 1 for submission to the Cabinet.

(2) Invited the Secretary of State for the Colonies to consult further with the Minister of State regarding the exact date of its publication, having regard to the difficulties of the Dutch in the Netherlands East Indies.

(3) Invited the Secretary of State for the Colonies to keep in close touch with the Secretary of State for India in making any future arrangements affecting the constitutional position of the Malay Rulers.

THE SECRETARY OF STATE FOR DOMINION AFFAIRS drew attention to the fact that under the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill it was not necessary to obtain the prior approval of the House of Commons to Orders in Council relating to the constitution of the Straits Settlements, nor had the Orders to be laid before the House after issue.

THE LORD CHANCELLOR and THE FIRST LORD OF THE ADMIRALTY thought that the provision was one which might well be criticised and an amendment moved. It might be well to forestall criticism by themselves proposing an amendment.

THE PARLIAMENTARY UNDER-SECRETARY OF STATE FOR THE COLONIES said that it had not been the general principle that Orders in Council relating to Colonial constitutions should require the prior approval of Parliament. The question whether a change in this general principle should now be made had been discussed at a meeting of Ministers under the chairmanship of the Lord

===== Page 73 =====
President shortly before Christmas. There were certain difficulties about making a change. These were now being considered and a further meeting was then to be held. In this particular case the time factor was important. Military administration was to end in Malaya on 1st March and it was necessary that plans for civil administration should be ready by then. The substance of the Order in Council to be issued would be contained in the White Paper and it was hoped that this would forestall criticism on this particular point. He also hoped that by the time the Bill was debated in Parliament it would have been possible to reach a decision on the general question.

THE COMMITTEE:

(4) Took note of the position.

Cabinet Office, S.W.1.

8TH JANUARY, 1946.

===== Page 74 =====
CABINET

MALAYAN CONSTITUTIONS.

Note by the Acting Chairman of the Colonial Affairs Committee.

As my colleagues are aware, the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill is to be introduced in the House of Lords at the beginning of the next Session. This Bill is required to give effect to the new constitutional measures for Malaya which the Secretary of State for the Colonies announced in the House of Commons on the 10th October, 1945 (Hansard Volume 414, No. 13, Column 255).

2. The announcement only briefly outlined the proposed constitutional arrangements for the Malayan Union and Singapore, and it is desirable that further information in the form of a White Paper should be available for Members of both Houses when the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill is taken.

3. The Colonial Affairs Committee considered at a meeting on the 7th January, a draft White Paper which was prepared by the Secretary of State for the Colonies and the text of which is contained in the Annexe, and agreed to submit for early Cabinet approval a recommendation that the White Paper should be laid not later than the beginning of the next Session.

4. I trust that my colleagues will agree to this.

(Intld.) A.

DOMINIONS OFFICE,
DOWNING STREET,
8th January, 1946.

===== Page 75 =====
Statement of Policy for the Future Constitution of the Malayan Union and the Colony of Singapore.

Previous Position.

1. The territories concerned in this statement comprise first, the Colony of the Straits Settlements (that is to say Singapore, Penang, and Malacca, in Malaya; Labuan off the north coast of Borneo; and Christmas Island and the Cocos-Keeling Islands to the south of Sumatra), secondly, the Federated Malay States of Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan, and Pahang, in the Malay Peninsula; and thirdly, the Unfederated Malay States of Johor, Kedah, Kelantan, Trengganu, and Perlis, also in the Malay Peninsula. The Colony of the Straits Settlements was, before its occupation by the Japanese, under direct British administration. In the Malay States, on the other hand, British authority rested upon agreements concluded with the Rulers, under which each Ruler retained sovereignty in his State, though each was bound to accept British advice on all matters except Mohammedan religion and Malay custom. Administrative coordination was achieved by the Governor of the Straits Settlements in his capacity as High Commissioner for the Malay States. As Governor of the Straits Settlements, he was in direct charge of the Colony's administration; on matters pertaining to the Federated Malay States, as High Commissioner, he exercised his functions through the British Resident in each of the four States and through the Federal Secretary at the Headquarters of the Federal Government (Kuala Lumpur); on the business of the Unfederated Malay States, again as High Commissioner, he exercised his functions through an Adviser in each State. This coordination had, however, to be effected through a variety of legislatures and administrations. Legislation in the Colony was enacted by the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements, legislation in the Federated Malay States by the Federal Council and each State Council, and legislation in each Unfederated Malay State by the State Council of each State. Uniformity of legislation in the Malay States, therefore, required separate action by at least six, and in some cases by ten, legislatures.

2. This divided and separatist position in a country significantly larger than England was formerly not inappropriate and in many ways indeed can be shown to have represented a necessary and useful stage in the development of the several States. But the increasing complexity of modern administrative, economic and social developments.

===== Page 76 =====
Developments demand a system of government less cumbersome, more adequate for large common services, and making better use of time and labour. A return to the old position would be manifestly contrary to the interests of the territories and their inhabitants in the post-war world, with its difficulties arising out of the war, and, for Malaya, the special problems created by the Japanese occupation. A stage has now been reached when the system of government should be simplified and reformed. International relations as well as the security and other interests of the British Commonwealth require that Malaya should be able to exercise an influence as a united and enlightened country appropriate to her economic and strategic importance. On a longer view, too, the pre-war system will not lend itself to that political adjustment which will offer, through broad-based institutions in which the whole community can participate, the means and prospect of developing Malaya's capacity in the direction of responsible self-government. In this development, all those who have made the country their homeland should have the opportunity of a due share in the country's political and cultural institutions. Efficiency and democratic progress alike demand therefore that the system of government should be simplified and reformed.

H.M. to possess jurisdiction in Malay States.

3. The British Crown must provide the common link which will draw together the communities of Malaya and promote a sense of common interest and the development of common institutions. Under the old situation, His Majesty had no jurisdiction in any of the Malay States, although under the Treaties the Malay Rulers had to accept British advice (except in matters of religion and custom). The new policy, however, requires that His Majesty should have jurisdiction in the Malay States so that he may be enabled to legislate for all the States alike under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. Accordingly, Sir Harold MacMichael, as Special Representative of His Majesty's Government, has been engaged on a mission to the Malay States and has successfully concluded with each of the Malay Rulers, after consultations conducted with friendliness and goodwill, an Agreement which, supplementing the existing Treaties, grants full jurisdiction in each State to His Majesty.

For the directions given to Sir Harold MacMichael see Appendix 7.

Outline of proposals.

4. It is proposed that the present combination of the Straits Settlements of Penang, Malacca, and Singapore in one political unit should be readjusted on the following lines:

(a) the Settlement of Singapore should be a separate Colony; and

===== Page 77 =====
(b) The Settlements of Penang and Malacca should be administered with the Malay States in a Malayan Union.

These proposals will be carried into effect, so far as the Malay States are concerned, under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act, 1890, and, as regards the Straits Settlements, under legislation now before Parliament (the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill).

5. In considering the need for a closer political integration in Malaya, His Majesty's Government consider that, at least for the time being, Singapore requires separate treatment. It is a centre of entrepot trade on a very large scale and has economic and social interests distinct from those of the mainland. It is recognised, however, that there were and will be close ties between Singapore and the mainland, and it is no part of the policy of His Majesty's Government to preclude or prejudice in any way the fusion of Singapore and the Malayan Union in a wider union at a later date should it be considered that such a course were desirable.

6. As mentioned above, the Settlement of Singapore, with which will be included the Cocos-Keeling Islands and Christmas Island, will be constituted as the Colony of Singapore under a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. During a transitional phase between the termination of the present military administration and the inauguration of the new constitution, the Governor will be empowered to legislate with the help of an Advisory Council, which he will be instructed to select on a basis as broadly representative as conditions in this phase allow. During this phase he will be instructed to give careful consideration to the questions of the composition of the Executive Council and of appointment and election to the Legislative Council, so as to ensure full and effective representation of the various sections of the community.

For the purposes of local government, the Municipality of Singapore, the Harbour Board, and the Singapore Improvement Trust will be reconstituted and set to work with the minimum of delay. During the transitional phase mentioned above the Governor will be instructed to give careful consideration to broadening the sphere of authority of the Municipal Commission and its basis of representation.

===== Page 78 =====
7. The constitution of the Malayan Union will provide for a central authority consisting of a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. In each State and Settlement there will be a local Council which will have such powers of administrative and subsidiary legislation delegated to it by the central authority, as are appropriate to the purposes of local government. These Councils, both central and local, will consist of official and unofficial members; but, while it is the intention that their constitution shall be broad-based and representative, the final determination of numbers and of the details of representation will not be decided until there has been consultation with local opinion. In the initial phase after the termination of the Military Administration and before the new constitution is brought into effect, the Governor will be empowered to enact necessary legislation with the help of an Advisory Council, which he will be instructed to select on a basis as broadly representative as conditions in this phase allow. During this phase the Governor will be instructed to complete his local consultations with a view to the formal institution of the Executive and Legislative Councils as soon as possible.

These changes in the legislative system will have respect for the continuity of the principles of land policy in the Malay States. The Malay Lands Reservation policy, in particular, will be maintained.

8. His Majesty's Government have borne in mind the special position of the Malay Rulers as traditional and spiritual leaders of the Malay people. It is intended, therefore, that the Ruler in each State shall preside over a Malay Advisory Council whose members he will appoint, subject to the approval of the Governor. The main functions of these Malay Advisory Councils will relate to matters affecting Mohammedan religion; but they will also advise the Ruler on other matters at the request of the Resident Commissioner acting with the Governor's approval. On matters of Mohammedan religion, excepting the collection of tithes and taxes, each Ruler, with the help of his Council, will have legislative powers within his State. Such legislation will require the Governor's assent, and for this purpose, he will be assisted by a Central Advisory Council of the Malay Rulers sitting together under his Chairmanship. In addition to passing in review all legislation affecting Mohammedan religion, this central Advisory Council may discuss other matters.

===== Page 79 =====
Other subjects, either at the instance of the Governor or at the instance of any of the Rulers with the Governor's consent. By these arrangements, it will be ensured that each of the Rulers can play his part not only in the affairs of his State but in the future development of Malaya as a whole. Each Ruler will, of course, be assured of a Civil List appropriate to his dignity.

Subjects of pan-Malayan importance.

9. There will be a special need to develop harmonious and mutually profitable relations between the Malayan Union and Singapore on matters of trade. Subjects of pan-Malayan importance which require full identity of policy throughout Malaya, such as Higher Education, Immigration, Currency, Income Tax, Civil Aviation, Posts and Telegraphs, and Shipping, will be matters of common arrangement between the Union and the Colony. The currency will continue to be managed under pan-Malayan agreement.

Malayan Union Citizenship.

10. The policy of His Majesty's Government is to promote a broad-based citizenship which will include, without discrimination of race or creed, all who can establish a claim, by reason of birth or a suitable period of residence, to belong to the country. It is proposed, therefore, to create by Order in Council a Malayan Union Citizenship. The following persons will acquire Malayan Union Citizenship:

(a) persons born in the territory of the Union or of the Colony of Singapore;

(b) persons who at the date on which the Order in Council becomes operative have been ordinarily resident in those territories for ten years out of the preceding fifteen. (In calculating the fifteen years' period, the period of Japanese occupation will be disregarded).

It will also be possible for persons to acquire Malayan Union citizenship after five years' ordinary residence in the Malayan Union or Singapore. British subjects who acquire Malayan Union citizenship will not thereby lose their British nationality. Save with the consent of the Governor, no person who is not a Malayan Union citizen will be admitted to public office or membership of Central and Local Councils.

Those acquiring Malayan Union citizenship otherwise than by birth will be required to affirm allegiance to the Malayan Union.

Courts.

11. There will be a Supreme Court of the Malayan Union and a Supreme Court of Singapore, each with a Chief Justice at its head. Each will consist of a Court of first instance and a Court of Appeal. The Chief Justices and the puisne Judges of the Supreme Court of the Union and of Singapore respectively will be ex officio judges in the other territory.

There

===== Page 80 =====
There will be appeals to the Privy Council from both Courts of Appeal.

Transfer of State and Settlement Property to the Union.

12. Arrangements will be made for the transfer, with certain exceptions, of the assets and liabilities of each of the Malay States and of the Settlements of Penang and Malacca to the Malayan Union. It will be necessary to arrange for an apportionment of the assets and liabilities of the Government of the Straits Settlements between the Governments of Singapore and of the Malayan Union.

Governor-General.

13. The co-ordination and direction of the policies of Government in the Malayan Union and Singapore will be among the responsibilities of a Governor-General.

===== Page 81 =====
APPENDIX

The following are the directions which were given to Sir Harold MacMichael for his discussions with the Malay Rulers:

"You will visit Malaya, at a date to be agreed by the S.A.O.S.E.A., and invite each Malay Ruler's cooperation in the establishment of a fresh constitutional organisation of Malaya which has been approved by His Majesty's Government and communicated to you and which is intended to ensure and facilitate the progress of the people of the country towards unity and ultimate self-government within the British Empire.

"In furtherance of this object you are authorised as Special Representative of His Majesty's Government to conclude with each Ruler on behalf of His Majesty's Government a formal Agreement by which he will cede full jurisdiction to His Majesty in his State."

===== Page 82 =====
