CABINET

COLONIAL AFFAIRS COMMITTEE

MALAYAN CONSTITUTIONS

Memorandum by the Secretary of State for the Colonies.

As my colleagues are no doubt aware, the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill is to be introduced in the House of Lords at the beginning of the next Session. This Bill is required in order that effect may be given to the new Constitutional measures for Malaya which I announced in the House of Commons on the 10th October, 1945 (Hansard Volume 414, No. 13, Column 255).

The announcement in question gave only a brief outline of the proposed Constitutional arrangements for the Malayan Union and Singapore, and it is very desirable that some further information should be available for Members of both Houses when the Straits Settlements Bill is taken.

I therefore recommend that a White Paper giving this information should be laid not later than the beginning of the next Session, and I attach a draft which has been prepared for this purpose.

I trust that my colleagues will agree that my recommendation should be adopted and should be submitted forthwith for Cabinet approval, so that the White Paper may be available as soon as Parliament reassembles.

It is desirable that the substance of the White Paper should be made public in Malaya on or about the same time as it is laid before Parliament. This is another reason for early approval of the White Paper.

Colonial Office,

3rd January, 1946.

A. C. J. for G. H.

===== Page 84 =====
MALAYA

Statement of Policy for the future constitution of the Malayan Union and the Colony of Singapore.

Previous position.

1. The territories concerned in this statement comprise first, the Colony of the Straits Settlements (that is to say Singapore, Penang, and Malacca, in Malaya; Labuan off the north coast of Borneo; and Christmas Island and the Cocos-Keeling Islands to the south of Sumatra); secondly, the Federated Malay States of Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan, and Pahang, in the Malay Peninsula; and thirdly, the Unfederated Malay States of Johore, Kedah, Kelantan, Trengganu and Perlis, also in the Malay Peninsula. The Colony of the Straits Settlements was, before its occupation by the Japanese, under direct British administration. In the Malay States, on the other hand, British authority rested upon agreements concluded with the Rulers, under which each Ruler retained sovereignty in his State, though each was bound to accept British advice on all matters except Mohammedan religion and Malay custom. Administrative co-ordination was achieved by the Governor of the Straits Settlements in his capacity as High Commissioner for the Malay States. As Governor of the Straits Settlements he was in direct charge of the Colony's administration; on matters pertaining to the Federated Malay States, as High Commissioner, he exercised his functions through the British Resident in each of the four States and through the Federal Secretary at the Headquarters of the Federal Government (Kuala Lumpur); on the business of the Unfederated Malay States, again as High Commissioner, he exercised his functions through an Adviser in each State. This co-ordination had, however, to be effected through a variety of legislatures and administrations. Legislation in the Colony was enacted by the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements, legislation in the Federated Malay States by the Federal Council and each State Council, and legislation in each Unfederated Malay State by the State Council of each State. Uniformity of legislation in the Malay States, therefore, required separate action by at least six, and in some cases by ten, legislatures.

Need for reform.

2. This divided and separatist position in a small country slightly larger than England was formerly not inappropriate and in many ways indeed can be shown to have represented a necessary and useful stage in the development of the several States. But with the increasing complexity of modern administrative, economic and social developments

===== Page 85 =====
Developments: It is unnecessarily cumbersome, inadequate for large common services, and wasteful of time and personnel. A return to this position would be manifestly contrary to the interests of the territories and their inhabitants in the post-war world, with its difficulties arising out of the war, and, for Malaya, the special problems created by the Japanese occupation. A stage has now been reached when the system of government should be simplified and reformed. International relations as well as the security and other interests of the British Commonwealth require that Malaya should be able to exercise an influence as a united and enlightened country appropriate to her economic and strategic importance. On a longer view, too, the pre-war system will not lend itself to that political adjustment which will offer, through broad-based institutions in which the whole community can participate, the means and prospect of developing Malaya's capacity in the direction of responsible self-government. In this development all those who have made the country their homeland should have the opportunity of a due share in the country's political and cultural institutions. Efficiency and democratic progress alike demand therefore that the system of government should be simplified and reformed.

H.M. to possess jurisdiction in Malay States.

3. The British Crown must provide the common link which will draw together the communities of Malaya and promote a sense of common interest and the development of common institutions. His Majesty's Government has therefore concluded that the old situation, in which His Majesty had no jurisdiction in the Malay States should be remedied and that fresh arrangements should be made with each of the Malay Rulers, under which such jurisdiction will be ceded to His Majesty as will enable him to legislate for the States under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. Accordingly Sir Harold MacMichael, as Special Representative of His Majesty's Government, has been engaged on a mission to the Malay States and has successfully concluded with each of the Malay Rulers after friendly discussion and consultation an Agreement which, supplementing the existing Treaties, grants full jurisdiction in each State to His Majesty.

For the directions given to Sir Harold MacMichael, see Appendix.

During this phase the Governor will be instructed to complete his local consultations with a view to the formal institution of the Executive and Legislative Councils as soon as possible.

These changes in the legislative system will have respect for the continuity of the principles of land policy in the Malay States. The Malay Lands Reservation policy, in particular, will be maintained.

8. His Majesty's Government have borne in mind the special position of the Malay Rulers as traditional and spiritual leaders of the Malay people. It is intended, therefore,

===== Page 97 =====
Therefore, that the Ruler in each State shall preside over a Malay Advisory Council whose members he will appoint, subject to the approval of the Governor. The main functions of these Malay Advisory Councils will relate to matters affecting Mohammedan religion; but they will also advise the Ruler on other matters at the request of the Resident Commissioner acting with the Governor's approval. On matters of Mohammedan religion, excepting the collection of tithes and taxes, each Ruler, with the help of his Council, will have legislative powers within his State. Such legislation will require the Governor's assent, and for this purpose he will be assisted by a Central Advisory Council of the Malay Rulers sitting together under his Chairmanship. In addition to passing in review all legislation affecting Mohammedan religion, this Central Advisory Council may discuss other subjects, either at the instance of the Governor or at the instance of any of the Rulers with the Governor's consent. By these arrangements it will be ensured that each of the Rulers can play his part not only in the affairs of his State but in the future development of Malaya as a whole. Each Ruler will of course be assured of a Civil List appropriate to his dignity.

Subjects of pan-Malayan importance.

9. There will be a special need to develop harmonious and mutually profitable relations between the Malayan Union and Singapore on matters of trade. Subjects of pan-Malayan importance which require full identity of policy throughout Malaya, such as Higher Education, Immigration, Currency, Income Tax, Civil Aviation, Posts and Telegraphs, and Shipping, will be matters of common arrangement between the Union and the Colony. The currency will continue to be managed under pan-Malayan agreement.

Malayan Union Citizenship: a broad-based citizenship which will include, without discrimination.

===== Page 98 =====
Discrimination of race or creed, all who can establish a claim, by reason of birth or a suitable period of residence, to belong to the country. It is proposed, therefore, to create by Order in Council a Malayan Union Citizenship.

The following persons will acquire Malayan Union Citizenship:

(a) persons born in the territory of the Union or of the Colony of Singapore;

(b) persons who at the date on which the Order in Council becomes operative have been ordinarily resident in those territories for ten years out of the preceding fifteen. (In calculating the fifteen years' period, the period of Japanese occupation will be disregarded).

It will also be possible for persons to acquire Malayan Union citizenship after five years' ordinary residence in the Malayan Union or Singapore. British subjects who acquire Malayan Union citizenship will not thereby lose their British nationality. Save with the consent of the Governor, no person who is not a Malayan Union citizen will be admitted to public office or membership of Central and Local Councils.

Those acquiring Malayan Union citizenship otherwise than by birth will be required to affirm allegiance to the Malayan Union.

Courts.

11. There will be a Supreme Court of the Malayan Union and a Supreme Court of Singapore, each with a Chief Justice at its head. Each will consist of a Court of first instance and a Court of Appeal. The Chief Justices and the puisne Judges of the Supreme Court of the Union and of Singapore respectively will be ex officio judges in the other territory. There will be appeals to the Privy Council from both Courts of Appeal.

12. Arrangements will be made for the transfer, with certain exceptions, of the assets and liabilities of each of the Malay States and of the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

===== Page 99 =====
To the Malayan Union, it will be necessary to arrange for an apportionment of the assets and liabilities of the Government of the Straits Settlements between the Governments of Singapore and of the Malayan Union.

13. The co-ordination and direction of the policies of Government in the Malayan Union and Singapore will be among the responsibilities of a Governor-General.

CABINET

COLONIAL AFFAIRS COMMITTEE

MALAYAN CONSTITUTIONS

Memorandum by the Secretary of State for the Colonies

As my colleagues are no doubt aware, the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill is to be introduced in the House of Lords at the beginning of the next Session. This Bill is required in order that effect may be given to the new Constitutional measures for Malaya which I announced in the House of Commons on the 10th October, 1945 (Hansard Volume 414, No.13, Column 255).

The announcement in question gave only a brief outline of the proposed Constitutional arrangements for the Malayan Union and Singapore, and it is very desirable that some further information should be available for Members of both Houses when the Straits Settlements Bill is taken.

I therefore recommend that a White Paper giving this information should be laid not later than the beginning of the next Session, and I attach a draft which has been prepared for this purpose.

I trust that my colleagues will agree that my recommendation should be adopted and should be submitted forthwith for Cabinet approval, so that the White Paper may be available as soon as Parliament reassembles.

It is desirable that the substance of the White Paper should be made public in Malaya on

===== Page 103 =====
or about the same time as it is laid before Parliament. This is another reason for early approval of the White Paper.

Colonial Office,
2nd January, 1946.

===== Page 104 =====
MALAYA

Statement of Policy for the future constitution of the Malayan Union and the Colony of Singapore.

Previous position.

1. The territories concerned in this statement comprise, first, the Colony of the Straits Settlements (that is to say Singapore, Penang, and Malacca, in Malaya; Labuan off the north coast of Borneo; and Christmas Island and the Cocos-Keeling Islands to the south of Sumatra); secondly, the Federated Malay States of Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan, and Pahang, in the Malay Peninsula; and thirdly, the Unfederated Malay States of Johore, Kedah, Kelantan, Trengganu and Perlis, also in the Malay Peninsula. The Colony of the Straits Settlements was, before its occupation by the Japanese, under direct British administration. In the Malay States, on the other hand, British authority rested upon agreements concluded with the Rulers, under which each Ruler retained sovereignty in his State, though each was bound to accept British advice on all matters except Mohammedan religion and Malay custom. Administrative co-ordination was achieved by the Governor of the Straits Settlements in his capacity as High Commissioner for the Malay States. As Governor of the Straits Settlements he was in direct charge of the Colony's administration; on matters pertaining to the Federated Malay States, as High Commissioner, he exercised his functions through the British Resident in each of the four States and through the Federal Secretary at the Headquarters of the Federal Government (Kuala Lumpur); on the business of the Unfederated Malay States, again as High Commissioner, he exercised his functions through an Adviser in each State. This co-ordination had, however, to be

===== Page 105 =====
Effected through a variety of legislatures and administrations. Legislation in the Colony was enacted by the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements, legislation in the Federated Malay States by the Federal Council and each State Council, and legislation in each Unfederated Malay State by the State Council of each State. Uniformity of legislation in the Malay States, therefore, required separate action by at least six, and in some cases by ten, legislatures.

2. This divided and separatist position in a small country slightly larger than England was formerly not inappropriate and in many ways indeed can be shown to have represented a necessary and useful stage in the development of the several States. But with the increasing complexity of modern administrative, economic and social developments it is unnecessarily cumbersome, inadequate for large common services, and wasteful of time and personnel. A return to this position would be manifestly contrary to the interests of the territories and their inhabitants in the post-war world, with its difficulties arising out of the war, and, for Malaya, the special problems created by the Japanese occupation. A stage has now been reached when the system of government should be simplified and reformed. International relations, as well as the security and other interests of the British Commonwealth, require that Malaya should be able to exercise an influence as a united and enlightened country appropriate to her economic and strategic importance. On a longer view, too, the pre-war system will not lend itself to that political adjustment which will offer, through broad-based institutions in which the whole community can participate, the means and prospect of developing Malaya's capacity in the direction

===== Page 106 =====
of responsible self-government. In this development, all those who have made the country their homeland should have the opportunity of a due share in the country's political and cultural institutions. Efficiency and democratic progress alike demand, therefore, that the system of government should be simplified and reformed.

3. The British Crown must provide the common link which will draw together the communities of Malaya and promote a sense of common interest and the development of common institutions. His Majesty's Government has therefore concluded that the old situation, in which His Majesty had no jurisdiction in the Malay States, should be remedied and that fresh arrangements should be made with each of the Malay Rulers, under which such jurisdiction will be ceded to His Majesty as will enable him to legislate for the States under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. Accordingly, Sir Harold MacMichael, as Special Representative of His Majesty's Government, has been engaged on a mission to the Malay States and has successfully concluded with each of the Malay Rulers, after friendly discussion and consultation, an Agreement which, supplementing the existing Treaties, grants full jurisdiction in each State to His Majesty.

4. It is proposed that the present combination of the Straits Settlements of Penang, Malacca, and Singapore in one political unit should be readjusted on the following lines:

(a) the Settlement of Singapore should be a separate Colony; and

(b) the Settlements of Penang and Malacca should be administered with the Malay States in a Malayan Union.

===== Page 107 =====
These proposals will be carried into effect, so far as the Malay States are concerned, under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act, 1890, and, as regards the Straits Settlements, under legislation now before Parliament (the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill).

5. In considering the need for a closer political integration in Malaysia, His Majesty's Government consider that, at least for the time being, Singapore requires separate treatment. It is a centre of entrepot trade on a very large scale and has economic and social interests distinct from those of the mainland. It is recognised, however, that there were and will be close ties between Singapore and the mainland, and it is no part of the policy of His Majesty's Government to preclude or prejudice in any way the fusion of Singapore and the Malayan Union in a wider union at a later date should it be considered that such a course were desirable.

6. As mentioned above, the Settlement of Singapore, with which will be included the Cocos-Keeling Islands and Christmas Island, will be constituted as the Colony of Singapore under a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. For the purposes of local government, it is intended that as broad and representative a basis as possible should be given to the restored Municipality of Singapore. During a transitional phase between the termination of the present military administration and the inauguration of the new constitution, the Governor will be empowered to legislate with the help of an Advisory Council. During this phase he will be instructed to give careful consideration to the questions of the composition of the Executive Council and of appointment and election to the Legislative Council and to the Municipal Commission, so as to ensure full and effective representation of the various sections of the community.

===== Page 108 =====
These proposals will be carried into effect so far as the Malay States are concerned, under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act, 1890, and, as regards the Straits Settlements, under legislation now before Parliament (the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill).

5. In considering the need for a closer political integration in Malaya, His Majesty's Government consider that, at least for the time being, Singapore requires separate treatment. It is a centre of entrepot trade on a very large scale and has economic and social interests distinct from those of the mainland. It is recognised, however, that there were and will be close ties between Singapore and the mainland, and it is no part of the policy of His Majesty's Government to preclude or prejudice in any way the fusion of Singapore and the Malayan Union in a wider union at a later date should it be considered that such a course were desirable.

6. As mentioned above, the Settlement of Singapore, with which will be included the Cocos-Keeling Islands and Christmas Island, will be constituted as the Colony of Singapore under a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. During a transitional phase between the termination of the present military administration and the inauguration of the new constitution, the Governor will be empowered to legislate with the help of an Advisory Council, which he will be instructed to select on a basis as broadly representative as conditions in this phase allow. During this phase he will be instructed to give careful consideration to the questions of the composition of the Executive Council and of appointment and election to the Legislative Council, so as to ensure full and effective representation of the various sections of the community.

For the purposes of local government, the Municipality of Singapore, the Harbour Board, and the Singapore Improvement Trust will be reconstituted and set to work with the minimum of delay. During the transitional phase mentioned above the Governor will be instructed to give careful consideration to broadening the sphere of authority of the Municipal Commission and its basis of representation.

===== Page 109 =====
7. The constitution of the Malayan Union will provide for a central authority consisting of a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. In each State and Settlement there will be a local Council which will have legislative powers, delegated to it by the central authority, appropriate to the purposes of local government. These Councils, both central and local, will consist of official and unofficial members; but, while it is the intention that their constitution shall be broad-based and representative, the final determination of numbers and of the details of representation will not be decided until there has been consultation with local opinion. In the initial phase after the termination of the Military Administration and before the new constitution is brought into effect, the Governor will be empowered to enact necessary legislation with the help of an Advisory Council, and during this phase the Governor will be instructed to complete his local consultations with a view to the formal institution of the Executive and Legislative Councils as soon as possible.

These changes in the legislative system will have respect for the continuity of the principles of land policy in the Malay States. The Malay Lands Reservation policy, in particular, will be maintained.

8. His Majesty's Government have borne in mind the special position of the Malay Rulers as traditional and spiritual leaders of the Malay people. It is intended, therefore, that the Ruler in each State shall preside over a Malay Advisory Council whose members he will appoint, subject to the approval of the Governor. The main functions of these Malay Advisory Councils will relate to matters affecting Mohammedan religion; but they will also advise the Ruler on other matters at the request of the Resident Commissioner acting with the Governor's approval. On matters

===== Page 110 =====
Of Mohammedan religion, excepting the collection of tithes and taxes, each Ruler, with the help of his Council, will have legislative powers within his State. Such legislation will require the Governor's assent, and for this purpose he will be assisted by a Central Advisory Council of the Malay Rulers sitting together under his Chairmanship. In addition to passing in review all legislation affecting Mohammedan religion, this Central Advisory Council may discuss other subjects, either at the instance of the Governor or at the instance of any of the Rulers with the Governor's consent. By these arrangements, it will be ensured that each of the Rulers can play his part not only in the affairs of his State but in the future development of Malaya as a whole. Each Ruler will, of course, be assured of a Civil List appropriate to his dignity.

9. There will be a special need to develop harmonious and mutually profitable relations between the Malayan Union and Singapore on matters of trade. Subjects of pan-Malayan importance, such as Higher Education, Immigration, Currency, Civil Aviation, Posts and Telegraphs, and Shipping, which require full identity of policy throughout Malaya, will be matters of common arrangement between the Union and the Colony. The currency will continue to be managed under pan-Malayan agreement.

10. The policy of His Majesty's Government is to promote a broad-based citizenship which will include, without discrimination of race or creed, all who can establish a claim, by reason of birth or a suitable period of residence, to belong to the country. It is proposed, therefore, to create by Order in Council a Malayan Union Citizenship. The following persons will acquire Malayan Union Citizenship:


===== Page 111 =====
(a) Persons born in the territory of the Union or of the Colony of Singapore.

(b) Persons who at the date on which the Order in Council becomes operative have been ordinarily resident in those territories for ten years out of the preceding fifteen. (In calculating the fifteen years' period, the period of Japanese occupation will be disregarded).

It will also be possible for persons to acquire Malayan Union citizenship after five years' ordinary residence in the Malayan Union or Singapore. British subjects who acquire Malayan Union citizenship will not thereby lose their British nationality. Save with the consent of the Governor, no person who is not a Malayan Union citizen will be admitted to public office or membership of Central and Local Councils.

Those acquiring Malayan Union citizenship otherwise than by birth will be required to affirm allegiance to the Malayan Union.

11. There will be a Supreme Court of the Malayan Union and a Supreme Court of Singapore, each with a Chief Justice at its head. Each will consist of a Court of first instance and a Court of Appeal. The Chief Justices and the puisne Judges of the Supreme Court of the Union and of Singapore respectively will be ex officio judges in the other territory. There will be appeals to the Privy Council from both Courts of Appeal.

12. Arrangements will be made for the transfer, with certain exceptions, of the assets and liabilities of each of the Malay States and of the Settlements of Penang and Malacca to the Malayan Union. It will be necessary to arrange for an apportionment of the assets and liabilities of the Government of the Straits Settlements between

===== Page 112 =====
The Governments of Singapore and of the Malayan Union.

13. The coordination and direction of the policies of Government in the Malayan Union and Singapore will be among the responsibilities of a Governor-General.

===== Page 120 =====
Mr. Creech-Jones has recorded in his minute that the Straits Settlement Repeal Bill should be introduced into the House of Lords on the last day before the Christmas recess, and that if at all possible the White Paper on Malaya should be laid in dummy simultaneously. The objective is that on the second reading of the Bill Parliament may have the White Paper available with a clear exposition of the policy in the Malayan Union and Singapore.

It seems desirable that the White Paper should go to the Colonial Affairs Committee and then to the Cabinet for approval before presentation to Parliament. It would also be desirable that when published here it should be available for local release in Malaya and elsewhere overseas at the same time.

On this basis we are proposing that the White Paper (a draft of which has been sent forward to higher authority in the Colonial Office) should go before the Colonial Affairs Committee in the first week of January before the Secretary of State leaves and should be approved by the Cabinet in the next few days thereafter. It should be presented to Parliament on re-assembly (22nd January) and there would presumably be a debate in Parliament about the end of January.

To provide, however, for simultaneous release in Malaya and elsewhere overseas, it would be desirable to lay the White Paper in dummy, during the recess, as soon as the Cabinet has approved (say, about 12th January) and copies

===== Page 121 =====
Copies should then immediately be sent confidentially to Malaya and elsewhere overseas for public release on the appointed day.

The above would not, of course, be in accordance with the idea of simultaneous laying in dummy of both the Bill and the White Paper, but it would still ensure that Parliament had the White Paper available when the Bill was debated and it would also provide for the White Paper being approved by the Colonial Affairs Committee and the Cabinet as well as for simultaneous release overseas. It will also allow time for the conclusion of Sir Harold MacMichael's work with the Sultans and for this fact to be definitely stated in the White Paper.

(sgd.) G.E.J. GENT. 17.12.45.

===== Page 122 =====
Paragraph 5.

It is hardly correct to say that there "were" close ties between Singapore and "the Malayan Union", and I suggest substituting "the mainland" for the latter. In consequence it might be better to say "Singapore and the Malayan Union" instead of "the two Administrations".

Paragraph 6.

As it has not been "mentioned above" that the settlement of Singapore would include the Cocos Keeling Islands and Christmas Island, I suggest substituting for the first two lines, "mentioned above, the settlement of Singapore, in which will be included the Cocos Keeling Islands and Christmas Island will," etc.

Paragraph 9.

In the previous draft I amended the original marginal note because "Pan-Malayan Subjects" seems to refer to the question of nationality, particularly when it is followed by a marginal note "Malayan Union citizenship." If this marginal note is retained I think "subjects" should at least be spelt with a small s, but would not "Subjects of Pan-Malayan importance" be better?

Paragraph 10.

"And" in the penultimate line should be "or".

Paragraph 13.

I think there is some substance in objecting to the title "Governor-General." Personally, I can see no objection to "High Commissioner."

I have not mentioned the most important point, which is whether paragraph 3 should state that the agreements have all been signed, the issue of the White Paper being delayed of course until that is the case. I will not go fully into this, because I know Sir George Gater has the point under consideration. I will only say that it does appear to be little easy for those who like to accuse this country of "imperialistic aggression" if the White Paper contains any suggestion that the Rulers are going to be forced to sign agreements whether they like it or not.

MALAYA

Statement of Policy for the future constitution of the Malayan Union and the Colony of Singapore.

1. The territories concerned in this statement comprise, first, the Colony of the Straits Settlements (that is to say Singapore, Penang, and Malacca, in Malaya; Labuan off the north coast of Borneo; and Christmas Island and the Cocos-Keeling Islands to the south of Sumatra); secondly, the Federated Malay States of Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan, and Pahang, in the Malay Peninsula; and thirdly, the Unfederated Malay States of Johore, Kedah, Kelantan, Trengganu and Perlis, also in the Malay Peninsula. The Colony of the Straits Settlements was, before its occupation by the Japanese, under direct British administration. In the Malay States, on the other hand, British authority rested upon agreements concluded with the Rulers, under which each Ruler retained sovereignty in his State, though each was bound to accept British advice on all matters except Mohammedan religion and Malay custom. Administrative co-ordination was achieved by the Governor of the Straits Settlements in his capacity as High Commissioner for the Malay States. As Governor of the Straits Settlements he was in direct charge of the Colony's administration; on matters pertaining to the Federated Malay States, as High Commissioner, he exercised his functions through the British Resident in each of the four States and through the Federal Secretary at the Headquarters of the Federal Government (Kuala Lumpur); on the business of the Unfederated Malay States, again as High Commissioner, he exercised his functions through a British Adviser in each State. This co-ordination had, however, to be effected through a variety of legislatures and administrations.

/Legislation

===== Page 124 =====
Legislation in the Colony was enacted by the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements, legislation in the Federated Malay States by the Federal Council and each State Council, and legislation in each Unfederated Malay State by the State Council of each State. Uniformity of legislation in the Malay States, therefore, required separate action by at least six, and in some cases by ten, legislatures.

2. This divided and separatist position in a small country not as large as England was formerly not inappropriate and in many ways indeed can be shown to have represented a necessary and useful stage in the development of the several States. But with the increasing complexity of modern administrative, economic and social developments it is unnecessarily cumbersome and wasteful of time and personnel. A stage has now been reached when the system of government should be simplified and reformed. A factor of particular importance is the need for a particular adjustment which will offer the means and prospect of developing Malaya's capacity in the direction of self-government. In this development all those who have made the country their homeland should have the opportunity of a due share in the country's political and cultural institutions.

3. The British Crown must provide the common link which will draw together the communities of Malaya and promote a sense of common interest and the development of common institutions. It is, therefore, necessary that the old situation, in which His Majesty had no jurisdiction in the Malay States, should be remedied, and that fresh Agreements with each Ruler should be concluded under which such jurisdiction will be ceded to His Majesty as will enable him to legislate for the States under the Foreign Jurisdiction.

===== Page 125 =====
Jurisdiction Act. Accordingly Sir Harold MacMichael, as Special Representative of His Majesty, has been engaged on a mission to conclude with each of the Malay Rulers new Agreements whereby full jurisdiction will be possessed by His Majesty.

4. It is proposed that the present combination of the Straits Settlements of Penang, Malacca, and Singapore in one political unit should be readjusted on the following lines:

(a) the Settlement of Singapore should be a separate Colony; and

(b) the Settlements of Penang and Malacca should be administered with the Malay States in a Malayan Union.

These proposals will be carried into effect, so far as the Malay States are concerned, under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act, 1890, and, as regards the Straits Settlements, under legislation now before Parliament (the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill).

5. In considering the need for a closer political integration in Malaya, His Majesty's Government consider that, at least for the time being, Singapore requires separate treatment. It is a centre of entrepot trade on a very large scale and has economic and social interests distinct from those of the mainland. It is recognised, however, that there were and will be close ties between Singapore and the Malayan Union, and it is no part of the policy of His Majesty's Government to preclude or prejudice in any way the fusion of the two administrations in a wider union at a later date should they both agree to such a course.

===== Page 126 =====
6. As mentioned above, the Settlement of Singapore, including the Cocos-Keeling Islands and Christmas Island, will be constituted as the Colony of Singapore under a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. For the purposes of local government, it is intended that as broad and representative a basis as possible should be given to the restored Municipality of Singapore. During a transitional phase between the termination of the present military administration and the inauguration of the new constitution, the Governor will be empowered to legislate with the help of an Advisory Council. During this phase he will be instructed to give careful consideration to the questions of the composition of the Executive Council and of appointment and election to the Legislative Council and to the Municipal Commission, so as to ensure adequate representation of the various sections of the community.

7. The constitution of the Malayan Union will provide for a central authority consisting of a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. In each State and Settlement there will be local Councils which will have legislative powers, delegated to them by the central authority, appropriate to the purposes of local government. These Councils, both central and local, will consist of official and unofficial members; but, while it is the intention that their constitution shall be broad-based and representative, the final determination of numbers and of the details of representation will not be decided until there has been consultation with local opinion. In the initial phase after the termination of the Military Administration and before the new constitution is brought into effect, the Governor will be empowered to enact necessary legislation with the help of an Advisory Council. In forming this interim Advisory the Governor will have close regard to the intended composition of the Legislative Council.

===== Page 127 =====
8. His Majesty's Government have borne in mind the special position of the Malay Rulers as traditional and spiritual leaders of the Malay people. It is intended, therefore, that the Ruler in each State shall preside over an Advisory Malay Council whose members he will appoint, subject to the approval of the Governor. The main functions of these Malay Advisory Councils will relate to matters affecting Mohammedan religion; but they will also advise the Ruler on other matters at the request of the Resident Commissioner acting with the Governor's approval. On matters of Mohammedan religion, excepting the collection of tithes and taxes, each Ruler, with the help of his Council, will have legislative powers within his State. Such legislation will require the Governor's assent, and for this purpose he will be assisted by a Central Advisory Council of the Malay Rulers sitting together under his Chairmanship. In addition to passing in review all legislation affecting Mohammedan religion, this central Advisory Council may discuss other subjects, either at the instance of the Governor or at the instance of any of the Rulers with the Governor's consent. By these arrangements it will be ensured that each of the Rulers can play his part not only in the affairs of his State but in the future development of Malaya as a whole.

9. There will be a special need to develop harmonious and mutually profitable relations between the Malayan Union and Singapore on matters of trade. Subjects of pan-Malayan importance, such as Posts and Telegraphs, Immigration, Currency, Civil Aviation, and Shipping, which require full identity of policy throughout Malaya, will be matters of common arrangement between the Union and the Colony. The currency will continue to be managed under pan-Malayan agreement.

10. The policy of His Majesty's Government is to promote a broad-based citizenship which will include,

===== Page 128 =====
Without discrimination of race or creed, all who can claim, by reason of birth or a suitable period of residence, to belong to the country. It is proposed, therefore, to create by Order in Council a Malayan Union Citizenship. The following persons will automatically acquire Malayan Union Citizenship:

(a) persons born in the territory of the Union or of the Colony of Singapore;

(b) persons who at the date on which the Order in Council becomes operative have been ordinarily resident in those territories for ten years out of the preceding fifteen. (In calculating the fifteen years' period, the period of Japanese occupation will be disregarded).

It will also be possible for persons to acquire Malayan Union citizenship after five years' ordinary residence in the Malayan Union or Singapore. British subjects who acquire Malayan Union citizenship will not thereby lose their British nationality. Save with the consent of the Governor, no person who is not a Malayan Union citizen will be admitted to public office and membership of Central and Local Councils.

11. There will be a Supreme Court of the Malayan Union and a Supreme Court of Singapore, each with a Chief Justice at its head. Each will consist of a Court of first instance and a Court of Appeal. The Chief Justices and the puisne Judges of the Supreme Court of the Union and of Singapore respectively will be ex officio judges in the other territory. There will be appeals to the Privy Council from both Courts of Appeal.

12. Arrangements will be made for the transfer, with certain exceptions, of the assets and liabilities of each of the Malay States and of the Settlements of Penang and Malacca to the Malayan Union. It will be necessary to arrange for an apportionment of the assets.

===== Page 129 =====
Assets and liabilities of the Government of the Straits Settlements between the Governments of Singapore and of the Malayan Union.

Governor-General. 13. The co-ordination and direction of the policies of Government in the Malayan Union and Singapore will be secured by the appointment of a Governor-General, who will reside in Singapore.

===== Page 130 =====
MALAYA

Statement of Policy for the future constitution of the Malayan Union and the Colony of Singapore.

Previous position.

1. The territories concerned in this statement comprise, first, the Colony of the Straits Settlements (that is to say Singapore, Penang, and Malacca, in Malaya; Labuan off the north coast of Borneo; and Christmas Island and the Cocos-Keeling Islands to the south of Sumatra); secondly, the Federated Malay States of Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan, and Pahang, in the Malay Peninsula; and thirdly, the Unfederated Malay States of Johore, Kedah, Kelantan, Trengganu and Perlis, also in the Malay Peninsula. The Colony of the Straits Settlements was, before its occupation by the Japanese, under direct British administration. In the Malay States, on the other hand, British authority rested upon agreements concluded with the Rulers, under which each Ruler retained sovereignty in his State, though each was bound to accept British advice on all matters except Mohammedan religion and Malay custom.

Administrative co-ordination was achieved by the Governor of the Straits Settlements in his capacity as High Commissioner for the Malay States. As Governor of the Straits Settlements he was in direct charge of the Colony's administration; on matters pertaining to the Federated Malay States, as High Commissioner, he exercised his functions through the British Resident in each of the four States and through the Federal Secretary at the Headquarters of the Federal Government (Kuala Lumpur); on the business of the Unfederated Malay States, again as High Commissioner, he exercised his functions

===== Page 131 =====
Functions through a British Adviser in each State. This coordination had, however, to be effected through a variety of legislatures and administrations. Legislation in the Colony was enacted by the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements, legislation in the Federated Malay States by the Federal Council and each State Council, and legislation in each Unfederated Malay State by the State Council of each State. Uniformity of legislation in the Malay States, therefore, required separate action by at least six, and in some cases by ten, legislatures.

2. This divided and separatist position in a small country not as large as England was formerly not inappropriate and in many ways indeed can be shown to have represented a necessary and useful stage in the development of the several States. But with the increasing complexity of modern administrative, economic and social developments it is unnecessarily cumbersome and wasteful of time and personnel. Still more important is the need to delay no longer the political adjustment of the country which will offer the means and prospect of developing Malaya's capacity in the direction of self-government in which all those who have made the country their homeland may possess and exercise their share in the country's political and cultural institutions.

3. The British Crown must provide the common link which will draw together the separate communities of Malaya and promote a sense of common interest and the development of common institutions. It is, therefore, necessary that the old situation, in which His Majesty had no jurisdiction in the Malay States, should be remedied, and that fresh Agreements with each Ruler should be concluded under which such jurisdiction will be ceded to His Majesty as will enable him to legislate.

===== Page 132 =====
Legislate for the States under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. Accordingly, Sir Harold MacMichael, as Special Representative of His Majesty, has been engaged on a mission to conclude with each of the Malay Rulers new Agreements whereby full jurisdiction will be possessed by His Majesty.

4. It is proposed that Singapore should be a separate Colony and that the Settlements of Penang and Malacca should be administered with all the Malay States of the Peninsula in a Malayan Union. This proposal could be carried into effect, so far as the Malay States are concerned, under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act, 1890, but, as respects the Straits Settlements, there are certain legal obstacles which the Straits Settlements (Repeal) Bill, now before Parliament, is designed to remove.

5. In considering the need for a closer political integration in Malaya, His Majesty's Government consider that, for the time being at any rate, Singapore requires separate treatment as a centre of entrepot trade with economic and social interests distinct from those of the mainland. It is recognised, however, that there were and will be close ties between Singapore and the Peninsula territory, and it is no part of the policy of His Majesty's Government to preclude or prejudice in any way the fusion of the two administrations in a wider union at a later date should they both agree to such a course. The Island of Labuan, as a detached part of the Colony of the Straits Settlements lying off the western shore of North Borneo, again requires separate treatment. It is proposed to incorporate it with that territory.

6. As mentioned above, the Settlement of Singapore, including the Cocos-Keeling Islands and Christmas Island but excluding Labuan, will be constituted

===== Page 133 =====
Constituted as the Colony of Singapore under a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. For the purposes of local government, it is intended that as broad and representative a basis as possible should be given to the restored Municipality of Singapore. During the initial phase, when the Governor is empowered to legislate with the help of an Advisory Council, he will give careful consideration to the questions of the composition of the Executive Council and of appointment and election to the Legislative Council and to the Municipal Commission, so as to ensure adequate representation of the various sections and interests in proportion to their importance and their contributions to the welfare and prosperity of Singapore.

7. The constitution of the Malayan Union will provide for a central authority consisting of a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. In each State and Settlement, there will be local Councils which will have executive and legislative powers delegated to them by the central authority, appropriate to the purposes of local government. These Councils, both central and local, will consist of official and unofficial members; but, while it is the intention that their constitution shall be broad-based and representative, the final determination of numbers and of the details of representation will not be decided until there has been consultation with local opinion. In the initial phase after the termination of the Military Administration and before the new constitution is brought into effect, the Governor will be empowered to enact necessary legislation with the help of an Advisory Council. In forming this interim Advisory Council, the Governor will have close regard to the intended composition of the Legislative Council.

===== Page 134 =====

It is intended, therefore, that the Ruler in each State shall preside over an Advisory Malay Council whose members he will appoint, subject to the approval of the Governor. The main functions of these Malay Advisory Councils will relate to matters affecting Mohammedan religion; but they will also advise the Ruler on other matters at the request of the Resident Commissioner acting with the Governor's approval. On matters of Mohammedan religion, excepting the collection of tithes and taxes, each Ruler, with the help of his Council, will have legislative powers within his State. Such legislation will require the Governor's assent, and for this purpose he will be assisted by a Central Advisory Council of the Malay Rulers sitting together under his Chairmanship. In addition to passing in review all legislation affecting Mohammedan religion, this Advisory Council will discuss other subjects, either at the instance of the Governor or at the instance of any of the Rulers with the Governor's consent. By these arrangements it will be ensured that each of the Rulers can play his part not only in the affairs of his State but in the future development of Malaya as a whole.

(a) persons born in the territory of the Union or of the Colony of Singapore;

(b) persons who at the date on which the Order in Council becomes operative have been ordinarily resident in those territories for ten years out of the preceding fifteen years. (In calculating the fifteen years' period, the period of Japanese occupation will be disregarded.)

State Council of each State. In addition, each of the four States of the Federation has its own legislative body, namely the Ruler in State Council, for certain subjects designated as State matters. United action therefore requires separate action by the Governments, a process which takes account the opportunity presented for local disagreement and different developments, does not make for speedy administration.

2. Efficiency and democratic progress alike demand a change in the existing constitutional and administrative system of Malaya. The former requires closer union between the Malay States, and the latter requires that advance towards self-government within the British Commonwealth shall be a development towards participation in Government by all the settled communities in Malaya, subject to a special recognition of the interests of the Malay race.

The Colony of Singapore will then be constituted under the British Settlements Act, of 1887 and 1945.

6. Under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act and the British Settlements Act, the Malayan Union will be constituted by an Order of His Majesty in Council and will comprise all the Malay States of the Peninsula together with the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

Constitutional arrangements.

7. The constitution of the Malayan Union will provide for a central authority consisting of a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. In each member State and Settlement there will be local authorities with executive and legislative powers, appropriate to local government purposes delegated to them by the central authority. All these Councils, both central and local, will combine official and unofficial membership, but while it is the intention

===== Page 149 =====
Intention that their character shall be broad-based and representative, the final fixation of numbers and of the details of representation are not matters for decision until there has been consultation with local opinion, in the initial phase between the change-over from Military Administration to Civil Government and the convening of the Legislative Council. In this interim period, when legislation to provide for the change-over to the Civil Government will be urgently necessary, the Governor will be empowered to legislate with the help of an Advisory Council, in selecting the members of which the Governor will guide himself by the intended composition of the Legislative Council. At the same time provision will be made for the continuing validity of the existing laws of each particular State and Settlement until altered or repealed, and for the continuance of proceedings pending in the Courts.

(b) persons who at the date on which the Order in Council becomes operative have ordinarily been resident in those territories for ten years out of the preceding fifteen. (In calculating the fifteen years period, the period of Japanese occupation will be disregarded).

The rights, as British citizens, of the peoples of the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.
