The main functions of these Malay Advisory Councils will relate to matters affecting Mohammedan religion; but they will also advise the Ruler on other matters at the request of the Resident Commissioner acting with the Governor's approval. On matters of Mohammedan religion, excepting the collection of tithes and taxes, each Ruler, with the help of his Council, will have legislative powers within his State. Such legislation will require the Governor's assent, and for this purpose he will be assisted by an Advisory Council of the Malay Rulers sitting together with him under his Chairmanship as one body. In addition to this function of passing in review all legislation affecting Mohammedan, this Advisory Council will discuss other subjects, either at the instance of the Governor or at the instance of any of the Rulers with the Governor's consent. By these arrangements it will be ensured that each of the Rulers can play his part not only in the affairs of his State but in the future development of Malaya as a whole.

10. There will be a High Court of the Malayan Union with a Chief Justice at its head, consisting of a Court of first

===== Page 151 =====
First instance and a Court of Appeal. The Chief Justices and the puisne Judges of the High and Supreme Courts of the Union and Singapore respectively will be declared ex officio judges in the other territory. The new constitution will provide for appeals to the Privy Council from the Court of Appeal of the Malayan Union.

11. In order that the Malayan Union may become a reality and be endowed in keeping with its constitutional authority and political responsibilities, arrangements will be made for the transferring, with certain exceptions, of the assets and liabilities of the Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca to the Union. As these two Settlements will be transferred from the jurisdiction of the Government of the Straits Settlements to that of the Union, it will be necessary to arrange for a division of their assets and liabilities between the Governments of Singapore and the Malayan Union.

12. The Settlement of Singapore, including the Cocos-Keeling Islands and Christmas Island but excluding Labuan, will be constituted as the Colony of Singapore under a separate Government of a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council. For the purposes of local government, it is proposed to extend the jurisdiction of the Municipal Commission, which as an unofficial body previously acted for the town area only, to cover the whole Island.

13. For an initial phase between the change-over from Military Administration to Civil Government and the convening of the Legislative Council, the Governor will be empowered to legislate with the help of an Advisory Council, and he will at the same time review in the light of public opinion the character not only of the Executive and Legislative Councils but also of the Municipal Commission.

===== Page 152 =====
Ensure adequate representation of the various sections and interests in relation to their importance and their contribution to the welfare and prosperity of Singapore.

14. There will be a special need to develop harmonious and mutually beneficial relations between the Malayan Union and Singapore on matters of trade, and subjects of pan-Malayan importance, such as Posts and Telegraphs, Immigration, Currency, Civil Aviation, and Shipping. These subjects, which require identity of policy throughout Malaya, will be regulated by particular agreements between the Union and the Colony.

15. The co-ordination and direction of the policies of Government in the Malayan Union and Singapore will be secured by the appointment of a Governor-General, who will reside in Singapore and will have power of control, not only over the Governors of the Union and Singapore but also over British Representatives in Borneo.

===== Page 153 =====
MALAYA

Statement of Policy for the Future Constitution of the Malayan Union and the Colony of Singapore.

1. Efficiency and democratic progress alike demand a change in the pre-war constitutional and administrative system of Malaya. The former requires closer union between the Malay States, and the latter requires that advance towards self-government within the British Commonwealth shall be a development towards participation in Government by the people of all the settled communities in Malaya, subject to special recognition of the interests of the Malay race.

2. Malaya, though a geographical unity, is not in fact a single country and forms neither a racial nor a constitutional entity. Appreciation of this is essential to any understanding of the problems facing the future administration. As organised under British control, Malaya consists of a British Colony, the Straits Settlements, the most important of which are Singapore, Penang, and Malacca, and of nine Malay States, four of which are joined in a Federation. The British position in the Malay States rests entirely upon agreements concluded with the several Rulers, under which they remain in a strictly legal sense sovereign and independent, though they are bound in fact to accept British advice on all matters except Malay religion and custom.

3. Until the seventies of the last century it was the policy to restrict British control to the three Settlements comprising the Colony and to discourage penetration into the Malay States, which were at that time essentially riverine communities, each under their own Ruler and considerably

===== Page 154 =====
Considerably isolated by mountain and jungle. During anarchy caused by pirate bands and by the impact on Malay society of traders and Chinese miners from the outside world, led in 1874 to requests from the Rulers of the four States, which were later included in the Federation, for assistance from the British Government in the administration of their territories. This assistance was embodied in Treaties providing for the appointment in each State of a British officer whose advice was to be acted upon in all questions other than those concerning Malay religion and custom. Similar Treaties were concluded at a later date with the other five Malay States, which remained outside the Federation and which were known as the Unfederated Malay States. In the Federated Malay States the officers appointed were styled British Residents, in the others British Advisers.

4. From the legal point of view these Treaties involved no derogation from the position of the Rulers as sovereign princes, nor did they confer any jurisdiction on His Majesty in the Malay States; and they led to important differences between the Straits Settlements, which is British territory, and the Malay States, which are under British protection. All persons born, for instance, in the Crown Colony are natural-born British subjects; those born in a Malay State are British protected persons but they are not British subjects. In the Colony every Ordinance requires the assent of the Governor, His Majesty's representative; in the Malay States new laws require the assent of the Ruler. In the Councils of the Colony the Governor presides; in every State Council the Ruler is President. In the Colony

===== Page 155 =====
Land is held from the Crown; in a Malay State from the Ruler.

5. From the historical point of view, the introduction of British Residents in the 1870's was followed by a rapid development, mainly by immigrant capital and labour, of the resources of the States concerned, which broke down their previous physical isolation. This led to proposals for administrative machinery for closer union and in 1895 the States of Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan, and Pahang agreed to the formation of a Federation. Paradoxically, the existence of the Federation proved in fact to be an obstacle to further unification. The rapidity of the country's material development outstripped the advance in education and administrative capacity of the Malays themselves. The British Residents found themselves compelled to take over more or less direct control of the administrative system in the Federated States, and there emerged a highly centralized bureaucracy based on the Federal capital, Kuala Lumpur. In consequence, the Rulers of the States outside the Federation, shy of the loss of power which they felt they would suffer in the administration of their States by joining the Federation and influenced by the fact that, in the main, the penetration of Malay communities by immigrant races had proceeded further in the Federated and in the Unfederated Malay States, have not hitherto welcomed moves towards the administrative unification of the Peninsula. A further obstacle lay in the divergence of economic interest between Singapore, which, as a great entrepot, was essentially mercantile in character, and the mainland, which was mainly a producer for exports and which

===== Page 156 =====
Which fear that in any closer union its interests would be sacrificed to those of Singapore.

6. Some attempt was made between the two wars to meet dissatisfaction in the States concerned with the centralization of legislative and administrative authority in the Federal machinery by the policy of gradually transferring many governmental functions from the Federal to the State Governments. It is important to realise, however, that this policy was regarded as an essential preliminary to the achievement of a longer-term policy, mainly the promotion of closer co-operation between the constituent parts of Malaya as a whole, by removing the deterrent which the existing Federation constituted against closer association of the Unfederated Malay States.

7. Administrative co-ordination was achieved by the Governor of the Straits Settlements acting also as High Commissioner for the Malay States. As Governor of the Straits Settlements he was in direct charge of the Colony's administration; on matters pertaining to the Federated Malay States, as High Commissioner, he controlled the Federal Government through the Federal Secretary at Kuala Lumpur and the States Government through the four British Residents; on the business of the Unfederated Malay States, again, as High Commissioner he controlled the State Government through the British Advisers in each State. This co-ordination had to be effected, however, through a variety of legislatures. Legislation for the Colony was enacted by the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements, legislation

===== Page 157 =====
Legislation for the Federated Malay States was by the Federal Council, and legislation for each Unfederated Malay State was by the Ruler in State Council of each such State. In addition, each of the four States of the Federation had its own legislative body, namely the Ruler in State Council, for certain subjects designated as State matters. United action in the past, therefore, required separate action by ten Governments, a process which, taking into account the opportunity presented for local disagreement and different developments, did not make for speedy administration.

8. Experience has shown, therefore, that the interests of efficient Government demand speedier measures to integrate closely both the legislative and the administrative arrangements of the Malay Peninsula.

9. On a longer view, too, the pre-war system does not seem capable of adjustments to the Colonial policy of permitting self-government on broad-based institutions in which the whole community can participate. Although the spread of British influence brought co-ordination in many places and successfully harmonized the interest of Malaya's racial communities, the separate entity of these States has remained nonetheless a reality. The Malays cherish a definite loyalty towards their Rulers, and this feeling conflicts with the development of any allegiance, as "Malayan," with the common duties and problems, towards a larger unit than the States. Furthermore, the extraordinary economic development which has accompanied

===== Page 158 =====
Accompanied by the spread of British influence, and the extensive introduction of capital and enterprise from China and India, a situation has arisen in which the non-Malay population in Malaya now outnumbers the Malay, though many of the former still regard their original land rather than Malaya as their real home. This resultant problem presents special obstacles to the development of a sense of common citizenship in Malaya. On the one hand, the relations of the British Government with the Malay States are based on a recognition of the rights of the Malay people to a secure position in their land and in their educational and other facilities for progress. On the other hand, the enterprise and capacity of the non-Malay peoples of the country, provided they are accompanied by a sense of civic responsibility and political loyalty, assert a claim to exercise a due share in the moulding of Malaya's future on a basis of common citizenship with the Malay subjects of the Rulers, with all the rights and obligations pertaining thereto. It is His Majesty's Government's policy, therefore, to promote a broad-based citizenship in Malaya in which all may participate without discrimination of race or creed, subject to qualifications of birth or a suitable period of residence in Malaya.

10. Within Malaya, there is no foreseeable future of a union of the existing Malay States under a native Ruler; nor would the emergence of a united Malay monarchy be acceptable to the other non-Malay communities with substantial influence in the country. The British Crown alone provides the common link of loyalty which will draw the separate communities together.

===== Page 159 =====
Together and promoted a sense of common interest and the development of common institutions. It is therefore necessary that the old situation in which His Majesty had no jurisdiction in the Malay States should be remedied, and that fresh Treaties with each Ruler should be concluded under which such jurisdiction will be ceded to His Majesty as will enable him to legislate for the States under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. Thereafter it will be possible to proceed to create a new constitution for the Malay States by Order-in-Council under statutory powers.

11. Accordingly, Sir Harold MacMichael, as Special Representative of His Majesty in Malaya, is engaged on a mission to conclude with each of the Malay Rulers new Agreements whereby full jurisdiction in their States will be ceded to His Majesty. At the same time fresh legislation in the United Kingdom Parliament is required by the repeal of the Straits Settlements Act of 1806, and by the amendment of the British Settlements Act of 1807, to enable the constitution of the Straits Settlements to be changed in accordance with His Majesty's Government's policy for a closer integration of Malaysia.

12. Under the statutory powers conferred by the Agreement and by legislation in Parliament affecting the Straits Settlements, it is proposed to constitute by Order-in-Council the Malayan Union, which will comprise all the Malay States in the Peninsula together with the Settlements of Penang and Malacca. The constitution of the Union will provide for a central and single authority consisting of a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council, from which all other authorities

===== Page 160 =====
Authorities in the Member States and Settlements shall derive their powers. The seat of Government of this Malayan Union will be conveniently at or near Kuala Lumpur, the capital of the present limited Federation. (At the same time by Order-in-Council it is proposed to constitute the Colony of Singapore under a separate Government consisting of a Governor with an Executive and Legislative Council.)

13. It is recognised that these measures will require the sacrifice by the State Rulers of some part of the authority and jurisdiction they have hitherto exercised. But there is no intention that the Rulers shall lose their personal positions as the natural leaders of the Malay people within their State territory, particularly in matters of Mohammedan religion. Indeed, the opportunity of the association of their territories in the Union should be taken to enable the Rulers to play their part in a wider sphere and that on Malay and Mohammedan matters the Governor should have the advice of the Rulers as a body. Their position in the constitution will be defined in and derived from the Order-in-Council constituting the Union, and this will provide for the establishment of an Advisory Council of the Malay Rulers, which shall be a deliberative, consultative and advisory, but not an executive, body, with the Governor, or in his absence the Officer Administering the Government, as its President. This Council, which will have a Malay title, will be a permanent body, will meet regularly, and its meetings will be conducted with all the ceremony.

===== Page 161 =====
Ceremony necessary in keeping with the personal dignity of the Rulers. The rules of business for the Council will be framed by the Governor after consultation with the Rulers, and complete records of the meetings will be kept in the Malay and English languages. The meetings will not be held in public, but communiques to the Press may be issued after each meeting.

The main functions of the Council will be:

(a) to advise the Governor on all matters affecting Mohammedan religion. In this connection, the Governor will consult his Advisory Council regarding legislation about Mohammedan religion which has been agreed by a Ruler acting with his own State Advisory Malay Council and which requires the Governor's assent, though the Governor will not be bound to accept its advice. Before any matter affecting Mohammedan religion is discussed or dealt with by the Union legislature, the Governor will be bound to have such matters discussed in the Advisory Council in the first instance.

(b) to advise the Governor on any other matter which the Governor decides should be discussed, either at his own instance or at the instance of any of the Rulers with the Governor's consent.

As the relationship between His Majesty's Government and each Ruler will be bound by Treaty, the following matters will be debarred from discussion in the Council:

(a) Treaties, rights, dignities and privileges of each Ruler and the members of his family, and the actions of each individual Ruler.

(b) The internal affairs of any individual State or Settlement which are properly within the purview of a State or Settlement Council.

The establishment of this Council will not prejudice in any way existing Treaty relations between His Majesty's Government and each Ruler, nor will the right of direct correspondence and access between any Ruler and the Governor be in any way impaired.

===== Page 162 =====
14. In each Malay State, side by side with the State Council (see below paragraph 22), there will be established Advisory Malay Councils to the Rulers, which will be presided over by the Ruler and whose members, subject to the approval of the Governor, will be appointed by the Ruler. The main functions of these Advisory Malay Councils will relate to matters affecting Mohammedan religion but they will also advise the Ruler on other matters at the request of the Resident Commissioner, acting with the Governor's approval. On such matters (with the exception of anything connected with the collection of taxes or tithes, which must be strictly left within the control of the central legislature or the State Council), it is proposed that the Ruler in Malay Advisory Councils shall have powers of legislation. Such legislation will require the Governor's assent and he will in no case grant or refuse his assent without consulting his own Advisory Council of Malay Rulers, though he will not be bound to accept its advice.

15. The residences and estates of the Rulers themselves and any buildings and lands which are used for the purposes of Mohammedan religion, as well as property pertaining to the endowment of Mohammedan religious institutions will be excluded from the transfer of State and Settlement properties to Union ownership. While the emoluments of the Rulers cannot be exempt from transfer to Union control, since the States will have only those revenues which they may receive for collection by the decision of the central legislature of the Union, it is nevertheless the intention to ensure that the Rulers' personal allowances shall be continued from year to year.

===== Page 163 =====
On a scale appropriate to their dignity and position.

16. With the intention of effectively broadening the basis of citizenship in the Union to include not only Malays but others who can claim to belong to the country, it is proposed to create by Order-in-Council a Malayan Union Citizenship. The following persons will automatically acquire Malayan Union Citizenship:

(a) persons born in the Union or in the Island of Singapore;

(b) persons who at the date on which the Order-in-Council becomes operative have ordinarily been resident in the Union or the Island of Singapore for ten years out of the preceding fifteen. (In calculating the fifteen years period, the period of Japanese occupation will be disregarded).

It will also be possible for persons to acquire Malayan Union Citizenship on application after five years ordinary residence in the Malayan Union or in Singapore. Such applicants will be required to take an oath of allegiance to the Government of the Malayan Union. Save with the consent of the Governor, no person who is not a Malayan Union citizen shall be admitted to the Public Service, and the appointment of unofficial persons to be members of local Councils and similar bodies shall be confirmed Malayan Union citizens.

17. Apart from the Governor, the three Principal officers of the Union Government will, in the usual practice of a Colonial Government, be the Chief Secretary, the Attorney-General and the Financial Secretary. The previous position of the British Residents and Advisers requires modification. Before the war, the status of these officers was higher than that of any other official save the High Commissioner, but with the establishment of

===== Page 164 =====
The Union Government: their importance will diminish and they will be subordinate to the Chief Secretary of the Union Government. These officers, as well as the officers at the head of the two Settlements of Penang and Malacca, shall be uniformly styled "Resident Commissioners".

18. The Order-in-Council constituting the Union will also provide for Executive and Legislative Councils.

19. The Executive Council will be composed of the Governor and two official and two unofficial members. Of the official members, the Chief Secretary, the Attorney-General, and the Financial Secretary will be ex-officio, while the other two will be nominated by the Governor. The unofficial members will also be nominated by the Governor. A racial basis will not be prescribed for such nominations, but in the first instance it is desirable that there shall be one European, one Malay, one Chinese, and one Indian unofficial member, the Governor being left to nominate the most suitable unofficial to fill the remaining vacancy regardless of race. Save with the consent of the Governor, unofficial membership of the Executive Council will be confined to Malayan Union citizens.

20. The Legislative Council will consist of the Governor as President, and twenty-one official and twenty-one unofficial members. Five of the official members will be the official members of the Executive Council and the remainder will be nominated by the Governor.

As regards the choice of the unofficial members, there has hitherto been no experience of any system of elections in Malaya, and for an initial phase at any rate the unofficial members will be nominated by the Governor. Fourteen of these will represent areas on a population basis.

===== Page 165 =====
basis, and, having regard to the estimated population in 1941, these areas will be:

1. Kedah and Perlis
2. Penang and Province Wellesley
3. Perak
4. Selangor and Pahang
5. Negri Sembilan and Malacca
6. Johore
7. Kelantan and Trengganu.

In view of the preponderance of their communities in these areas, it is clear that in practice unofficial members nominated on this basis will be either Malays or Chinese. The remaining seven unofficial members will therefore be nominated to represent important interests throughout the Union, thereby affording a method to secure adequate representation for European, Indian and Eurasian interests.

21. When the Military Administration ceases and the new Civil Government is established in the Peninsula, it will not be practicable to convene the Legislative Council immediately. At the same time legislation to provide for the change-over to Civil Government will be urgently necessary. To cover the intervening period between the end of the Military Administration and the convening of the Legislative Council, the new constitution will specifically empower the Governor also to legislate with the help of an Advisory Council. The Governor will consult this Advisory Council before enacting legislation, but will not be bound to accept the Council's advice. In selecting the members of this Council the Governor will guide himself as far as possible by the intended composition of the Legislative Council. At the same time provision will be made for the continuing validity of the existing laws of each particular State and Settlement, until altered.

===== Page 166 =====
Altered or repealed by the new legislature and for the continuance of proceedings pending in the Courts.

22. The various Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Melacca comprising the Malayan Union will retain their geographical identities and as such will become the basis for the organisation of local government by the establishment of State and Settlement Councils, deriving their powers from the central legislature of the Union. They will be presided over by the Resident Commissioners and they will be empowered to legislate on all matters which, in the opinion of the Governor in Council, are of a merely local or private nature in the State or Settlement; and

(b) on all subjects in respect of which power is delegated to them by law by the Legislative Council of the Malayan Union.

25. There will be a High Court of the Malayan Union with a Chief Justice at its head, and judges of the High Court will be stationed at various suitable centres throughout the Peninsula. In accordance with previous practice and the needs of economy, the Chief Justice and the Puisne Judges of the Supreme Court in Singapore will be declared ex officio judges in the High Court of the Malayan Union and vice versa as regards the Chief Justice and Puisne Judges of the High Court of the Malayan Union. In practice the Court of Appeal, whether sitting in the Union or in Singapore, will invariably be composed of the two Chief Justices and one Puisne Judge. The new constitution will provide for appeals to the Privy Council from the Court of Appeal of the Malayan Union.

26. In order that the Malayan Union becomes a reality and be endowed in keeping with its constitutional authority and political responsibilities, all the properties, subject

===== Page 167 =====
Subject to certain exceptions as noted below, the member States and Settlements will be transferred to the Union Government, which will also assume full liability for State and Settlement debts. The Union will take over all revenues from the States and Settlements, their cash, bank balances, securities, etc., together with all State land, mines and minerals, and all railways, ports and harbours (with the exception of the port and harbour properties vested in the Penang Harbour Board). The exception to this wholesale transfer of property will be the residences and estates of the Malay Rulers themselves and any buildings and land which are used for the purposes of Mohammedan religion, as well as property pertaining to the endowment of Mohammedan religious institutions. As Penang and Malacca will, under the new constitution, be transferred from the jurisdiction of the Government of the Straits Settlements to that of the Malayan Union, it will be necessary for an agreement to be reached as to what is to be the division of the assets and liabilities of these two Settlements between the new Government of the Colony of Singapore and the Government of the Malayan Union.

25. In considering the need for a clear integration of the Governments of Malaysia, His Majesty's Government have concluded that, for the time being at any rate, the Island of Singapore requires separate treatment and should not be a part of the Malayan Union. As a great centre of entrepot trade and predominantly Chinese in population, Singapore has interests distinct from those of the mainland, as a producer largely for export, and it is vital that at the outset the successful establishment of the Malayan Union,

===== Page 168 =====
With its own capital, should not be imperilled by the divergence of view and friction that might possibly arise if the Island and the Peninsula were joined in one political group. The Settlement of Singapore including the Cocos or Keeling Islands and Christmas Island but excluding Labuan, will therefore be constituted as the Colony of Singapore and will be governed by a Governor with the assistance of an Executive and a Legislative Council.

26. The Executive Council will consist of the Governor (Chairman), five ex officio Members and such Official Members, and such Unofficial Members not exceeding three as the Governor on His Majesty's instructions may appoint.

27. The Legislative Council will consist of the Governor (President), four ex officio Members, five Official Members to be nominated by the Governor, nine elected Unofficial Members and two nominated Unofficial Members. An unofficial majority will therefore be created. The four ex officio Members will be:

(1) The Colonial Secretary;
(2) The Senior Officer Commanding the Troops, Singapore;
(3) The Attorney General; and
(4) The Financial Secretary.

The choice of the five Official Members will be in the Governor's discretion, subject to His Majesty's Instructions. It is not considered possible at this stage to introduce any form of individual electoral franchise for the election of the nine Unofficial Members. The proposal is to grant to representative bodies and Associations the right to elect representatives.

===== Page 169 =====
To seats on the Legislative Council, but it is not proposed to specify at this stage the bodies or Associations to whom these powers will be entrusted. This matter will be considered by the Governor, who will review in the interval between his appointment and the convening of the Legislative Council not only the claims to representation of existing bodies or Associations but also other sections of the community not at present effectively organised to express their political views. It will be a condition of the grant of a right of election to the Legislative Council to the bodies or Associations concerned that their rules governing procedure for the election shall first have been approved by the Governor. The Governor will be empowered to nominate members to represent communities pending the formation of representative Associations.

28. It is intended that there shall be a Court of unlimited civil and criminal jurisdiction in the Colony, which will be a successor to and exercise similar powers to those formerly exercised by the Supreme Court of the Straits Settlements.

29. Local Government in the Island of Singapore was carried out in the town area by the Municipal Commission, which was an unofficial body levying its own rates, and in the rural areas by the Rural Board, which was manned by Government officers and subsidised from Government resources. To reduce the number of administrative bodies and to provide uniformity and centralised control in what is territorially a small area, it is proposed eventually to abolish the Rural Board and to extend the limits of the Municipality to cover the whole Island. The Municipal Commission,

===== Page 170 =====
Commission, as reconstituted, will consist of the President, who will be a senior officer of the Malayan Civil Service, and of twenty-four unofficial members. Of these, sixteen will be elected by various representative bodies or Associations, and eight will be appointed by the Governor. In making these appointments, the Governor will pay particular attention to securing adequate representation of rural areas. Four Commissioners will represent rural areas, and there is intended to be adequate representation of the various sections and interests of the community in proportion to their relative importance and their contribution to the prosperity and welfare of the Colony.

30. The Singapore Improvement Trust, a separate body but closely connected with the Municipal Commission and with the Rural Board, formerly dealt with slum clearance, town improvement, and town and country planning. It was financed by an Improvement rate levied on property within the Municipality and by a contribution from the general revenue of the Colony. It is proposed that the Improvement Trust shall be reconstituted as before with a Board consisting of eleven Members, five of whom are ex officio Trustees and six are nominated Trustees.

31. The Singapore Harbour Board, which owned and administered the Dock and Harbour area, will be constituted before with a Chairman and not less than four or more than eight other Members, all appointed by the Governor.

32. It is vital that Singapore should be assured of a fair share of trade with the Peninsula and that it should not suffer from any discriminatory tariff which might be imposed by the Malayan Union in favour of Penang or Malacca. It is recommended, therefore, that it shall be provided in

===== Page 171 =====
Some appropriate way, whether in Royal Instructions issued to the Governors of Singapore and of the Malayan Union respectively or in some other constitutional instrument, that no legislation or tariff or other duty, which the Governor of Singapore represents to be likely to injure the interest of Singapore, shall be enacted or brought into effect without the prior sanction of the Secretary of State.

33. When the Military Administration ceases and the new Civil Government is established in Singapore, it will be impracticable to convene the Legislative Council immediately. At the same time, legislation to provide for the change-over to Civil Government will be urgently necessary. To cover the period between the end of the Military Administration and the convening of the Legislative Council, the new Constitution will specifically empower the Governor alone to legislate with the help of an Advisory Council. The Governor will consult this Advisory Council before enacting legislation but will not be bound to accept the Council's advice. In selecting the members of the Council, the Governor will guide himself as far as is practicable by the intended composition of the Legislative Council.

34. It is recognised that there were and will be close ties between the Island and the Peninsula, and it is no part of the policy of His Majesty's Government to preclude or prejudice in any way the fusion of the two administrations in a wider union at a later date should they both agree to such a course. In the meantime, there is a special need to develop harmonious and mutually beneficial relations between them on matters of trade and subjects of pen-Malayan importance, such as Posts and Telegraphs, Immigration,

===== Page 172 =====
Currency, Civil Aviation, and Shipping. These subjects, which require identity of policy throughout Malaysia, will be regulated by particular agreements between the Union and the Colony.

61341 cipher C.A.1. 24 Oct 45. TOP SECRET.

Following from Colonial Office.

BEGIN. From Secretary of State for the Colonies.

Your 466 B.M.A. (M) cipher 9 Oct. The wording of paragraph 5 of the Memorandum of 20th September on future constitution regarding membership of the Legislative Council is based on final memorandum prepared in consultation with Willan which in turn repeated wording of second draft containing amendments consequential on discussions held last December.

The point about allowing for a future unofficial majority is appreciated but if Legislative Council is constituted as stated in memorandum of 20th September this would not finalise constitution permanently nor bar future amendments to permit unofficial majority when time considered appropriate for change. It would however be desirable for such change-over which would represent an important stage in constitutional development to be made formally by order-in-council and not by administrative action as would happen if seats for official members were not filled to total of twenty-one. Other constitutional changes would then need consideration, especially vesting of reserved powers of legislation in the Governor.

Following for Greater Colonial Office from MacMichael.

I note that paragraph 10 of memorandum on future constitution for Malayan Peninsula, in referring to properties to be excluded from transfer to Union ownership, restricts allusion to religious properties of Mohammedans only. In drafting Order in Council it will obviously be desirable (repeat desirable) to obviate any possible charge of discrimination against other religions (repeat religions).

Of course such criticisms would have no (repeat no) real force since question of transfer only arises in case of State (repeat State) property but this does not make misunderstanding any less undesirable.

EMERGENCY

Cite 142 TOP SECRET.

Addressed to S. of S. for the Colonies.
Repeated to S.A.C.S.R.A.

Following from MacMichael.

1. His Highness the Sultan of Johore and I, duly signed the treaty at 4.25 this afternoon, 20th October, in an atmosphere of greatest possible friendliness.

2. The question of publicity arises. I have asked the Sultan not to inform anyone that he has signed, but to reply to any enquiries that negotiations are proceeding in a smooth and cordial atmosphere, and that no doubt a formal announcement will be made by His Majesty's Government in due course. I shall take a similar line myself in the event of enquiries, and General Dempsey, with whom I have discussed the matter, is giving similar instructions. This will not, of course, preclude my showing any other Sultan, with whom I am dealing, the treaty signed by Sultan of Johore, in confidence.

===== Page 179 =====
The fact that a treaty or treaties has been signed will no doubt leak out sooner or later, but I assume that the first formal announcement should come from your end, and that informal announcements from this end are therefore out of place. Moreover, it is important that the remaining Sultans should not get the impression that their hands are being publicly forced by pressure of publicity.
