===== Page 226 =====
17th March, 1945.

SECRET.

Dear Gent,

I enclose the further note about your problems in the Malay States which I promised you. The 1944 correspondence to which the note refers included Mr. Amery's letter of 1st May to your Secretary of State and Kemp's letter of 27th June to Duncan.

The outcome of your policy in Malaya may have repercussions in India, and we should be very grateful if you could keep us in touch with developments.

If there is any further help that we could give, Sir Kenneth Fitze, Political Adviser to the Secretary of State, and I would be glad to come over and discuss.

G.E.J. Gent Esq., C.M.G., D.S.O., O.B.E., M.C.

===== Page 227 =====
The policy of H.M.G. is stated in Annex II to W.P.(44) 762. The immediate object is to seek new treaties with the Rulers which would give His Majesty a sufficient measure of jurisdiction to enable him to legislate for the States under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. The ultimate intention is to create a Malayan Union, under a Governor with an Executive and a Legislative Council, which would include both the British Settlements and the Malay States. The Colonial Office now asks advice on how to ensure that H.M.G.'s policy is not frustrated by the possible recalcitrance of individual Rulers.

There is no precise parallel in India to the policy which H.M.G. proposes to apply to the Malayan Rulers. It is the case that in very small states, e.g., in Western India, the Crown has in the past absorbed pretty full jurisdiction: and many Rulers have been required to cede jurisdiction over Railway lands, cantonment areas, and the like, within State territory. But broadly speaking, there is no precedent in India (subject to the very doubtful one of the Chaube Jagirs, on which a note is attached as Appendix I) for the permanent removal of general jurisdiction from a Ruler's dynasty, in the face of the Ruler's opposition, since the policy of annexation was abandoned in 1858. The jurisdiction ceded by Rulers over Railway lands and in cantonment areas has been exercised by the Crown Representative, in succession since the Act of 1935 to the Governor-General in Council, under the India Foreign Jurisdiction Order in Council, 1937. The same Order has been used to express the powers of administration assumed by the Crown in certain very small States without having obtained these by a formal cession from the Rulers. When however, under the Act of 1935 it was proposed to constitute a Federation of India with a responsible government and legislature, the Rulers were invited to accede to the Federation with such limitations on the direct exercise of Federal authority in their territories as might be mutually agreed with the Crown: there was no question of compelling any Ruler to federate; and from the very small States such as those mentioned above which did not possess the powers to carry out the obligations of federation, the offer to federate was withheld.

Some parallel to H.M.G.'s policy for Malaya may be found in the agreements negotiated in 1883, 1899, and 1903 with the Khan of Kalat where, by areas of his territory known as the Quetta Niabat, the Nushki District and Niabat, and the Niabat of Nasirabad, were leased in perpetuity to form a considerable part of the existing Chief Commissioner's Province of British Baluchistan for quit-rents amounting to something over Rs. 1½ lakhs. In this portion of British Baluchistan, administration is expressed to be under the Foreign Jurisdiction Order in Council. But there is at present no legislature in this Province which is administered personally by the Chief Commissioner.

The case of Berar affords a somewhat nearer parallel. Berar came under British administration in 1853, when it was assigned "temporarily" to the E.I. Company to meet certain financial obligations of the Nizam of Hyderabad. In 1902 a perpetual lease was negotiated with the Nizam, on payment of Rs. 25 lakhs a year. When the Government of India Act of 1935 was passed, it was necessary to provide for Berar (which had been administered directly — i.e., not under foreign jurisdiction — as part of the Central Provinces) passing under the government of an Autonomous Province, and a new Agreement was negotiated with the Nizam: this involved various concessions. A dynastic title incorporating Berar was granted him, the annual payment of Rs. 25 lakhs was confirmed, certain rights in regard to the flying of his flag, grant by him of honours, holding of Durbars, etc., were accorded; he is consulted before a Governor is appointed and has the right to maintain an agent at the provincial capital. Further if the

===== Page 228 =====
Government of India Act of 1935 is amended in a manner inconsistent with the Agreement, the Nizam may at six months' notice terminate its continuance.

The Colonial Office have already been warned (correspondence in May/June 1944) of the difficulties that arise if—as in the case of Berar—the cession by the Ruler of jurisdiction is not sufficiently full to avoid the necessity of securing a fresh agreement from him whenever constitutional changes are made in the government of the territory involved. The other important point to be observed is that the Berar agreements were obtained by negotiation and concessions. There was no open resistance on the part of the Khan of Kalat or the Nizam of Hyderabad to the terms of the agreements which they concluded ceding jurisdiction in portions of their territories. Nor have Rulers resisted the cession without consideration of jurisdiction over Railway lands or lands required for military cantonments. If resistance had been offered, despite the indirect advantage to be obtained from the cession of jurisdiction desired, it would have no doubt been necessary to "advise" the Ruler in the name of the Paramount Power to execute the necessary agreement. The right of the Paramount Power to override opposition from the Ruler of a State was asserted in a letter from Lord Reading in 1926 to the Nizam of Hyderabad. Some general propositions extracted from that letter are attached as Appendix III.

In the exercise of the functions of Paramountcy it has not been uncommon for a Ruler to be deprived of his Ruling Powers (as a result, e.g., of gross misgovernment), but such action by the Paramount Power is confined to an individual Ruler and does not extend to the dynasty. A note is attached (Appendix III) which shows inter alia the limits which the Paramount Power has set upon the exercise of its rights of interference in a Ruler's jurisdiction. When a Ruler is deprived of his powers, the Crown Representative acts by virtue of the general supremacy and paramount authority of the Crown, and substitutes for the Ruler, as an act of State, a person (probably a British official) who is for the time being the supreme judicial and administrative authority in the State. Reforms of an essential nature may be introduced, but no major constitutional changes are made. The same applies during a Minority Administration. It will thus be seen that the practice of Paramountcy in India gives little help in the present situation.

The only useful advice one can give to the Colonial Office is:

(1) It would be best to cajole, or bribe, the Rulers (taking the larger first) than attempt to coerce them.

(2) If coercion is inevitable, an arbitrary Act of State, supported by troops if necessary, is the only thing. Presumably a Proclamation would be issued showing the grounds on which action was being taken ("efficiency and democratic progress"). This would in effect mean unilateral denunciation of treaty provisions such as Article 3 of the 1923 Kedah Agreement; some publicity and perhaps parliamentary criticism might be anticipated.

===== Page 229 =====
The CHAUBE JAGIRS are five small contiguous estates, with a total area of 142 square miles, a population of 25,000, and revenue of Rs. 96,000 (about £7,200). The Jagirdars inherited grants from their ancestors, which the British Government confirmed to them and their descendants in the possession of their villages in perpetuity. They conducted independent administrations which, owing to misgovernment and poverty, fell far below modern standards. They resisted efforts to group them voluntarily in a cooperative scheme of administration, and in 1941 the Secretary of State approved a proposal to deprive all five of their jurisdiction and set up a joint administration on a permanent footing. It has not yet been decided whether this case has signalized a new departure in political practice. The joint administration is run in the name of the Jagirdars, and might be described merely as the operation of formal "advice" from the Paramount Power. But it is of some significance that the intention is to deprive the jagirdars' dynasties of jurisdiction. The places are, however, so small that this "Act of State," even if properly so described, is unlikely to attract attention. It might, in fact, be said that if a ruler is small enough, it is safe to bully him.

===== Page 230 =====
APPENDIX II

Hyderabad case, 1926

From the letter of His Excellency Lord Reading to His Exalted Highness the Nizam the following general propositions may be extracted:

"The Sovereignty of the British Crown is supreme in India, and therefore no Ruler of an Indian State can justifiably claim to negotiate with the British Government on an equal footing. Its supremacy is not based only upon treaties and engagements, but exists independently of them and, quite apart from its prerogative in matters relating to foreign powers and policies, it is the right and duty of the British Government, while scrupulously respecting all treaties and engagements with the Indian States, to preserve peace and good order throughout India.

"The right of the British Government to intervene in the internal affairs of Indian States is another instance of the consequences necessarily involved in the supremacy of the British Crown. The British Government have indeed shown again and again that they have no desire to exercise this right without grave reason. But the internal, no less than the external, security which the Ruling Princes enjoy is due ultimately to the protecting power of the British Government, and where Imperial interests are concerned, or the general welfare of the people of a State is seriously and grievously affected by the action of its Government, it is with the Paramount Power that the ultimate responsibility of taking remedial action, if necessary, must lie. The varying degrees of internal sovereignty which the Rulers enjoy are all subject to the due exercise by the Paramount Power of this responsibility.

"It is the right and privilege of the Paramount Power to decide all disputes that may arise between States, or between one of the States and itself, and even though a Court of Arbitration may be appointed in certain cases, its function is merely to offer independent advice to the Government of India, with whom the decision rests."

===== Page 231 =====
Appendix II

British Responsibilities to the Indian Princes

In Chapter 9, Part I, Vol. I of the Indian Statutory Commission's Report is quoted the following passage from the Montagu-Chelmsford Report:

"In spite of the varieties and complexities of treaties, engagements and sanads, the general position as regards the rights and obligations of the Native States can be summed up in a few words. The States are guaranteed security from without; the paramount power acts for them in relation to foreign powers and other States, and it intervenes when the internal peace of their territories is seriously threatened. On the other hand, the States' relations to foreign powers are those of the paramount power; they share the obligation for the common defence; and they are under a general responsibility for the good government and welfare of their territories."

2. The formal basis for British responsibilities is primarily the Corpus of Treaties, Engagements and Sanads. Only with some 40 States were Instruments in the form of Treaties actually concluded; but most States have agreements of a formal character or Sanads. They all involve acceptance by the British Government of responsibility for the external relations of the States and for their external and internal defence. But the relationship so established varied in accordance with the circumstances of the time when the individual engagements were made. The leading features of the three main historical periods of treaty-making are briefly described in Annexure 1 to this note.

3. Interpretation of British Responsibilities in the light of changing conditions. In a Despatch in 1917 the Secretary of State for India, Sir Austen Chamberlain, wrote:

"In the scrupulous maintenance of our Treaty Obligations nothing less than the honour of the Sovereign is involved and no Government can seek to belittle them. The relations of Government with the Chiefs are, however, necessarily subject to variation, and the literal fulfilment of an obligation may become impossible, either through change in essential circumstances, or by the mere passage of time. Again many of the Treaties were concluded before the Crown stood forth - in Lord Canning's phrase - the unquestioned Ruler and Paramount Power in all India; and, since that event, considerations of the general good of the Indian body politic have necessarily become a new factor in their interpretation. While they remain unrepealed in the letter and binding in the spirit, a constant development of constitutional doctrine is in process. But of that process the superintendence, direction and control must remain in the hands of the Paramount Power".

"Sanad" may be translated as a diploma, patent, or deed of grant by a Sovereign of an office, privilege, or right.

===== Page 232 =====
A careful analysis of the only actual treaties with Indian States (some 40 in number as stated in para. 1) was made by the Government of India in a note attached to a Despatch of 1930. Though too lengthy for reproduction as an annexure to this note, this analysis can be readily made available for perusal if desired. It shows that only half these treaties had assurances of "absolutism", and that there is good cause for interpreting even these assurances at less than their face value. In fact, it may be said that the responsibility of the British Government for the protection of the Indian States has involved, in the process of time and by the exercise of paramount power, a series of encroachments on the internal sovereignty of the Rulers, which it was originally responsible for maintaining. The Canning Sanads of 1862 (see para. 5 above) expressed the "desire of the Queen Empress that the governments of the Sanad-holders should be perpetuated", but it is a natural corollary that "nothing which is inherently capable of survival should be artificially perpetuated," (the latter quotation is from a communique issued by the Government Representative on the 16th April, 1943, in connection with the Western India Attachment scheme).

4. Occasions of Intervention and extent to which above definitions of British Responsibilities are accepted by Indian Rulers. An endeavour to list the encroachments on the internal affairs of States was made by the Government of India in the same Despatch of 1930. The "occasions of intervention" as they were then described are detailed in Annexure 2 to this note. Some of these "occasions of intervention" are obviously acceptable to Rulers, some indeed are embodied in agreements with Rulers. But in regard to many of them (e.g., intervention to improve the administration) there has been much controversy. In fact, the doctrine of Paramountcy as defined in Sir Austen Chamberlain's Despatch (para. 7 above) has never been accepted by the Princes as a whole. Sir Leslie Scott and other counsel were briefed by a body of them to represent to the Indian States Enquiry Committee in 1928-9 (the Butler Committee) that their relationship with the Crown is contractual in a sense and to an extent which precludes either party from modifying it except with full and willing acquiescence of the other. They contended that the Rulers continue to be sovereign and independent in their internal affairs except to the extent to which they have expressly surrendered any portion of that sovereignty. The Butler Committee did not accept this contention, its conclusion being that the relationship between the Crown and the States is sui generis.

"We have endeavoured, as others before us, to find some formula which will cover the exercise of paramountcy, and we have failed, as others before us have failed, to do so. The reason for such failure is not far to seek. Conditions alter rapidly in a changing world, Imperial necessity and new conditions may at any time raise unexpected situations. Paramountcy must remain paramount; it must fulfil its obligations, defining or adopting itself according to the shifting necessities of the time and the progressive development of the States. Nor need the States take alarm at this conclusion. Through paramountcy and paramountcy alone have grown up and flourished those strong benign relations between the Crown and the Princes on which at all times the States rely. On paramountcy and paramountcy alone can the States rely for their preservation through the generations that are to come. Through paramountcy is pushed aside the danger of destruction or annexation," (para. 47 of Butler Committee Report).

===== Page 233 =====
5. Individual Obligations to States. This note does not deal with the various specific obligations owed by the Crown to individual States, which might be said to come under the head of British Responsibilities. Many of these were considered in detail by the Indian States (Financial) Enquiry Committee (the Davidson Committee) in 1932, with a view to their absorption in the scheme of Federation. They include rights in respect of Salt, Posts and Telegraphs, Customs in a few cases, and currency claimed on the basis of written engagements or usage against a regime otherwise applicable by law in British India and by agreement with the State to India as a whole.

Obviously the Crown is responsible for ensuring that the Princes are enabled to negotiate a fair bargain in respect of these rights as a condition of inclusion in any all-India federation, and in fact they formed one of the main subjects of the Federal discussions with the Princes up to the outbreak of War.

There are also specific undertakings to keep "subsidiary forces" in certain States (Hyderabad, Cochin, etc.) which in many instances have either lapsed or been honoured in the breach. They may be said to fall under the general responsibility for protection and it is pertinent to observe that in several important cases (notably Hyderabad and Baroda) the assurance of protection and the resources for maintaining the subsidiary protective forces were dependent upon cessions by the States concerned of great tracts of territory which thus became embodied in British India. Hyderabad has gone so far as to suggest that any withdrawal of the assurances would involve the return of these territories.

===== Page 234 =====
The Treaty Making Periods

Up to the French Withdrawal. From 1730 (Treaty with Sawantwadi for protection against piracy, offensive and defensive) up to 1813, agreements concluded with some 48 States (mainly along fringes of the Peninsula in the portion of India where we were in contact with the Maharatta Power) were mainly of the character appropriate to engagements between equal contracting parties for common ends.

The Subordinate Isolation Period. Between 1814 to 1857 relations with the majority of the States were established as British power extended, and the tone of the language of engagements noticeably changed; admissions of British supremacy and professions of attachment and fidelity were often included. Though in and about 1818 a group of treaties were concluded, mainly by Lord Lake, with States in Rajputana and Central India such as Udaipur, Jodhpur, Bikaner, and Bhopal, which contained a clause recognising the Ruler as sovereign and promising that British jurisdiction should never be extended to his territories.

The Post-Mutiny Period. A new conception of British responsibilities was inaugurated by Lord Canning. In 1860 he laid down two principles: (1) that the integrity of States should be preserved by perpetuating the Rule of the Princes, (2) that flagrant mis-government must be prevented or arrested by timely intervention. In connection with (1), he issued a series of Sanads in 1862 guaranteeing the recognition of heirs adopted by Rulers without natural heirs.

Queen Victoria's Proclamation of 1858. It was in this period that the Queen-Empress issued the Proclamation to which the utmost importance is attached by the Princes:

"We hereby announce to the Native Princes of India that all Treaties and Engagements made with them by or under the authority of the Honourable East India Company are by Us accepted and will be scrupulously observed; and We look for the like observance on their part. We desire no extension of the territorial possessions, and while We admit no aggression upon Our dominions or Our rights to be attempted with impunity, We shall sanction no encroachment on those of others. We shall respect the rights, dignity, and honour of Native Princes as Our own; and We desire that they as well as Our own subjects shall enjoy that prosperity and that social advancement which can only be secured by internal peace and good government."

The essential features of this pronouncement have been repeated in subsequent Royal Proclamation (though not by the present Monarch or his Predecessor).

===== Page 235 =====
These were stated by the Government of India to be for the following purposes: to check maladministration and expedite reforms (the extent of legitimate intervention is of course very controversial ground), to regulate disputed successions, to remove Rulers guilty of criminal or flagrantly immoral conduct unconnected with their ruling power, to supervise minority Regency administrations and training of minor Rulers, to assist Rulers against rebellion, to prevent dismemberment of States, to settle boundary and interstatal disputes, to control arrangements for defence and security (which may include e.g. limitation of armaments, provision of expert advice, location of British cantonments, passage of British troops, location and maintenance of strategic railways and other communications, control of wireless, telephones, aviation developments, exclusion of undesirable foreign influences, jurisdiction over Indian Army personnel, arrangements for recruitment to Imperial forces, etc.), to perform functions outside the competence of small States (e.g. judicial, financial, commercial arrangements), to administer all-India subjects such as Ports and Communications, to fulfil obligations arising out of the Paramount Power's control of external relations (e.g. jurisdiction over foreigners, enforcement of international obligations), to restrict the employment of Europeans and Government pensioners, to protect persons holding authoritative British guarantees.

===== Page 244 =====
NOTE ON PROPOSALS FOR FUTURE MALAYAN CONSTITUTION

The War Cabinet has approved, for planning purposes, the policy of establishing after the war a Malayan Union which will consist of the nine Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, and that this shall be achieved by the negotiation of fresh Agreements with the Rulers which shall cede such jurisdiction to His Majesty as will enable him to legislate for their territories under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. Attached to this note, as Appendix A, is a memorandum prepared in consultation between the Colonial Office and the Malayan Planning Unit, which contains detailed proposals for the framing of the post-war Malayan Constitution and which deals with the practical application of the policy approved by the War Cabinet. It is felt that each of the proposals contained in the memorandum is implicit in the policy itself and is inherent in the very idea of a Malayan Union (as opposed to an extended Federation of Malay States).

For convenience, a brief summary of the memorandum is also attached to this note, as Appendix B.

2. The memorandum has been discussed in draft with Sir Harold MacMichael, and in consultation with him the following points have emerged, which may not be suitable for inclusion in the memorandum itself, but which it is nevertheless desirable to bring to the attention of the Secretary of State.

3. The cession of jurisdiction by each Ruler to His Majesty is accepted as a necessary step, but it is one which is capable of being misrepresented by ignorant, and still more by mischievous, people as a wanton breach of faith and an act of naked imperialism. If the negotiations

===== Page 245 =====
Negotiations are allowed to become "bogged" by argument and counter-argument; the chances diminish of our policy being represented in the right light from the outset, and the opportunities for misrepresentation will increase. This cardinal consideration leads to several conclusions.

4. The timing of the negotiations with the Malay Rulers is of the utmost importance. Missing opportunities through inadequate forethought or insufficient boldness of outlook, leading inevitably to uncertainty of touch and consequent hesitation, would not only jeopardize the negotiations themselves but would have other ill-effects which might be very far-reaching. Consequently, the work of elaborating the proposed new Constitution and drafting the necessary Instruments should be achieved as rapidly as possible. Once the present draft memorandum has been approved by the Secretary of State, with such alterations as he desires to make, the drafting of an Order in Council establishing the new Constitution should be begun immediately.

5. The actual Treaties with the Rulers will, of course, be very short, and will merely enshrine the cession of jurisdiction to His Majesty. They will not go into the consequential details of what His Majesty will do with the jurisdiction when acquired. Nevertheless, these consequences will be of the greatest interest to the Malay Rulers, and it may be assumed that they will fundamentally affect their willingness to sign the Treaties. While there is room for argument as to whether the actual draft Order in Council should be shown to them or not, it is important that we should be able to explain our proposals to them in a clear, comprehensive and convincing manner (and in a Malay language version).

6. Equally important, if we are to attain our objective of a smooth and rapid settlement, will be the method and procedure of handling individual Rulers both before and during the negotiations. It has been agreed

===== Page 246 =====
With S.A.C., S.E.A., the Military authorities shall not be involved in the negotiations, which are to be left to Sir Harold MacMichael. Nevertheless, it is essential that the Military authorities should be thoroughly acquainted with the situation and should avoid any action which may subsequently embarrass Sir Harold's task. In particular, they should not formally recognize any claimant in any of the nine States as being the legitimate Ruler of that State, even if he has held such a position uninterruptedly since before the Japanese occupation. This will mean considerable vigilance, since claimants may be expected to try and establish their position at the earliest possible moment and by all possible means. It will no doubt be necessary for the Military authorities to use the de facto authority of this or that Malay personage in each area in order to achieve their essential objectives of maintenance of security, prevention of disease and unrest, distribution of supplies, etc.; but it is essential that none of the Rulers should have been placed by the Military in a securely established position by the time when Sir Harold arrives in Malaya. In the first place, a recognized Malay Ruler who refused to sign a new Treaty might have to be deposed (an extremely inconvenient course, to say the least); and in the second place, if any of the Rulers feel that the ground is firm under their feet, they will unquestionably be more likely to adopt a non-cooperative, obstructive, and non-possumus attitude. They must realize that for their reinstatement their willingness to fall in with the policy of H.M.G. will be an indispensable condition. This does not mean, of course, that H.M.G. will be prepared to recognize as a Ruler of any of the States whoever is "prepared to sign on the dotted line." Such a course might mean the acceptance in some or all cases of the most worthless candidate and this again would open the door to reasonable criticism and be, indeed, a poor beginning to the history of the new Malaya. In the event

===== Page 247 =====
Event of an otherwise worthy claimant being unwilling to follow the accepted policy of H.M.G., great efforts would doubtless be made to persuade him before drastic steps were taken. But it must be recognised that in the last resort a "potential" Ruler who remained adamant might have to be displaced in favour of the best alternative candidate; and no Ruler should therefore be officially recognised before the negotiations are concluded.

7. For the rest, the Military authorities can be of great assistance to Sir Harold when he starts his work. For the reasons already given, there should be no unduly protracted delay in his arrival, after the liberation of the Peninsula. On the other hand, it would be a mistake if he were to appear on the scene immediately. There should be a brief period of, say, one month, during which the Military Administration would have time to assess the position in each of the States. They could then advise Sir Harold, on his arrival, as to the "candidate" in each State whose claims and conduct entitle him in their view to be the chosen instrument for the negotiations and the local throne. In many cases, the choice would probably be obvious. Sir Harold would then pursue his negotiations, proceeding in each case according to the established local custom. (The customs of the various States are being examined, and he will be provided with the facts before his departure). Thus, in some States it may be necessary for the new Treaty to be signed by certain of the State Chiefs as well as by the Ruler himself. The Ruler must be recognised before he actually signs the Treaty, since the signature of the State Ruler is necessary; but he should be firmly committed to sign (perhaps by initialling a draft after obtaining whatever approval of Chiefs is required by the custom of his State) before recognition takes place.

8. Then again, if the negotiations are to result in that speedy settlement at which we should aim, it will be important that Sir Harold MacMichael should have plenipotentiary powers.

===== Page 248 =====
Powers to negotiate on behalf of H.M.G. so that, having worked the Rulers (or "potential" Rulers) up to the point where they are ready to sign, he will be able to clinch the business then and there. If at that point, or at an earlier stage in the proceedings, he has to take refuge in expressions of uncertainty or doubt on issues of vital moment, and to refer home various points raised, this will throw open the door to conditions, qualifications, and second thoughts on the part of the Rulers, as a result of which the opportunity of any (and certainly of a rapid) settlement may be lost.

9. Finally, Sir Harold has represented that his task will be greatly eased if he is able to say something reassuring to the Malays regarding the degree of support to be accorded to their own position vis-à-vis the Chinese. It is true that under the proposals for Malayan Union citizenship, every Malay will automatically achieve citizenship, while only a proportion of Chinese will do so. This is an argument which can be legitimately used to show that the Malays will not be deserted by H.M.G. under the new policy, although our plans embody a belated recognition and regularising of the reasonable rights of those non-Malays who have definitely chosen to make their homes in Malaya, and those plans would in fact go far to safeguard the Malay position in its political aspects to a greater extent than could be said of our policy in the past. The full strength of this argument, however, may not be apparent to those with whom it will be used, and Sir Harold hopes that it may be backed up by others.

10. For instance, it is assumed that he will have every warrant for promising that particular attention will be given to the education of Malays and their training in the field of local government; also that the policy of land reservations for the needs of the Malays will be maintained. He further suggests that it would be both justifiable

===== Page 249 =====
Justifiable and helpful if he could point to a forward policy of encouraging, or at least facilitating, the immigration of Malays from the Netherlands East Indies as a counterpoise to the immigration of Chinese and Indians. The Colonial Office is already in touch with the Foreign Office, with a view to an approach being made to the Dutch Government on this very point. If the Dutch response is favourable, it may be possible to go a long way towards meeting this point; though the need of avoiding any overt discrimination against Chinese and Indians will, it is fully recognised, have to be carefully watched.

11. Above all the question must be faced of the course to be followed if the Rulers, or any of them, flatly decline to sign new Treaties. For reasons suggested in paragraph 6 above it will not be practicable simply to refuse recognition of the recalcitrant party or parties and select more pliable instruments. It is to be assumed, on the other hand, that H.M.G. will not on this account jettison their policy in respect either of those States which refuse assent to it, or, in desperation, of all. What, then, are the ultimate sanctions applicable? It may be impossible at this stage to formulate procedure, step by step, with precision and exactitude, but for purpose of negotiation, it is necessary to success (and particularly so in dealing with an Eastern people) that the broad lines to be followed in such an eventuality should be known both to Sir Harold who must be sure of his ground, and to the several Rulers and Chiefs who must be left in no doubt as to H.M.G.'s definite intention. If once it is clear beyond any doubt that the policy decided upon will be carried out, and doubt is at the same time removed as to the way in which this can and will be done, the issue is made a clear cut one and the chances of protracted obstruction are minimised.

12. H.M.G.'s decision on future policy for Malaya offers a bright hope for the efficiency, security and the promotion of self-government in that country, and it offers a safeguard for the political future of the Malays themselves since they will

===== Page 250 =====
be the most numerous element in the citizenship. It is therefore imperative that a satisfactory answer should be found for the question of ultimate sanctions as, if left unsolved, it may wreck the possibility of achieving that policy.

13. The sanctions which it is possible to consider appear to be the following:

(a) annexation of any State whose ruler refuses to cede jurisdiction;

(b) refusal to recognise as Ruler any claimant who refuses to do so and the acceptance and recognition of the next best on the list of claimants who will agree to fall in with H.M.G's policy.

Of these the first course can be expected to create severe local reactions amongst the Malays throughout the Peninsula, and to be adversely criticised by world opinion. The second would expose us to criticism inside and outside Malaya, though its degree might depend upon the extent to which the chosen ruler was merely a worthless puppet. This course might not be a complete solution if in any State there were no possible Malay candidate willing to accept our terms.

14. It is clear from a consideration of the matter of sanctions, that difficult decisions will remain to be taken if we fail to secure the agreement of each Ruler. It is therefore of the first importance to strengthen Sir Harold MacMichael's position by all possible means in order to secure their agreement; with this object it will be essential that he should be able to express H.M.G's resolve to carry their policy through for Malaya, if necessary without the agreement of any particular Ruler. If in fact any Ruler does refuse agreement, the exact procedure H.M.G. will adopt in order to enforce their policy can probably not be predicted at this stage, and the least objectionable course may only become clear in the light of the actual circumstances. It would not be essential that

Sir Harold

===== Page 251 =====
Sir Harold MacMichael should be able to state definitely to any Ruler what the precise course H.M.G. would adopt if he were to insist on withholding agreement. Sir Harold would find it enough to be able to say with confidence that the policy would certainly be enforced and that it would be H.M.G.'s affair to decide what steps to take for the purpose.

15. To sum up, it is imperative that the negotiations which Sir Harold will have to undertake shall proceed rapidly to a successful conclusion, and with this end in view, the Secretary of State is asked to approve the following points:

(a) That the work of elaborating the new Malayan Constitution and drafting the necessary Instruments shall be set on foot immediately.

(b) That the British Military authorities, on their arrival in Malaya, should be warned of the importance of exercising great caution in their dealings with any claimants to the throne in any of the nine States and of refusing to recognise any such claimant as the de jure authority in the State in question.

(c) That Sir Harold, whose arrival in Malaya should be timed to take place about one month after that of the Military authorities (subject to the progress of operations), should be authorised to open negotiations with each selected "candidate" on the basis that willingness to fall in with the policy of H.M.G. will be an indispensable condition of de jure recognition as Ruler.

(d) That Sir Harold should be granted plenipotentiary powers to bring negotiations to a conclusion on behalf of H.M.G.

(e) That he will be authorised to reassure the Malays regarding the maintenance of their special position, by promising that particular attention will be given to their education and their training in the field of local government, by undertaking that the policy of land

===== Page 252 =====
Land reservations for the needs of the Malays will be maintained, and by pointing (if possible) to a forward policy of encouraging, or at least facilitating, the immigration of Malays from the Netherlands East Indies as a counterpoise to the immigration of Chinese and Indians.

(f) That he will be placed in a position to make it quite clear during the negotiations that the policy of H.M.G. will be carried out by one method or another, whatever the obstacles.

===== Page 253 =====
TOP SECRET

FUTURE CONSTITUTION FOR MALAY PENINSULA.

Division of Malaya into two parts.

1. In the War Cabinet Secret Paper CMB(44)3 dated 14th January, 1944, the Secretary of State for the Colonies sets out, in general terms, the future constitutional policy for British Colonial Territories in South East Asia. It is clear that as regards Malaya the aim is -
(a) A separate Government for Singapore Island.
(b) The Union of the four Federated Malay States, the five Unfederated Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

2. Before discussing a new constitutional policy for Malaya it is as well to state briefly what is the present constitution. Malaya is divided into three parts -
(a) The Straits Settlements, a Crown Colony, for which His Majesty is able to legislate under the Straits Settlements Act, 1866.
(b) The four Malay States of Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan and Pahang over which His Majesty has no powers under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. These States formed themselves into a Federation in 1896, and in 1909 a Federal Council was established which enacted legislation having the force of law throughout the Federation.
(c) The five Unfederated Malay States of Johore, Kedah, Kelantan, Trengganu and Perlis. Each of these States is independent and His Majesty has no powers under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act over any of them. Each such State has its own legislature, namely the Ruler in State Council.

All the nine Malay States are bound to Great Britain by treaties which date from 1874, in the case of Perak, to 1930, in the case of Perlis. The provisions of these treaties follow the same general lines, namely that each Malay Ruler

===== Page 254 =====
Shall accept a British officer whose advice shall be asked and acted upon in all questions other than those concerning Mohammedan religion and Malay custom. In addition, as regards Perlis, the 1930 treaty contains a provision by which His Majesty's Government have undertaken that they "will not merge or combine the State of Perlis with any other State without the written consent of the Raja of Perlis in Council", and, as regards Kedah, by the 1923 treaty, His Majesty's Government have undertaken that they "will not merge or combine the State of Kedah or her territories with any other State or with the Colony of the Straits Settlements without the written consent of His Highness the Sultan in Council".

Under the present constitutional set up legislation for the Settlements of Penang and Malacca is enacted by the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements, legislation for the Federated Malay States by the Federal Council, and legislation for each Unfederated Malay State by the Ruler in State Council of each such State.

In the Federated Malay States, in addition to the Federal Council, each of the four States has its own local legislative body, namely the Ruler in State Council. These State Councils existed prior to British intervention in Malaya. After that intervention and the establishment of the Federal Council, legislative enactments of the State Councils decreased in Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan and Pahang as the following figures show:

| Enactments passed by State Councils | 1900 - 1909 | 1910 - 1925 |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| Perak | 181 | 17 |
| Selangor | 188 | 13 |
| Negri Sembilan | 189 | 13 |
| Pahang | 175 | 20 |

In 1932, as a result of the policy of decentralization and constitutional re-organisation, the State Councils were reconstituted so as to be transmuted into representative and

===== Page 255 =====
Efficient bodies. Under that policy of decentralization, certain subjects were designated as State and others as Federal matters, and legislation on such subjects was enacted by the State or Federal Council in accordance with the appropriate designation. In any case in which doubt existed as to whether legislation on a particular matter should be enacted by the State or Federal Council, it was decided on the advice of the four Residents. It cannot be said that the Residents had any clear uniform views on whether a law should be State or Federal, nor did their decisions appear to follow any well-defined principles.

The effect of the reconstitution of the State Councils in each of the four Federated Malay States in 1932 was that more legislation was enacted by them, as is seen by the following figures:

Enactments passed by State Councils
1935 - 1940

Perak............... 32
Selangor............ 36
Negri Sembilan...... 27
Pahang............... 28

But, if the Supply and Final Supply (i.e., State Budgets) Enactments (in the period mentioned they amount to an average of 11 Enactments in each State) and also certain Enactments which, though enacted separately in each State, contain provisions which are practically identical throughout the four States (i.e., they might just as well have been Federal Enactments) are not taken into account, very few laws remain which, on account of relating primarily to local matters, needed to be enacted by State Councils. In fact, when the State legislation for the period 1935 - 1940 is examined, it is true to say that only Enactments such as the Dindings Cession Enactment and the Sultan Idris Religious and Charitable Trust (Amendment) Enactment in Perak, the Malayan Collieries Railway Enactment in Selangor, and a few other Enactments were

===== Page 256 =====
Really necessary to be enacted as State Enactments.

Accordingly, even after the policy of decentralization had been implemented, it could not be said that in the Federated Malay States, so far as legislation was concerned, State Councils played a vital part in the constitutional setup. I have no doubt that the reason for the reconstitution of State Councils in 1932 was a political step in an attempt to loosen the ties of the Federation in the hope of achieving a greater Union later, and not because such Councils were of any real value.

3. Reverting to the Secretary of State's Memorandum contained in the Secret Paper CMB(44)3 of the 14th January, 1944, the first step in the formation of a Malayan Union is that a fresh treaty should be concluded with each Sultan as soon as possible after re-occupation, under which such jurisdiction would be ceded to His Majesty as would enable him to legislate for the Malay States under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. When those fresh treaties have been executed, a new constitution can be made by Order-in-Council under statutory powers. This must provide for some kind of constitutional union of the nine Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

In view of the authoritative statements which have been made regarding the independence of the Malay States, and the assurances given to the Malay Rulers that His Majesty's Government have no intention of impairing that independence, it is appropriate to quote here some of them because they are directly contrary to the present policy for the formation of a Malayan Union. The main assurances and the years in which they were made are:

(i) Sir John Anderson, High Commissioner for the Malay States, 1909.

"It was no small sacrifice of individuality on the part of the Rulers who entered on the compact of federation, and to complete and crown it by the Agreement in virtue of which we are assembled. They made that sacrifice willingly and gladly, because

===== Page 257 =====
They felt the debt which they owe to the protection and help which has been extended to them by England, and because they feel that the prosperity and progress of their States is bound up with the advancement of British interests in the Malay Peninsula. They have done this in the full confidence, based on the facts of the past, that, whatever is done by the British Government or by this Council, the faith and obligations of the treaties under which they agreed to accept British Advisers will always be scrupulously observed. They are confident that we will never forget that our powers are derived wholly from their gift, and that we are here in a Malay country as advisers and counsellors of its Malay sovereigns. I am sure that every one of us will bear that in mind, and that the Rulers, who have so earnestly at heart the real good and advancement of the people subject to them, will never have cause to regret that they agreed to this step.

(ii) Sir Laurence Nurns Guillemard, High Commissioner for the Malay States, 1921.

In Federal Council Sir Laurence Guillemard said it was not the policy of Government "to exert, in any manner whatsoever, any pressure upon any other State to enter the Federation." He went on to say that if other States chose to apply for membership their applications would be carefully considered. He stressed that the general policy was one of friendly co-operation between the several Malayan units in all matters of common interest, on the understanding that each party was free to act as it thought best in local matters.

(iii) Sir Hugh Clifford, High Commissioner for the Malay States, 1927.

In Federal Council Sir Hugh Clifford said -

"The adoption of any kind of Government by majority would forthwith entail the complete submersion of the indigenous population who would find themselves hopelessly outnumbered by the folk of other races; and this would produce a situation which would amount to a betrayal of trust which the Malays of these States, from the highest to the lowest, have been taught to impose in His Majesty's Government."

(iv) Mr. Ormsby Gore, Under-Secretary of State for the Colonies, 1928.

Mr. Ormsby Gore said - see Cmd. 3225, 1928 -

"They were, they are, and they must remain 'Malay' States ..."

===== Page 258 =====
The States were, when our co-operation in government was invited, Mohammedan monarchies and such they are today. We have neither the right nor the desire to vary this system of government.

(v) Sir Samuel Wilson, Under-Secretary of State for the Colonies, 1932.

In his report on his visit to Malaya Sir Samuel Wilson said:

"It seems clear that the maintenance of the position, authority and prestige of the Malay Rulers must always be a cardinal point in British policy... His Majesty's Government have no intention of requiring the Ruler of any Unfederated Malay State to enter against his will into any kind of Malayan League or Union."

(vi) Sir P. Cunliffe-Lister (now Lord Swinton), Secretary of State for the Colonies, 1933.

On the 14th July, 1933, Sir P. Cunliffe-Lister said in the House of Commons - Hansard Vol. 281 page 1429:

"There is no question at all of altering in any degree, even by a comma, the Treaties which bind us, either in the Federated or in the Unfederated Malay States and which are charters of the agreements with the Rulers both of the Federated and Unfederated Malay States."

4. The authoritative pronouncements and assurances mentioned in paragraph 3 are bound to be referred to by the Malay Rulers when we come to negotiate the new treaties, and His Majesty's Government must be prepared and armed with cogent reasons why the proposed new constitutional setup for a unified Malay Peninsula is necessary. One of the reasons I have heard mentioned is that the existence of nine separate and distinct States and three Settlements in a country no larger than England makes it difficult to prepare plans for the defence of such country. But this reason will not carry us very far because I believe that any proposals put up for the defence of Malaya have always been agreed to by all the Malay Rulers, and whilst such Rulers cannot help but concede that there has been a certain amount of delay in obtaining their consent to such proposals, I imagine that His Majesty's Government cannot plead that if there had been only one government of Malaya

===== Page 259 =====
The handling of the defence of Malaya would have been no better.

In my view, we must approach and argue this matter on a much higher plane, so that the proposed new constitutional setup for the Malay Peninsula will survive scrutiny and criticism in the calm atmosphere of the post-war peace, rather than giving it the appearance of a plan hastily devised during the present war.

In my view, the main reasons why a Union comprising the Malay Peninsula, i.e., the Federated Malay States, the Unfederated Malay States, and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, is necessary are:

(a) The development of Malaya is due to members of immigrant races, and its further development lies largely in their hands.

(b) The present-day position of China as one of the four great Allied Nations.

(c) The need for creating Malayan nationalism.

(d) The present constitutional setup, with its several units, impairs efficiency and is not conducive to the political progress and economic development of the country.

As regards (a), a study of the Annual Reports of the Federated Malay States, and even some of the Unfederated Malay States, demonstrates how the development of the resources of the country has been carried out by non-Malays.

The Malay is essentially an agriculturalist; hence, in tin mining, where there is not the possibility that rubber cultivation offers of small holdings run by a single individual or a single family, the Malays, for all practical purposes, have no share whatsoever. Tin mines are owned almost exclusively by Europeans or Chinese, and the latter race supplies the bulk of the labour force to work them. Similarly

===== Page 260 =====
In businesses, these are in the hands of Europeans, Chinese, and Indians, and only occasionally are Malays found who are sufficiently capable for employment in commercial concerns.

Turning to rubber, one would expect to find the Malays playing an important role in the production of this agricultural product. Admitting that Malays generally have not had the capital necessary to open up large rubber estates, there has been no obstacle placed in their way of becoming labourers on rubber estates. Yet the reluctance of the Malays to enter this labour market is shown in the 1931 Census which found, in the Federated Malay States, only 7,373 Malays on rubber estates as compared with 37,863 Chinese and 203,036 Indians. Even for the Malays, these figures are on the high side because many of them included in the estate population are permitted to live on the estate and are not employed there.

Of the 3,071,319 acres of rubber which had been planted in Malaya up to the end of 1931, 1,855,797 acres were made up of estates of 100 acres and over, and 1,215,522 acres of estates under 100 acres. Malay ownership of the former is insignificant and probably not much more than 50% of the latter.

Hence it is true to say that as regards the two main industries of tin and rubber, which gave rise to the importance of Malaya in the economic world and laid the foundation of the country's wealth, though the Malays benefited to a considerable extent from the many social services financed from such wealth, they played little part in their development or progress. The importance of tin and rubber to Malaya cannot be overemphasized. Together they make up more than 90% of the exports and furnish a high percentage of the national income. Had it not been for the Europeans, Chinese, and Indians, these two important industries would not have been properly developed and, in the march of progress, it is

===== Page 261 =====
Impossible to expect in British protected countries, such as Malaya, that the people who make their homes there and develop the country's resources are continually to be denied any real political status in the country.

I do not subscribe to the view that the Sultans are petty autocratic Malay Rulers—they might have been but for their British Advisers—but, in a country such as Malaya with its heterogeneous population and its large commercial interests, the Sultans are really anachronisms. Without British assistance the Sultans would never have attained their present position; without such assistance, they would have remained local chiefs. The continuance of the British policy of "Malaya for the Malays" is gradually earning for the Malays the jealousy of the other Eastern races in Malaya, because members of those other races dislike seeing persons who are their inferiors in intellect and capabilities continually being favoured at their expense. If this pro-Malay policy is allowed to continue it is bound to lead to political unrest and may even end with the Malays regarding the British as their worst enemies. Some Malays already believe that many changes which have been brought about have been for the benefit of foreigners, and that the pro-Malay policy of the British is merely to resist the political demands of the immigrant races rather than the protection of Malay interests.

With regard to (b), i.e. the present-day position of China, there can be no doubt that in the post-war world China will wish to have a large say in Far Eastern affairs, and in this respect she will have the support of other civilized nations, particularly the United States of America where non-American interests are concerned.

In view of the high proportion of Chinese in the population of Malaya it is natural that the Chinese Government will do all in its power to assist its own nationals in obtaining a bigger

===== Page 262 =====
say in the Government of Malaya, and it would be wise for His Majesty's Government to anticipate such a demand by making an early public pronouncement of its intentions as to the proposed Union of the States and Settlements making up the Malay Peninsula.

In the Appendix to this Memorandum I set out in tabular form the racial position of the population of Malaya in 1931 (the latest actual census figures) in so far as it relates to the Malays, Chinese and Indians. From the figures in that Appendix the following two salient points emerge:

(a) The Malays throughout the whole of Malaya only outnumber the Chinese by 252,629.

(b) In addition to the preponderance of Chinese over Malays in Singapore and Penang, the Chinese outnumber the Malays in three of the Federated Malay States and almost equal them in numbers in the Unfederated Malay State of Johore.

If this increase in the population continues at the same rate in the future as it has done in the past, the Chinese will soon, if they have not already done so (see estimated population as at 30th June, 1941, set out in paragraph 7(B) below), outnumber the Malays. The following table gives the percentage increases by race during two decades.

1911 - 1921. 1921 - 1931.

Malays ... 15% 17%
Chinese ... 28% 46%

The problem of the political status of the Chinese is one which has been raised frequently in the past, fortunately not as forcibly as it might have been, and on our return to Malaya, with China as a powerful member of the victorious Allied Nations, there can be no doubt that forceful representations will be made to His Majesty's Government and to the local government in Malaya for the Chinese to have a larger say in

===== Page 263 =====
That government. It is sound policy to forestall this representation by making public at the appropriate time His Majesty's Government's intention to create a Malayan Union of the Federated Malay States, the Unfederated Malay States, and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

In fact, I think it might be said that should greater political liberty be denied to the immigrant races in Malaya, and the policy of reconstruction built on the unstable foundations of Malay monarchy, His Majesty's Government would find it difficult to maintain her position in Malaya for any lengthy period.

I have seen it stated that Indonesia has been promised powers and status equal to those of Holland itself when the Axis war is over. If this is so, it cannot be expected that the peoples of Malaya, other than the Malays, will be content to remain in the slough of narrow parochialism whilst the inhabitants of Sumatra, for instance, just across the Straits of Malacca, are granted a greater say in planning their own destinies on a national basis.

I regard (c), i.e., the need for creating a Malayan nationalism, as probably the most cogent reason for establishing a Malayan Union.

The declared policy of His Majesty's Government with regard to our Colonies and Protectorates has always been, and still is, one of trusteeship with the ultimate granting of self-government. This has been wholly achieved as regards the Dominions, which were once Colonies, and some of our present Colonies are well on the road to that ultimate goal.

One of the great difficulties in achieving such a goal is that in most Colonies societies are plural, i.e., they consist of groups of people speaking different languages, having different outlooks, following different occupations, and with different customs. That being so, such plural

===== Page 264 =====
Societies can have no common will or political outlook. The members of such societies cannot be expected to view problems from a nationalistic outlook but only from the point of view of the group to which they belong. This is particularly so in Malaya where the Malayan Chinese and Indians look towards China and India respectively. So long as the present constitutional set up of the Malay States continues, so long will be the deferment of any national spirit. Without such national spirit Malaya will find it difficult to play its proper part in the post-war world. Hence what is required is the creation and fostering of a national outlook by means of which the peoples of Malaya can turn their eyes to the country as a whole, instead of towards China, India or to the particular Malay State to which they belong, thus lifting problems on to the higher plane of national statesmanship and pooling the resources of the various States and Settlements for the benefit of a united Malaya.

Under the present constitutional set up any such change of outlook is not feasible. The only solution is through the medium of some kind of union. Such union should not only raise the outlook of the people to a national one but should also achieve the political integration of the plural society in Malaya. Without union such political integration is impossible, without political integration there can be no national outlook and, in my view not much hope in the future of granting self-government to Malaya.

Finally, as regards (d), i.e. the loss of efficiency and the difficulties due to Malaya being split up into several administrative units. In order to appreciate the significance of this it is necessary to bear in mind that in the Federated and Unfederated Malay States, which make up a country no larger than England, there are ten different legislatures. Six of these legislatures, the Federal Council and the State Councils of the five Unfederated Malay States, are separate and

===== Page 265 =====
Independent. The system is in no way analogous to that of English County Councils, because each such legislature possesses, for all practical purposes, sovereign powers. For example, Negri Sembilan (this is a federation of nine miniature states) is smaller than Devonshire, Selangor is only slightly larger, and even Perak or Johore only a little more extensive than Yorkshire. With all these Governments enacting their own legislation, it has been difficult in the past to maintain uniformity in laws, an essentiality for a country which, in fact, is one economic unit. This has been more forcibly impressed on my mind in planning the legal side of the Military Administration for the re-occupation of Malaya.

In such a small area, where most of the problems are common to each of the various Governments, it is inconceivable that in order to obtain a uniformity of policy on essential matters, two Governments and nine Malay Sultans must be consulted. Such a policy must result in compromises at times, because the Sultans have always had the right of direct appeal to the Secretary of State for the Colonies, and naturally a Governor tries to avoid this.

From whatever angle the constitutional problem of Malaya is viewed, common sense dictates the answer that the only solution is a Malayan Union, which has as its object the welfare of the local Malays and the immigrant races who have made Malaya their home, and which, at the same time, will safeguard the historical individuality of each Malay State and the personal prestige of its Ruler.

I make no apology for setting out at some length the introductory material in this and the previous paragraphs, because it is essential to get a clear background of the present constitutional set up and the reasons why it is desirable, even necessary, to create a Union.

===== Page 266 =====
5. In the War Cabinet Secret Paper OMB(44)3 of 14th January, 1944, it is laid down that:

(a) There should be a Union of all the Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

(b) It is desirable that the Island of Singapore should not be included in that Malayan Union.

The particular paragraphs of the Secret Paper setting out in general terms the proposed constitutional scheme are reproduced here in extenso, because I have endeavoured to keep the constitution, which I propose later in this Memorandum, within the general framework of the scheme set out in those paragraphs. The relevant paragraphs are:

"10. Within Malaya conditions of dynastic pride and local particularism militate against the emergence within any foreseeable future of a union of the existing Malay States under a native Ruler; nor, for reasons explained above, would the emergence of a unified Malay monarchy for the whole province be acceptable to other non-Malay communities with substantial interests in the country. The British Crown alone provides the common link and loyalty which will draw the separate communities together and promote a sense of common interest and the development of common institutions. It is therefore necessary that, as a first step, the old situation in which His Majesty has no jurisdiction in the Malay States should be remedied. The legal view is that our present Treaties with the Malay Rulers are at present to be regarded as still operative (though for practical purposes in suspension owing to enemy occupation of the territory). A fresh treaty, therefore, with each Ruler should be concluded as soon as possible after re-occupation under which such jurisdiction would be ceded to His Majesty as would enable him to legislate for the States under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act, notwithstanding, in the case of Kedah and Perlis, the Clauses mentioned in paragraph 5 above. Thereafter it will be possible to proceed to create a new constitution for Malaya by Order-in-Council under statutory powers. This will involve fresh legislation in the United Kingdom Parliament in respect of the Straits Settlements.

11. Our constitutional scheme should be designed, first and foremost, to provide for a Union of all the Malay States and the settlements of Penang and Malacca. A central authority representing these States and Settlements should be created, and at its head there should be a Governor with an Executive and Legislative Council. The seat of Government of this Malayan Union would be conveniently at or near Kuala Lumpur, the capital of the present limited Federation. Subject to the new central authority, the several States and Settlements would be empowered to deal with such local affairs as may be devolved upon them by the central authority. These local authorities would be so constituted as to be representative of the principal communities and interests in the State or Settlement concerned."

===== Page 267 =====
12. It is desirable that the Island of Singapore should not be included in the present conception of the Malayan Union. Its inclusion might adversely affect the Malay attitude towards the proposals for the Union of the States, which is the first and foremost object of our policy and the necessary basis for any more extensive Union. The basis for a separate organisation for the Island of Singapore already existed in the Municipality of Singapore, but the appointment of a separate Governor or Lieutenant-Governor for the Settlement will be desirable. There will, of course, be many matters of administration e.g. Posts and Telegraphs, on which the closest collaboration will be required between the authorities at Singapore and the rest of the Peninsula, but these can be the subject of particular agreements. Nevertheless, it should be made clear that His Majesty's Government has no desire to preclude or prejudice in any way the fusion of the two Administrations in a wider Union at any time should they both agree that such a course were desirable.

14. It is recognised that this programme will require the sacrifice by the State Rulers of some part of the authority and jurisdiction hitherto exercised by them. The interests of Malaya as a whole clearly require such a sacrifice. But there is no intention that the Rulers should lose their personal position as the natural leaders of their Malay people within their State territory. Indeed, the association of their territories in the Union will give the Rulers an opportunity to play their part in a wider sphere of affairs, for it is intended to devise means to associate the Rulers personally with the machinery of the central authority, possibly as an advisory body to the Governor in respect of Malay and Mohammedan affairs. In any event, our past obligations will require that the elaboration of the new constitutional arrangements should have regard to the political, economic and social interests of the Malay race.

In addition to the scheme set out in the above mentioned paragraphs, the Secretary of State for the Colonies, in a secret and personal letter to the Supreme Allied Commander, South East Asia Command, dated the 13th June, 1944, expressed the view (inter alia) that the Sultans should derive from a central Malayan authority any temporal authority which they are to exercise in the post war arrangement, and that they should not be given any grounds for believing that they will have derived authority in the constitution - apart from their personal prestige and dignity - either by reason of delegation from any external British authority or by reason of their own royal status.

I refer to this later because in the constitution suggested by me, the Sultans are granted what I regard as

===== Page 268 =====
Their fit and proper position with certain defined functions to be performed by them.

It appears clear from paragraph 11 of the Secret Paper quoted above, that in the proposed scheme the several States and Settlements, as they now stand, are to be retained together with their present boundaries. That is my interpretation of the words:

"Subject to the new central authority, the several States and Settlements would be empowered to deal with such local affairs as may be devolved upon them by the central authority. These local authorities would be so constituted as to be representative of the principal communities and interests in the State or Settlement concerned."

The retention of the States and Settlements, as they now stand, would be in keeping with British ideals and policy, namely to build on whatever fabric of government is found in territory ceded to, or coming under the protection of, Great Britain, by the moulding of a new constitution upon the fabric of government already existing, rather than by the total destruction of whatever scheme of government is found, with a consequential complete rebuilding.

Malay Rulers.

6. The basis for the creation of a Union and the establishment of its constitution is the negotiation of separate treaties with the Sultans of the four Federated and five Unfederated Malay States, whereby each Sultan will cede to His Majesty such jurisdiction as will enable His Majesty to legislate under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act for all the nine Malay States, thereby setting up, under an Order-in-Council, a central legislature with powers to legislate for all these nine States. All those States will be incorporated into a Malayan Union, together with the British Settlements of Penang and Malacca, which at present form part of the Straits Settlements.

Subject to the usual power of disallowance by His Majesty, there will be no restriction on the legislative powers of this central legislature. Legislation required to have the force

===== Page 269 =====
Of law throughout the whole of the Malayan Union will be enacted by this new central legislature instead of, as formerly, by the Federal Council in respect of the four Federated Malay States, and by the State Council for each Unfederated Malay State, and by the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements as regards the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

The negotiation of these new treaties (one treaty with each separate Sultan) creates difficulties, because in fact His Majesty's Government is requesting each Sultan to sign away many of his powers. This immediately brings to the forefront what position the Malay Sultans will have in the new constitution, and the whole success or failure of the negotiations for the execution of these new treaties hangs on this matter.

As a statement of fact it is true to say that under the present constitution the position in the Federated Malay States is that the Residents rule and the Sultans advise, whereas in each Unfederated Malay State the Sultan rules and the British Adviser advises.

In law, the Sultans in the Federated Malay States, if they wished, could prevent a law passed by the Federal Council coming into force by refusing to assent to it, and in each of the Unfederated Malay States, the Sultan's assent is essential before any law passed by the State Council can have the force of law.

Clearly, in the Union, the central legislature must be supreme and the laws passed by it cannot depend for their force of law upon the assent of each one of the nine Sultans. Accordingly, in the new constitution, the Sultans as sovereign Rulers can have no place in the central legislature and the laws passed by that legislature must be assented to solely by the Governor.

===== Page 270 =====
The possibility had occurred to me of the new Central Legislature being bicameral in form, with the composition of the Second Chamber made up of the nine Sultans and the Governor. The difficulties of such a scheme are many. The main ones are to obtain suitable representation for the Settlements of Penang and Malacca; to devise ways and means of settling differences between the Lower and Upper Chambers; without the inclusion in the Second Chamber of the principal officers of Government, e.g., Chief Secretary, Attorney-General, and Financial Secretary, the business of the Second Chamber relating to legislation, finance, and matters of a technical nature would be very difficult of transaction.

Apart from these difficulties, a Second Chamber, composed of the Malay Rulers, would not be truly representative of the peoples of the Malay Peninsula, because the Sultans would be bound to look at problems mainly from the point of view of the Malays and also from the parochial view of their own States.

Further, the main functions of a Second Chamber are (a) to prevent the discharge of precipitate and ill-considered legislation by a single House, (b) to look on problems from a non-party aspect, and (c) to discuss at leisure matters of general and non-political importance.

In short, as Bagehot has stated, a Second Chamber "Besides independence to revise judicially and power to revise effectively, has leisure to revise intellectually." The Sultans, either individually or collectively, do not possess the necessary qualifications to perform effectively the real functions of a Second Chamber and on this account, and on account of the difficulties I have just mentioned, I am satisfied that any such Second Chamber is impracticable. In any case, it would be impolitic to attempt to find it a place within the scheme of a unified constitution for the Malay Peninsula.

===== Page 271 =====
But some place must be found for the Sultans in that constitution, because I consider they will not be satisfied with being informed, to quote from paragraph 14 of the Secret Paper, that their position is merely that of "natural leaders of their Malay people within their State territories."  

A provision common to all the treaties between His Majesty's Government and the Sultans is that on matters of Mohammedan Religion and Malay Custom the Sultan is not bound to ask and accept the advice of the Resident or Adviser. These are matters which only affect the Malay community of the Malay States and, as regards the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, which are British territory, such provisions are not relevant. It is right and proper that on those matters the Governor in the Union should have the advice of the Sultans as a body. Accordingly the new constitution should provide for the establishment of a Council composed of all the Sultans (the term "Malay Rulers" must be abandoned) which should be a deliberative, consultative and advisory, but not an executive, body, with the Governor, or in his absence the Officer Administering the Government, as its President. On account of the fact that the Yam Tuan of Negri Sembilan and the Raja of Perlis are not Sultans this Council should have a Malay title as it would not be politic to give it the title in English "Council of Sultans." The main functions of this Council should be -  

(a) To advise the Governor on all matters affecting Mohammedan religion and Malay custom, before any such matter is discussed or dealt with by the Union Legislature, and the Governor should be bound to have such matters discussed in the Council of Sultans in the first instance.  

(b) To advise the Governor on any other matter which the Governor decides ought to be discussed in the Council of Sultans.

===== Page 272 =====
(c) To discuss any matter proposed for discussion by any Sultan, provided the Governor approves its discussion.

To restrict the functions of this Council to those mentioned in (a) alone would not satisfy the Sultans, and I see no harm in including (b) and (c).

As the relationship between His Majesty's Government and each Sultan is bound by treaty, the following matters should be debarred from discussion in the Council:

(a) Treaties, rights, dignities and privileges of each Sultan and members of his family, and the actions of each individual Sultan.

(b) The internal affairs of any individual State or Settlement which are properly within the purview of a State or Settlement Council.

It should be stated clearly that the establishment of this Council shall not prejudice in any way the treaty relations (by this I mean the new treaty arrangements) between His Majesty's Government and each Sultan, nor should the right of direct correspondence and access between any Sultan and the Governor be in any way impaired.

Such a Council should meet at regular intervals and should be opened with ceremony in keeping with the prestige of the Sultans.

Complete records of all Council Meetings should be kept in the English and Malay languages.

Council Meetings should not be public, but communiques to the press should be issued after each Meeting.

If such a Council is established with due ceremony and with the intention that the Governor should look upon it as a permanent consultative and advisory body, this ought to go a long way towards appeasing the Sultans, especially those of the Unfederated Malay States, for the loss of many of their present powers. With regard to the loss of those powers, it

===== Page 273 =====
It is appropriate here to suggest that immediately on the re-occupation of Malaya, the policy of the Military Government should be to stress the fact that the immediate Government of Malaya is a British Military Administration and give the appearance that if the Sultans act in a reasonable manner, His Majesty's Government will restore to them such of their former rights, dignities and privileges as are consonant with the proposed new constitution. This policy should tend to give the impression that it is the Sultans, rather than His Majesty's Government, who are seeking concessions.
