If the new constitution is accepted by the Sultans, the Council of Sultans will form a valuable consultative and advisory body to which the Governor of the Central Government can look for advice, particularly on matters touching Mohammedan Religion and Malay Custom, because such advice will be collective rather than individual. In fact, each Sultan, as a member of this Council, will play his part in a wider sphere than hitherto. It should be pointed out to the Sultans that in the post-war world, the policy, not only of Great Britain but also of other nations, will be to deal with affairs on a regional basis rather than by individual States.

The rules of business for the Council should be framed by the Governor after consultation with the Sultans, and no meeting of the Council should be held unless the Governor or the Officer Administering the Government can attend, as it would be impolitic to hold any meeting with the Senior Sultan presiding.

I now revert to the Secretary of State's letter to which reference has been made in paragraph 5 above immediately following the citation of the relevant paragraphs from the Secret Paper CMB(44)3.

As I read the relevant passage quoted from that letter, the new constitution set up by the King-in-Council under the

===== Page 274 =====
Powers contained under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act will contain no reference to the functions of the Sultans. I would point out that it is the nature of the new constitution which will interest the Sultans more than the new treaties, because the latter merely provide the machinery by means of which the new constitution can be made.

If the Sultans find that they have been allotted no place in the new constitution, then, in my view, they will not sign the new treaties. If they are told that an appropriate place will be allotted to them by the "central Malayan authority" when it has been established (there is no precise description of the central authority contemplated and it may mean the Legislative Council of the Union Government) equally they will not be satisfied. In fact, if the "central Malayan authority" contemplated is the Legislative Council it would mean that the Malay, Chinese and Indian unofficial members of such Council would be able to air their views in public as to what position the Sultans should occupy. Further, any Malayan authority which gives powers to the Sultans could amend or revoke such powers, which would place the Sultans in the hands of that authority.

I cannot, myself, see the necessity for such a policy. The new constitution will take away from the Sultans most of their powers, so surely it should set out the position they are to occupy, and also specify, in general terms, the functions they are desired to perform. I am certain the Sultans would wish to be placed in their appropriate position in the new constitution by His Majesty the King-in-Council, after negotiations have taken place with each Sultan, rather than by any local Malayan authority. Also, any future change in their status and functions would then depend on the King-in-Council, after further discussions with the Sultans themselves, rather than their prestige and dignity being opened to discussion in local politics.

===== Page 275 =====
In an affair of this nature the end to be achieved is the same but it is the way of achieving it which is an all important matter.

7. Paragraph 11 of the Secret Paper states that there should be a Governor at the head of the Union Government with an Executive and Legislative Council. As this means that the Union Government should be conducted and constituted in the same way as the government of a Colony, it is necessary to consider the composition of both the Executive and Legislative Councils.

(A) Executive Council.

Since the Government will be run on the same lines as the government of a Colony I assume that, apart from the Governor, the three principal officers of government will be the Chief Secretary, the Attorney-General and the Financial Secretary. I have used the title "Attorney-General" because the term "Legal Adviser", used in the Federated and Unfederated Malay States, will no longer be appropriate.

Before setting out what I consider should be the composition of the Executive Council, the status of the British officers who are to be the heads of States and Settlements must be discussed. Under the present constitution the status of the British Resident is higher than that of any other executive officer of government in the Federated Malay States, and in each of the Unfederated Malay States the status of the British Adviser (General Adviser in Johore) is more or less equivalent to that of a British Resident in the Federated Malay States.

With the establishment of the Union Government there will no longer be the necessity to accord to the senior administrative officer in each State the same status as he held previously because his functions will be less important than those of many of the heads of departments of the Union Government and, in any case, he must be subordinate to the Chief Secretary. I suggest that the titles of the posts to be held

===== Page 276 =====
By the senior executive officers in the various States and Settlements, the title should be changed from "British Resident" in the Federated Malay States, "British Adviser" ("General Adviser" in Johore) in the Unfederated Malay States, and "Resident Councillor" in the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, to "Resident Commissioner" in each State and Settlement. The term "State Commissioner" would not be appropriate because in Johore certain Malay officials are called by that title. It will not be possible to have the eleven Resident Commissioners as members of Executive Council since their status will be less than that of the former British Residents, British Advisers, and Resident Councillors. In any case, their inclusion as members of Executive Council would not be appropriate in the unified constitution.

There should be five official members of Executive Council. Only the Chief Secretary, Attorney-General, and Financial Secretary should be ex-officio, leaving two other officials to be nominated by the Governor.

There should be an equal number of unofficial members of Executive Council, but it would be preferable not to prescribe such unofficial representatives on a racial basis. In the first instance, however, it is desirable that there should be one European, one Malay, one Chinese, and one Indian unofficial member on Executive Council, leaving the Governor free to nominate the most suitable unofficial to fill the remaining vacancy.

It is not necessary to lay down that the membership of Executive Council should be restricted to members of Legislative Council, though this is the ideal to be aimed at eventually.

The question arises whether membership of Executive Council should be restricted to persons who are Malayan Union Citizens, and having examined the provisions of the proposed Malayan Union Citizenship Directive, I do not think such restriction will create difficulties.

This will give an Executive Council consisting of the Governor and ten members.

===== Page 277 =====
(B) Legislative Council.

I assume that the unofficial members of the Legislative Council of the Union would be nominated because, in its present stage of development, the Malay Peninsula is unsuited to the electoral system.

Under the present constitution, the Federal Council of the Federated Malay States consists of:

High Commissioner - President.

Official Members:
- Resident of Perak.
- Resident of Selangor.
- Resident of Negri Sembilan.
- Resident of Pahang.
- Federal Secretary.
- Legal Adviser.
- Financial Secretary.
- Secretary for Chinese Affairs.
- Controller of Labour.
- General Manager, F.M.S. Railways.
- Adviser on Agriculture.
- Comptroller of Customs.
- Adviser on Medical Services.
- Adviser on Education.
- Additional member (Adviser on Public Works).

Unofficial Members:
- 5 Europeans.
- 4 Malays.
- 2 Chinese.
- 1 Indian.

Hence, in addition to the Governor, there are at present fifteen official and twelve unofficial members of the Federal Council.

Under the new constitution, unofficial representation on the Legislative Council should be on a population basis as far as possible. To this end, it is convenient to divide the Malay Peninsula into the following areas:

1. Kedah and Perlis.
2. Penang and Province Wellesley.
3. Perak.
4. Selangor and Pahang.
5. Negri Sembilan and Malacca.
6. Johore.
7. Kelantan and Trengganu.

===== Page 278 =====
The estimated population of these areas, by race, as on 30th June, 1941, is:

Area                      Malays   Europeans   Eurasians   Chinese    Indians    Others    Total

1. Kedah & Perlis          387,735     674        151      116,672    62,025     16,051   583,308
2. Penang & Province Wellesley 119,913  2,833     2,682     230,679    60,503     2,437    419,047
3. Perak                   335,385    4,113      1,590     450,197   196,056     5,350    992,691
4. Selangor & Pahang       281,236    5,606      2,831     413,632   210,730     9,317    923,352
5. Negri Sembilan & Malacca 217,912    2,029     3,346     217,931    87,552     3,326    532,096
6. Johore                  302,104    1,264       352      308,901    58,498     4,178    675,297
7. Kelantan & Trengganu    555,836      262        57       40,319     9,000     8,250    613,724

It is clear from the above figures that for the areas mentioned the nominated unofficials, in every case, will be Malays and Chinese, therefore some means must be evolved whereby adequate representation can be afforded to Europeans, Indians and Eurasians. This could be done by fixing the total number of unofficial members of Legislative Council at twenty-one, out of which fourteen members would be nominated to represent the above mentioned areas on a population basis. The remaining seven unofficial members would be nominated to represent important interests throughout the Union, thereby affording a suitable method of securing adequate representation for European, Indian and Eurasian interests.

There should be twenty-one official members of Legislative Council of whom five would be the official members of Executive Council.

(C) Temporary Advisory Council.

When Military Administration ceases and the new Civil Government is established in the Malay Peninsula it will be impossible for that Government to set up a Legislative Council immediately. To cover the interregnum between the end of the

===== Page 279 =====
Military Administration and the setting up of a Legislative Council. The new constitution should specifically provide for the establishment of an Advisory Council to the Governor which would temporarily take the place of a Legislative Council. This Advisory Council should consist of an unspecified number of official and unofficial members. During that temporary period, an Executive Council, consisting of five official and five unofficial members, would be in existence and function as it would when the Civil Government is established.

It is probable that the Chief Civil Affairs Officer will set up, during the period of Military Administration, an Advisory Council on which there will be unofficial representation, thus affording an opportunity for earmarking unofficials who would be suitable to serve on the Governor's Advisory Council.

Nomenclature.

(D) Nomenclature.

Suitable terms must be considered for what the Union Government and its Legislature are to be called. In order to stress that it is a Union, the following would appear to be appropriate terms:

(a) Union of Malaya.
(b) Government of the Union of Malaya.
(c) Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya.

The use of the term "Malaya" is not strictly accurate because Malaya includes Singapore, which is excluded from the Union, but this minor inaccurate geographical expression should be condoned in favour of stressing that the object is the unification of Malaya.

8. Reference has already been made to State Councils in the Federated Malay States being reconstituted in 1932. Perak and Negri Sembilan adopted the bi-cameral system, whereas in Selangor and Pahang there was only one chamber.

Putting aside the bi-cameral system in Perak and Negri Sembilan, the general composition of a State Council in the Federated Malay States was that it was presided over by the Sultan, with the British Resident, Malay Chiefs, State officials,

===== Page 280 =====
Unofficial Europeans; Malays, Chinese and Indians, as members.

Under the new constitution the Sultan must drop out and the President of a State or Settlement Council must be the Resident Commissioner. It is difficult to say now what the actual membership of a State or Settlement Council should be, but the members of those Councils should be appointed by the Governor after consulting the Resident Commissioner. As regards the nomination of unofficial members some consideration should be paid to the racial population of each State and Settlement, but I would not make this the only guide.

In view of the fact that the Sultans will have nothing to do with the State Councils it is advisable that an Advisory Council to the Sultan should be established in each State with appropriate functions and that such Council should be presided over by the Sultan. Its members should be appointed by the Sultan subject to the approval of the Governor.

The functions of that Advisory Council should be:

(a) To advise the Sultan on all matters affecting Mohammedan religion and Malay custom.

(b) To advise the Sultan on any other matter which the Resident Commissioner, with the approval of the Governor, refers to this Advisory Council.

As regards the functions of the State and Settlement Councils, paragraph 11 of the Secret Paper, quoted in paragraph 5 of this Memorandum, states:

"Subject to the new central authority, the several States and Settlements would be empowered to deal with such local affairs as may be devolved upon them by the central authority."

If by central authority is meant the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya, I beg to differ because in my view that would unnecessarily restrict the functions of a State or Settlement Council.

I suggest that each State and Settlement Council should be empowered to legislate.

===== Page 281 =====
(a) On all matters which, in the opinion of the Governor-in-Council, are of a merely local or private nature in the State or Settlement.

(For instance, the Malayan Collieries Enactment, enacted by the State Council of Selangor, deals solely with the Malayan Collieries Ltd. building a private railway inside the State of Selangor).

Before the Governor-in-Council decides whether a matter requiring legislation should be enacted by a State or Settlement Council, he must first be satisfied by a certificate signed by the Attorney-General that it is a fit and proper matter for legislation by such a Council.

Having so decided, the Clerk to Executive Council will sign a certificate to that effect.

(b) On all subjects in respect of which the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya by law delegates the power of making laws to a State or Settlement Council.

(Laws made by a State or Settlement Council under this provision would be in the nature of rules or regulations, etc., made under Ordinances passed by the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya. I use the word "Ordinance" because I consider that it is the appropriate term to use for laws passed by the Legislative Council. The present word "Enactment" is more applicable to the legislative acts of a local body, and accordingly I suggest that the laws passed by State or Settlement Councils should be termed "Enactments".)

As regards other business of the State and Settlement Councils, I suggest that they should be empowered to discuss questions of a purely local nature. Provision can be made in their constitutions restricting discussions on other business to matters of a local nature. Such restrictions can be achieved by a provision.

===== Page 282 =====
Analogous to the usual clause contained in Instructions under the Sign Manual and Signet issued to a Governor, in which it is laid down that the Governor alone is entitled to submit questions to Executive Council. If Resident Commissioners are given similar powers, there should be no danger of discussions in State and Settlement Councils extending to subjects which extend beyond local matters.

9. A re-organisation of the Courts will be necessary consequent on the formation of a Union. Before discussing what form this re-organisation should take, it is desirable to set out briefly the present constitution of the Supreme Courts throughout Malaya.

The Supreme Court of the Colony is constituted by the Courts Ordinance (Cap. 10) and exercises within the Colony the same jurisdiction and authority as are held by His Majesty's High Court of Justice in England. Its Judges are appointed by His Majesty by Letters Patent under the Public Seal of the Colony. Hence those Judges are His Majesty's Judges functioning in the King's Courts.

The Supreme Court of the Federated Malay States is constituted by the Courts Enactment (Cap. 2) and comprises the Court of Appeal and the Courts of a Judge. The Court of a Judge has full criminal and civil jurisdiction. The Chief Justice and the other Judges are appointed jointly by the Rulers of the Federated Malay States and hence they are the Sultans' Judges and not Judges of His Majesty.

Under the Straits Settlements Courts Ordinance, the Judges of the Federated Malay States are declared ex officio Judges of the Supreme Court of the Colony and a similar law makes Judges of the Colony ex officio Judges of the Supreme Court of the Federated Malay States.

As regards appeals from the Supreme Court, in civil and criminal matters the Colony and the Federated Malay States each has its own Court of Appeal consisting of three or more Judges.

===== Page 283 =====
In practice, it is generally made up of the two Chief Justices and a puisne Judge.

I do not propose to go into the constitution of all the Courts of the Unfederated Malay States. I merely set out what is the position in the State of Johore under its Courts Enactment (Cap. 54). There the Supreme Court is made up of the Court of Appeal and the Court of a Judge. The Judge stationed in Johore is appointed by the Sultan and a provision of the Johore Courts Enactment makes the two Chief Justices and all the Judges of both the Colony and the Federated Malay States ex officio Judges of the State of Johore. As regards the Johore Court of Appeal, it is composed of two or more Judges and, in practice, the Chief Justice and one other Judge of the Colony sit.

In 1932 correspondence took place between the Governor and the Colonial Office regarding the establishment of a Supreme Court of Malaya, consisting of a Malayan Court of Appeal and a High Court of Justice for the Colony and for each of the Federated Malay States, and such other Malay States as desired to join. It was suggested that the Judges should consist of a Chief Justice (to be styled "Chief Justice of Malaya"), Appeal Judges and puisne Judges. At the time this proposal was made one of the main objects was to create a stronger Court of Appeal since at that time the Chief Justice of the Colony was not ex officio a Judge of the Federated Malay States, with the result that in the Federated Malay States the Court of Appeal was either composed of the Chief Justice of the Federated Malay States and two puisne Judges or, if the appeal was against a decision of the Chief Justice himself, it was heard before three puisne Judges. This state of affairs was quickly rectified by altering the law of the Federated Malay States in order to make the Chief Justice of the Colony an ex officio Judge of the Supreme Court of the Federated Malay States.

===== Page 284 =====
There was much opposition to the suggested judicial re-organisation. It was opposed by the then Acting Chief Justice, Federated Malay States, and also by the Federated Malay States Bar Committee. I was in Malaya at the time and know that most of the opposition was due to the feeling that if this judicial re-organisation came about with a single Chief Justice stationed in Singapore, the people of Singapore would have the ear of the Chief Justice and the members of the Bar of the Federated Malay States would come a very bad second.

In the correspondence between the Governor and the Colonial Office much was made of the fact that in both East and West Africa, Courts of Appeal have been set up for the purpose of hearing appeals from politically and legally distinct High Courts or Supreme Courts. This is true, on paper, but it is not true, in East Africa for instance, to say that the composition of the Court of Appeal is any stronger than it was in Malaya after the law was amended making the Chief Justice of the Colony an ex officio Judge of the Supreme Court of the Federated Malay States. After that amendment of the law the Court of Appeal was invariably composed of the two Chief Justices and one puisne Judge. From my experience in East Africa the Court of Appeal there is very often composed of two Chief Justices and a puisne Judge.

In view of the fact that the Island of Singapore and the rest of Malaya are to be two separate entities, with separate Governments, I see no useful purpose in having a Supreme Court for the whole of Malaya.

I realise that some of the executive departments in the new constitution will be pan-Malayan, but the reason for this is to keep policy uniform throughout Malaya on vital matters affecting the whole Union. The Court and its Judges are not concerned with any question of policy.

===== Page 285 =====
Accordingly, I suggest that there should be a separate High Court of the Union of Malaya with a Chief Justice at its head. The term "High Court" is used because it appears to be the custom to reserve the term "Supreme Court" for Crown Colonies. The Chief Justice will have sufficient work to do in reorganising the judicial system of all the Malay States and the two Settlements of Penang and Malacca, without having any responsibility for the Island of Singapore. In the first instance, a lot of his time will be taken up in travelling in order to get acquainted with local conditions, particularly in the Unfederated Malay States.

In addition to the Chief Justice, there must be a number of Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya stationed at various centres throughout the country. I need not go into the question of how many puisne Judges will be required or where they should be stationed.

In accordance with previous practice, the Chief Justice and the puisne Judges of the Supreme Court in Singapore should be declared ex officio Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya, and vice versa as regards the Chief Justice and puisne Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya. In practice, the Court of Appeal, whether sitting in the Union of Malaya or in Singapore, will invariably be composed of the two Chief Justices and one puisne Judge. I see no reason for creating a separate Court of Appeal which would mean the appointment of Appeal Judges with consequential extra expense.

This suggested constitution of the High Court of the Union of Malaya means that the judicial system of those Malay States which provide for Malay Judges and Appeal Courts composed of unqualified Malay Judges and a British Adviser (e.g., Perlis and Trengganu) must go. All these unqualified Judges must be replaced by qualified personnel and all appeals must go to the Court of Appeal of the Union of Malaya.

===== Page 286 =====
Death sentences in the Union of Malaya should be considered by the Governor-in-Council and not by the Malay Rulers.

I might say here, in order to give a fuller picture of this judicial system, I consider that the District Judge system in force in the Colony should be applied to the Union of Malaya, and that these appointments of District Judges should be made from the Colonial Legal Service. In fact, I think it is a matter for consideration whether District Courts should be given more extensive jurisdiction than they now possess in the Straits Settlements.

With regard to appeals from the Court of Appeal, at the present time appeals to the Privy Council are only provided for in the Colony, the Federated Malay States and in the State of Johore. In the Colony, there is an appeal to the King-in-Council as of right by virtue of the Charter and the constitution of the Straits Settlements as a Crown Colony. The Malay States, being merely protected States, are not subject to Orders-in-Council except at the request of the Rulers thereof.

In 1906, the four Sultans of the Federated Malay States requested the King-in-Council to entertain appeals from the Federated Malay States Court of Appeal in civil matters and in pursuance of such request the Federated Malay States Order-in-Council, 1906, was promulgated giving effect to the wishes of the Sultans. That Order-in-Council was revoked and replaced by the Federated Malay States Appeal Order-in-Council in 1912 consequent upon the enactment of what is now section 33 of the Courts Enactment (Cap. 2) which reads as follows:

"From any judgment or order of the Court of Appeal in any civil matter an appeal may be made to His Britannic Majesty in Council subject to such rules and regulations as may from time to time be prescribed by order of His said Majesty in Council."

The same procedure was followed in Johore in 1921.

The new constitution should provide for appeals from the Court of Appeal of the Union of Malaya to the Privy Council.

===== Page 287 =====
10. Having dealt with the constitutional setup regarding the Government of the Union of Malaya, its Executive and Legislative Councils, the State and Settlement Councils, and the Courts, it is now appropriate to discuss the question of the transfer to the Union Government of the property, etc., belonging to the various States and Settlements.

As the constitution of the Union of Malaya is to be that of a Union and not a Federation, and since the local legislatures in the States and Settlements will be completely subordinate to the central government, and also because the Sultans will no longer be sovereign Rulers but will collectively form an Advisory Council to the Governor, I feel that there is no room for any compromise in the transference of State and Settlement property to the Union Government and in the latter assuming full liability for State and Settlement debts. It appears to me that if the new constitution is to be a Union, let it be one in practice and not merely on paper. Accordingly, I have been largely guided by the provisions of the South Africa Act, 1909, in making the following suggestions. I am well aware that the State of Johore is wealthy compared with the other Unfederated Malay States, and that in creating a Union the State of Johore will contribute into the common pool assets greater in extent than those made by other Malay States. Although it will be difficult to persuade the Sultan of Johore to see reason on this, it cannot be avoided, because to have an effective Union there can be no exceptions.

It is not possible in a general Memorandum of this kind to provide for every particular matter in the transfer of State and Settlement property to the Union Government, and I merely set out below the main subjects to which consideration must be given.

(a) Revenues.

Under the present system in the Federated Malay States, certain revenues are appropriated to finance Federal Services and other revenues remain State. In the new constitution, we are not, as stated above, endeavouring

===== Page 288 =====
To create a greater federation, the aim is a Union and therefore there is no necessity for deciding what revenues shall be retained by the various States and Settlements and what shall go to the Union Government. All revenues, from whatever source arising, over which the States had powers of appropriation at the time of the establishment of the Union must vest in the Union Government. The financial experts may consider it desirable to appoint a commission after the establishment of the Union, to enquire into the financial arrangements which should exist between the Union Government and the various States and Settlements.

(b) Transfer of State and Settlement property.

All cash, bankers' balances, securities for moneys etc. belonging to each State and Settlement at the establishment of the Union must be transferred to the Union Government. If, however, the balance of any funds raised at the establishment of the Union has been raised by law for any special purpose in any State or Settlement, the Union Government must appropriate such balance for the special purpose for which it was raised.

(c) State land, mines and minerals.

By section 8 of the Federated Malay States Land Code (Cap. 138) and by similar provisions in the land laws of the Unfederated Malay States, the entire property in, and control of, State land is vested solely in the Ruler of the State.

As regards the alienation of State land, each British Resident in the Federated Malay States is empowered to alienate it on behalf of the Ruler of the State, and in the Unfederated Malay States although by law the Sultan in Council is the authority having the power to alienate State land, in fact the Sultan in Council has delegated such power to other bodies and [POSSIBLY MISSING].

===== Page 289 =====
persons. At one time, I thought it might be unnecessary to amend the law whereby State land is vested in the Ruler of the State, but having read and thought much about a unified constitution, I am of the opinion that all State land, public works, mines and minerals, and all property, whether moveable or immoveable, and all rights of whatever description belonging to the several States and Settlements must be transferred to the Union Government, which will take such property subject to any encumbrances thereon.

(d) Railways, Ports and Harbours.

With regard to the Railways, the Federated Malay States Government owns the entire railway system of Malaya, with the exception of the Johore State Railway which is leased from Johore. The latest lease with Johore was executed on 30th November, 1932, and provides that the Federated Malay States Government shall hold a lease for the Johore State Railway until the 31st December, 1953. Under that lease, the Federated Malay States Government pays to Johore a token sum of one dollar per year in advance and also a certain percentage of the net revenue on the working of the Federated Malay States Railway System. If there is a deficit on such a working, Johore gets nothing, but, on the other hand, Johore does not have to stand any proportionate share of any deficit.

Under the new constitution, the whole railway must be transferred to the Union Government which means that all the capital sunk in the Railway by the Federated Malay States Government and also by the Johore Government accrues to the benefit of the Union Government.

In recent years, it cannot be said that Johore has been making much profit from their share in the

===== Page 290 =====
Federated Malay States Railway System, because, after 1931, the running of the railway has generally resulted in a deficit, and by the end of 1934 the Railway Renewals Reserve Fund had been depleted to such an extent that it only stood at $7,478,164.

Hence, as regards the transfer of the railway to the Union Government, it means a great sacrifice by the Federated Malay States Government, with a correspondingly lesser one by the Johore Government, and a resultant gain to the remainder of the States and Settlements forming the Union.

With the exception of the port and harbour property at Penang, Prai and Butterworth which is vested in the Penang Harbour Board by section 30(1)(c) of the Straits Settlements Ports Ordinance (Cap. 149), all ports and harbours in the various States and Settlements must be transferred to the Government of the Union of Malaya.

(e) State and Settlement debts.

The Union of Malaya Government having assumed ownership of all the property formerly belonging to the various States and Settlements must, in equity, assume liability for all their debts, and the interest thereon, as they stand at the time of the establishment of the Union.

(f) Financial arrangements between the Straits Settlements and the Government of the Union of Malaya.

Since Penang and Malacca are part of the Straits Settlements and under the new constitution they will be transferred from the jurisdiction of the Government of the Straits Settlements to that of the Government of the Union of Malaya, agreement must be reached as to what is to be the division of the assets and liabilities of these two Settlements.

===== Page 291 =====
Between the new Government of the Island of Singapore and the Government of the Union of Malaya, presumably this matter will be decided by agreement between the two Governments.

11. At the establishment of the Union, all the laws of each particular State and Settlement will be in force, and until the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya begins to function, there will not be in existence any Union Legislation. Accordingly, the constitution itself must provide that all the laws in force in the several States and Settlements at the establishment of the Union shall continue in force in the respective States and Settlements until repealed or amended by the Legislature of the Union of Malaya.

If the judicial system as suggested in paragraph 9 of this Memorandum is adopted, provision must be made for the pending cases and appeals being taken over by the new Courts from the old Courts.

As regards legislation after the establishment of the Union, one difficulty occurs to me. All the Malay States have not reached an equal stage of development, e.g., Trengganu, Kelantan, and Perlis are behind Kedah and much behind the four Federated Malay States and Johore. In these backward States, there is no legislation on several highly technical subjects, e.g., bills of exchange, and it will not be desirable to legislate on such subjects in these backward States for some time to come.

12. I have carried out a certain amount of research as to whether the Courts should be given express power to interpret the constitution or whether the granting of such express power is unnecessary.

I do not think there can be any doubt that the Courts must have power to interpret the constitution should the occasion arise.

===== Page 292 =====
In South Africa and Canada, there is no law expressly conferring power on the Courts to interpret the constitution, and yet numerous decided cases show that there is no doubt that the Courts in those two Dominions have assumed such jurisdiction.

On the other hand, in Australia, section 76 of the Commonwealth of Australia Constitution Act provides (inter alia) that Parliament may make laws conferring jurisdiction on the High Court on any matter arising under the constitution or involving its interpretation.

By section 30 of the Commonwealth Judiciary Act, 1903-1934, the High Court is given original jurisdiction in all matters arising under the constitution and involving its interpretation. Thus, in Australia, the Federal High Court has been given express powers to interpret the constitution.

In the Colonies, the most useful case I can find on this matter is Jerusalem - Jaffa District Governor v Suleiman Murra (1926) A.C.321, in which it was held by the Privy Council that it was the right and duty of the Supreme Court of Palestine to consider whether an Ordinance was consistent with the terms of the Mandate.

I have not had time to go into this matter in detail but incline to the view that, for the avoidance of doubts, it is preferable to include express provision in the constitution empowering the Courts to interpret it, and express provision must be included to the effect that, in case of conflict, Ordinances of the Union of Malaya must prevail over Enactments of State and Settlement Councils.

13. Paragraph 15 of the Secret Paper states -

"The complete details of the application of this policy and in particular the detailed measures to be designed for the creation of a self-governing institution can only be worked out on the spot after re-occupation in the light of the prevailing conditions."

Knowing the mentality of the Sultans and the necessity for getting the new treaties executed as quickly as possible after the re-occupation of Malaya, I consider that the new

===== Page 293 =====
Constitution must be in complete draft form before we return to Malaya. The new treaties are merely the machinery by means of which a new constitution can be created, and it is the new constitution which will interest the Sultans. It would be fatal to let it appear that we had no cut and dried scheme for that constitution on our return. The less delay in negotiating the new treaties, the better chance we have of the Sultans agreeing to them. They will not agree unless we are in a position to tell them exactly what our plans are regarding the new constitution. Therefore, I urge that the new treaties and the new constitution should be in complete draft form before the re-occupation of Malaya.

Conclusion.

14. The constitution as outlined in this Memorandum was originally submitted to the Colonial Office on 15th November 1944, as representing the views of the writer only.

Since that date discussions have taken place between representatives of the Colonial Office and of C.A. (M.P.U.). As a result of those discussions, a few amendments of minor importance have been made in the Memorandum as originally drafted, and I think that the Memorandum as it now stands represents the considered views of those representatives of the Colonial Office and of C.A. (M.P.U.) who read the Memorandum in its original form and attended those discussions.

C.A. (M.P.U.),
38 Hyde Park Gate,
LONDON, S.W.7.

H.C. WILLAN

8th March 1945.

===== Page 295 =====
The War Cabinet has approved, for planning purposes, the policy of creating after the war a Malayan Union, as opposed to an extended Federation of Malay States. Accordingly, the memorandum postulates throughout that the central legislature to be established on our return shall constitute the supreme authority from which all other authorities in the Peninsula shall derive their powers. This postulate is inherent in the conception of a Union as opposed to that of a Federation (in which the constituent units retain their sovereignty, and merely devolve some of their powers upon a central body).

2. Subject to this basic conception the memorandum proposes (page 16) that the nine States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca shall be retained as geographical units with their present boundaries.

3. In considering the position of the Malay Rulers (pages 16-23), the memorandum considers and rejects the idea that there should be an Upper Chamber of Sultans. The idea is rejected partly because of the difficulty of devising satisfactory relations between the Lower and Upper Chambers, and partly because the Sultans do not possess the necessary qualifications to perform the functions of a Second Chamber in any real and effective manner. Nevertheless, the memorandum contends that some place must be found for the Sultans in the new Constitution, and proposes that the Sultans collectively should form an Advisory Council to the Governor. The subjects upon which this Council would deliberate would be principally questions of Mohammedan religion and Malay custom, (in relation to which the special position of the Sultans has always been recognised), but other subjects would also be open for discussion in the Council.

===== Page 296 =====
Council, either at the instance of the Governor or at the instance of any of the Sultans with the Governor's consent. The Council would meet regularly under the presidency of the Governor and would be regarded as a permanent body. Its meetings would be attended by all the ceremony necessary to maintain the prestige of the Sultans. It would not, however, impair the right of direct access between individual Sultans and the Governor. In concluding this section (Section 6), the memorandum argues that the new position of the Sultans should be defined in the Constitution itself. When the time comes to negotiate the new Treaties, this alone will satisfy the Sultans, but they will not be content to be told that an appropriate position will be allotted to them by the new central legislature.

4. The memorandum assumes (page 23) that, apart from the Governor, the three principal officers of Government will be the Chief Secretary, the Attorney-General, and the Financial Secretary. It goes on to argue (pages 23 and 24) that the position of the British Residents or Advisers in each of the States should be modified. Before the war, the status of these officers was higher than that of any other official save the High Commissioner, but with the establishment of the Union Government their importance will diminish and they must be made subordinate to the Chief Secretary. The memorandum proposes that these officers, as well as the officers at the head of the two Settlements, should be uniformly styled "Resident Commissioner."

5. The memorandum then proposes the creation of an Executive and Legislative Council, and discusses the constitution of each. The Executive Council (page 24) should have 5 Official and 5 Unofficial members, in addition to the Governor. Of the Official members, the Chief Secretary, the Attorney-General, and the Financial Secretary should be ex officio, while the other two would be nominated by the Governor. The Unofficial members would also be nominated by

===== Page 297 =====
By the Governor. A racial basis should not be prescribed for such nomination, but in the first instance, there should probably be one European, one Malay, one Chinese, and one Indian Unofficial member, the Governor being left free to nominate the most suitable Unofficial to fill the remaining vacancy. Membership of the Executive Council would be confined to Malayan Union citizens. The Legislative Council (pages 25 and 26) should have, besides the Governor, 21 Official and 21 Unofficial members. Fourteen of the latter should be nominated to represent areas on a population basis, and the remainder to represent important interests throughout the Union. Five of the Official members would be the Official members of the Executive Council and the remainder would be nominated by the Governor.

6. The memorandum then deals (pages 27 - 30) with State and Settlement Councils. In the past, the Sultans were Presidents of the State Councils. This practice must cease, and the President of the State or Settlement Council of the future must be the Resident Commissioner. Exact membership cannot be defined in advance, but members should be appointed by the Governor after consulting the Resident Commissioner. As regards the nomination of unofficial representatives, some consideration should be paid to the racial population of each State and Settlement but this should not be the only guide. Side by side with the State Councils there should be established in each State Advisory Councils to the Sultans, which should be presided over by the Sultan, and should advise him first on matters affecting Mohammedan religion and Malay custom and secondly on other matters referred to it by the Resident Commissioner with the approval of the Governor. The members, subject to the Governor's approval, would be appointed by the Sultan.

The State and Settlement Councils would be empowered to legislate (a) on all matters which, in the opinion

===== Page 298 =====
Opinion of the Governor in Council, are of a merely local or private nature in the State or Settlement and (b) on all subjects in respect of which power is delegated to them by law by the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya.

7. Next, the memorandum discusses the organisation of the Courts (pages 30-34). The idea of establishing a Supreme Court for the whole of Malaya, including the Island of Singapore as well as the Malayan Union, is rejected. It is suggested that there should be a separate High Court of the Union of Malaya with a Chief Justice at its head. In addition to the Chief Justice, there must be Judges of the High Court stationed at various centres throughout the Peninsula. In accordance with previous practice, the Chief Justice and the Puisne Judges of the Supreme Court in Singapore should be declared ex officio Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya and vice versa. In practice, the Court of Appeal, whether sitting in the Union of Malaya or Singapore, will invariably be composed of the two Chief Justices and one Puisne Judge. The memorandum sees no reason for creating a separate Court of Appeal, which would involve the appointment of Appeal Judges. The new Constitution should provide for appeals from the Court of Appeal of the Union of Malaya to the Privy Council.

8. The next section (pages 35-39) is devoted to the transfer of State and Settlement property etc. to the Union Government. In order that the future Union of Malaya may become a reality, the memorandum considers it necessary that all State and Settlement property shall be transferred to the Union Government which shall assume full liability for State and Settlement debts. The Union will take over all revenues from the States and the Settlements, all cash, bank balances, securities, etc., all State land, mines and minerals and all railways, ports and harbours (with the exception of the port and harbour property at Penang, Prai and Butterworth which is vested in the Penang Harbour Board). The memorandum recognises that this wholesale pooling

===== Page 299 =====
Wholesale pooling of resources may be objectionable to a rich State such as Johore, which stands to lose by the arrangement, but contends that nothing short of this will permit the future Union to become an effective reality.

9. Finally (pages 39 and 40) the memorandum states the view that there should be express provision in the Constitution empowering the Courts to interpret it, and laying down that in case of conflict, ordinances of the Union of Malaya must prevail over enactments of State and Settlement Councils.

===== Page 300 =====
1. In the War Cabinet Secret Paper CMB(44)3 dated 14th January 1944, the Secretary of State for the Colonies sets out, in general terms, the future constitutional policy for British Colonial Territories in South East Asia. It is clear that as regards Malaya the aim is:
   (a) A separate Government for Singapore Island.
   (b) The Union of the four Federated Malay States, the five Unfederated Malay States, and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

2. Before discussing a new constitutional policy for Malaya, it is as well to state briefly what is the present constitution. Malaya is divided into three parts:
   (a) The Straits Settlements, a Crown Colony, for which His Majesty is able to legislate under the Straits Settlements Act, 1866.
   (b) The four Malay States of Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan, and Pahang over which His Majesty has no powers under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. These States formed themselves into a Federation in 1896, and in 1909 a Federal Council was established which enacted legislation having the force of law throughout the Federation.
   (c) The five Unfederated Malay States of Johore, Kedah, Kelantan, Trengganu, and Perlis. Each of these States is independent and His Majesty has no powers under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act over any of them. Each such State has its own legislature, namely the Ruler in State Council.

All the nine Malay States are bound to Great Britain by treaties which date from 1874, in the case of Perak, to 1930, in the case of Perlis. The provisions of these treaties follow the same general lines, namely that each Malay Ruler

===== Page 301 =====
Shall accept a British officer whose advice shall be asked and acted upon in all questions other than those concerning Mohammedan religion and Malay custom. In addition, as regards Perlis, the 1930 treaty contains a provision by which His Majesty's Government have undertaken that they "will not merge or combine the State of Perlis with any other State without the written consent of the Raja of Perlis in Council", and, as regards Kedah, by the 1923 treaty, His Majesty's Government have undertaken that they "will not merge or combine the State of Kedah or her territories with any other State or with the Colony of the Straits Settlements without the written consent of His Highness the Sultan in Council".

Under the present constitutional set up legislation for the Settlements of Penang and Malacca is enacted by the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements, legislation for the Federated Malay States by the Federal Council, and legislation for each Unfederated Malay State by the Ruler in State Council of each such State.

In the Federated Malay States, in addition to the Federal Council, each of the four States has its own local legislative body, namely the Ruler in State Council. These State Councils existed prior to British intervention in Malaya. After that intervention and the establishment of the Federal Council, legislative enactments of the State Councils decreased in Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan and Pahang as the following figures show:

Enactments passed by State Councils.

1900 - 1909.   1910 - 1925.

Perak ........ 181     17
Selangor ..... 188     13
Negri Sembilan 189     13
Pahang ....... 175     20

In 1932, as a result of the policy of decentralization and constitutional re-organisation, the State Councils were reconstituted so as to be transmuted into representative and

===== Page 302 =====
Efficient bodies. Under that policy of decentralization, certain subjects were designated as State and others as Federal matters, and legislation on such subjects was enacted by the State or Federal Council in accordance with the appropriate designation. In any case in which doubt existed as to whether legislation on a particular matter should be enacted by the State or Federal Council, it was decided on the advice of the four Residents. It cannot be said that the Residents had any clear uniform views on whether a law should be State or Federal, nor did their decisions appear to follow any well-defined principles.

The effect of the reconstitution of the State Councils in each of the four Federated Malay States in 1932 was that more legislation was enacted by them, as is seen by the following figures:

Enactments passed by State Councils,
1935 - 1940

Perak .................. 32
Selangor ............... 36
Negri Sembilan ........ 27
Pahang ................. 28

But, if the Supply and Final Supply (i.e., State Budgets) Enactments (in the period mentioned they amount to an average of 11 Enactments in each State) and also certain Enactments which, though enacted separately in each State, contain provisions which are practically identical throughout the four States (i.e., they might just as well have been Federal Enactments) are not taken into account, very few laws remain which, on account of relating primarily to local matters, needed to be enacted by State Councils. In fact, when the State legislation for the period 1935 - 1940 is examined, it is true to say that only Enactments such as the Dindings Cession Enactment and the Sultan Idris Religious and Charitable Trust (Amendment) Enactment in Perak, the Malayan Collieries Railway Enactment in Selangor, and a few other Enactments were

===== Page 303 =====
Really necessary to be enacted as State Enactments.

Accordingly, even after the policy of decentralization had been implemented, it could not be said that in the Federated Malay States, so far as legislation was concerned, State Councils played a vital part in the constitutional setup. I have no doubt that the reason for the reconstitution of State Councils in 1932 was a political step in an attempt to loosen the ties of the Federation in the hope of achieving a greater Union later, and not because such Councils were of any real value.

3. Reverting to the Secretary of State's Memorandum contained in the Secret Paper CMB(44)3 of the 14th January, 1944, the first step in the formation of a Malayan Union is that a fresh treaty should be concluded with each Sultan as soon as possible after re-occupation, under which such jurisdiction would be ceded to His Majesty as would enable him to legislate for the Malay States under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. When those fresh treaties have been executed, a new constitution can be made by Order-in-Council under statutory powers. This must provide for some kind of constitutional union of the nine Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

In view of the authoritative statements which have been made regarding the independence of the Malay States, and the assurances given to the Malay Rulers that His Majesty's Government have no intention of impairing that independence, it is appropriate to quote here some of them because they are directly contrary to the present policy for the formation of a Malayan Union. The main assurances and the years in which they were made are:

(i) Sir John Anderson, High Commissioner for the Malay States, 1909.

"It was no small sacrifice of individuality on the part of the Rulers who entered on the compact of federation, and to complete and crown it by the Agreement in virtue of which we are assembled. They made that sacrifice willingly and gladly, because

===== Page 304 =====
They felt the debt which they owe to the protection and help which has been extended to them by England, and because they feel that the prosperity and progress of their States is bound up with the advancement of British interests in the Malay Peninsula. They have done this in the full confidence, based on the facts of the past, that, whatever is done by the British Government or by this Council, the faith and obligations of the treaties under which they agreed to accept British Advisers will always be scrupulously observed. They are confident that we will never forget that our powers are derived wholly from their gift, and that we are here in a Malay country as advisers and counselors of its Malay sovereigns. I am sure that every one of us will bear that in mind, and that the Rulers, who have so earnestly at heart the real good and advancement of the people subject to them, will never have cause to regret that they agreed to this step.

(ii) Sir Laurence Nunns Guillemard, High Commissioner for the Malay States, 1921.

In Federal Council Sir Laurence Guillemard said it was not the policy of Government "to exert, in any manner whatsoever, any pressure upon any other State to enter the Federation." He went on to say that if other States chose to apply for membership their applications would be carefully considered. He stressed that the general policy was one of friendly co-operation between the several Malayan units in all matters of common interest, on the understanding that each party was free to act as it thought best in local matters.

(iii) Sir Hugh Clifford, High Commissioner for the Malay States, 1927.

In Federal Council Sir Hugh Clifford said -

"The adoption of any kind of Government by majority would forthwith entail the complete submersion of the indigenous population who would find themselves hopelessly outnumbered by the folk of other races; and this would produce a situation which would amount to a betrayal of trust which the Malays of these States, from the highest to the lowest, have been taught to impose in His Majesty's Government."

(iv) Mr. Ormsby Gore, Under-Secretary of State for the Colonies, 1928.

Mr. Ormsby Gore said - see Cmd. 3225, 1928 -

"They were, they are, and they must remain 'Malay' States ..."

===== Page 305 =====
The States were, when our co-operation in government was invited, Mohammedan monarchies and such they are today. We have neither the right nor the desire to vary this system of government."

(v) Sir Samuel Wilson, Under-Secretary of State for the Colonies, 1932.

In his report on his visit to Malaya Sir Samuel Wilson said -

"It seems clear that the maintenance of the position, authority and prestige of the Malay Rulers must always be a cardinal point in British policy ... His Majesty's Government have no intention of requiring the Ruler of any Unfederated Malay State to enter against his will into any kind of Malayan League or Union."

(vi) Sir P. Cunliffe-Lister (now Lord Swinton) Secretary of State for the Colonies, 1933.

On the 14th July, 1933, Sir P. Cunliffe-Lister said in the House of Commons - Hansard Vol. 281 page 1429 -

"There is no question at all of altering in any degree, even by a comma, the Treaties which bind us, either in the Federated or in the Unfederated Malay States and which are charters of the agreements with the Rulers both of the Federated and Unfederated Malay States."

4. The authoritative pronouncements and assurances mentioned in paragraph 3 are bound to be referred to by the Malay Rulers when we come to negotiate the new treaties, and His Majesty's Government must be prepared and armed with cogent reasons why the proposed new constitutional set up for a unified Malay Peninsula is necessary. One of the reasons I have heard mentioned is that the existence of nine separate and distinct States and three Settlements in a country no larger than England makes it difficult to prepare plans for the defence of such country. But this reason will not carry us very far because I believe that any proposals put up for the defence of Malaya have always been agreed to by all the Malay Rulers, and whilst such Rulers cannot help but concede that there has been a certain amount of delay in obtaining their consent to such proposals, I imagine that His Majesty's Government cannot plead that if there had been only one government of Malaya.

===== Page 306 =====
The handling of the defence of Malaya would have been any better.

In my view, we must approach and argue this matter on a much higher plane than that, so that the proposed new constitutional setup for the Malay Peninsula will survive scrutiny and criticism in the calm atmosphere of the post-war peace, rather than giving it the appearance of a plan hastily devised during the present war.

In my view, the main reasons why a Union comprising the Malay Peninsula, i.e., the Federated Malay States, the Unfederated Malay States, and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca is necessary are:

(a) The development of Malaya is due to members of immigrant races, and its further development lies largely in their hands.

(b) The present-day position of China as one of the four great Allied Nations.

(c) The need for creating a Malayan nationalism.

(d) The present constitutional setup, with its several units, impairs efficiency and is not conducive to the political progress and economic development of the country.

As regards (a), a study of the Annual Reports of the Federated Malay States, and even some of the Unfederated Malay States, demonstrates how the development of the resources of the country has been carried out by non-Malays.

The Malay is essentially an agriculturalist; hence, in tin mining, where there is not the possibility that rubber cultivation offers of small holdings run by a single individual or a single family, the Malays, for all practical purposes, have no share whatsoever. Tin mines are owned almost exclusively by Europeans or Chinese, and the latter race supplies the bulk of the labour force to work them. Similarly

===== Page 307 =====
In businesses, these are in the hands of Europeans, Chinese, and Indians, and only occasionally are Malays found who are sufficiently capable for employment in commercial concerns.

Turning to rubber, one would expect to find the Malays playing an important role in the production of this agricultural product. Admitting that Malays generally have not had the capital necessary to open up large rubber estates, there has been no obstacle placed in their way of becoming labourers on rubber estates. Yet the reluctance of the Malays to enter this labour market is shown in the 1931 Census which found, in the Federated Malay States, only 7,373 Malays on rubber estates as compared with 37,863 Chinese and 203,036 Indians. Even for the Malays these figures are on the high side because many of them included in the estate population are permitted to live on the estate and are not employed there.

Of the 3,071,319 acres of rubber which had been planted in Malaya up to the end of 1931, 1,855,797 acres were made up of estates of 100 acres and over, and 1,215,522 acres of estates under 100 acres. Malay ownership of the former is insignificant and probably not much more than 50% of the latter.

Hence it is true to say that as regards the two main industries of tin and rubber, which gave rise to the importance of Malaya in the economic world and laid the foundation of the country's wealth, though the Malays benefited to a considerable extent from the many social services financed from such wealth, they played little part in their development or progress. The importance of tin and rubber to Malaya cannot be overemphasized. Together they make up more than 90% of the exports and furnish a high percentage of the national income. Had it not been for the Europeans, Chinese, and Indians, these two important industries would not have been properly developed and, in the march of progress, it is

===== Page 308 =====
Impossible to expect in British protected countries, such as Malaya, that the people who make their homes there and develop the country's resources are continually to be denied any real political status in the country.

I do not subscribe to the view that the Sultans are petty autocratic Malay Rulers—they might have been but for their British Advisers—but, in a country such as Malaya with its heterogeneous population and its large commercial interests, the Sultans are really anachronisms. Without British assistance, the Sultans would never have attained their present position; without such assistance, they would have remained local chiefs. The continuance of the British policy of "Malaya for the Malays" is gradually earning for the Malays the jealousy of the other Eastern races in Malaya, because members of those other races dislike seeing persons who are their inferiors in intellect and capabilities continually being favoured at their expense. If this pro-Malay policy is allowed to continue it is bound to lead to political unrest and may even end with the Malays regarding the British as their worst enemies. Some Malays already believe that many changes which have been brought about have been for the benefit of foreigners, and that the pro-Malay policy of the British is merely to resist the political demands of the immigrant races rather than the protection of Malay interests.

With regard to (b), i.e. the present-day position of China, there can be no doubt that in the post-war world China will wish to have a large say in Far Eastern affairs, and in this respect she will have the support of other civilized nations, particularly the United States of America where non-American interests are concerned.

In view of the high proportion of Chinese in the population of Malaya, it is natural that the Chinese Government will do all in its power to assist its own nationals in obtaining a bigger

===== Page 309 =====
Say in the Government of Malaya, and it would be wise for His Majesty's Government to anticipate such a demand by making an early public pronouncement of its intentions as to the proposed Union of the States and Settlements making up the Malay Peninsula.

In the Appendix to this Memorandum I set out in tabular form the racial position of the population of Malaya in 1931 (the latest actual census figures) in so far as it relates to the Malays, Chinese and Indians. From the figures in that Appendix the following two salient points emerge:

(a) The Malays throughout the whole of Malaya only outnumber the Chinese by 252,629.

(b) In addition to the preponderance of Chinese over Malays in Singapore and Penang, the Chinese outnumber the Malays in three of the Federated Malay States and almost equal them in numbers in the Unfederated Malay State of Johore.

If this increase in the population continues at the same rate in the future as it has done in the past, the Chinese will soon, if they have not already done so (see estimated population as at 30th June, 1941, set out in paragraph 7(B) below), outnumber the Malays. The following table gives the percentage increases by race during two decades:

1911 - 1921    1921 - 1931
Malays        15%           17%
Chinese       28%           46%

The problem of the political status of the Chinese is one which has been raised frequently in the past, fortunately not as forcibly as it might have been, and on our return to Malaya, with China as a powerful member of the victorious Allied Nations, there can be no doubt that forceful representations will be made to His Majesty's Government and to the local government in Malaya for the Chinese to have a larger say in

===== Page 310 =====
That government. It is sound policy to forestall this representation by making public at the appropriate time His Majesty's Government's intention to create a Malayan Union of the Federated Malay States, the Unfederated Malay States, and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

In fact, I think it might be said that should greater political liberty be denied to the immigrant races in Malaya, and the policy of reconstruction built on the unstable foundations of Malay monarchy, His Majesty's Government would find it difficult to maintain her position in Malaya for any lengthy period.

I have seen it stated that Indonesia has been promised powers and status equal to those of Holland itself when the Axis war is over. If this is so, it cannot be expected that the peoples of Malaya, other than the Malays, will be content to remain in the slough of narrow parochialism whilst the inhabitants of Sumatra, for instance, just across the Straits of Malacca, are granted a greater say in planning their own destinies on a national basis.

I regard (c), i.e., the need for creating a Malayan nationalism, as probably the most cogent reason for establishing a Malayan Union.

The declared policy of His Majesty's Government with regard to our Colonies and Protectorates has always been, and still is, one of trusteeship with the ultimate granting of self-government. This has been wholly achieved as regards the Dominions, which were once Colonies, and some of our present Colonies are well on the road to that ultimate goal.

One of the great difficulties in achieving such a goal is that in most Colonies societies are plural, i.e., they consist of groups of people speaking different languages, having different outlooks, following different occupations, and with different customs. That being so, such plural

===== Page 311 =====
Societies can have no common will or political outlook. The members of such societies cannot be expected to view problems from a nationalistic outlook but only from the point of view of the group to which they belong. This is particularly so in Malaya where the Malayan Chinese and Indians look towards China and India respectively. So long as the present constitutional setup of the Malay States continues, so long will be the deferment of any national spirit. Without such national spirit Malaya will find it difficult to play its proper part in the post-war world. Hence what is required is the creation and fostering of a national outlook by means of which the peoples of Malaya can turn their eyes to the country as a whole, instead of towards China, India or to the particular Malay State to which they belong, thus lifting problems on to the higher plane of national statesmanship and pooling the resources of the various States and Settlements for the benefit of a united Malaya.

Under the present constitutional setup any such change of outlook is not feasible. The only solution is through the medium of some kind of union. Such a union should not only raise the outlook of the people to a national one but should also achieve the political integration of the plural society in Malaya. Without union such political integration is impossible, without political integration there can be no national outlook, and, in my view, not much hope in the future of granting self-government to Malaya.

Finally, as regards (d), i.e. the loss of efficiency and the difficulties due to Malaya being split up into several administrative units. In order to appreciate the significance of this it is necessary to bear in mind that in the Federated and Unfederated Malay States, which make up a country no larger than England, there are ten different legislatures. Six of these legislatures, the Federal Council and the State Councils of the five Unfederated Malay States, are separate and

===== Page 312 =====
Independent. The system is in no way analogous to that of English County Councils, because each such legislature possesses, for all practical purposes, sovereign powers. For example, Negri Sembilan (this is a federation of nine miniature states) is smaller than Devonshire, Selangor is only slightly larger, and even Perak or Johore only a little more extensive than Yorkshire. With all these Governments enacting their own legislation, it has been difficult in the past to maintain uniformity in laws, an essentiality for a country which, in fact, is one economic unit. This has been more forcibly impressed on my mind in planning the legal side of the Military Administration for the re-occupation of Malaya.

In such a small area, where most of the problems are common to each of the various Governments, it is inconceivable that in order to obtain a uniformity of policy on essential matters, two Governments and nine Malay Sultans must be consulted. Such a policy must result in compromises at times, because the Sultans have always had the right of direct appeal to the Secretary of State for the Colonies, and naturally a Governor tries to avoid this.

From whatever angle the constitutional problem of Malaya is viewed, common sense dictates the answer that the only solution is a Malayan Union, which has as its object the welfare of the local Malays and the immigrant races who have made Malaya their home, and which, at the same time, will safeguard the historical individuality of each Malay State and the personal prestige of its Ruler.

I make no apology for setting out at some length the introductory material in this and the previous paragraphs, because it is essential to get a clear background of the present constitutional setup and the reasons why it is desirable, even necessary, to create a Union.

===== Page 313 =====
5. In the War Cabinet Secret Paper CMB(44)3 of the 14th January, 1944, it is laid down that:

(a) There should be a Union of all the Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

(b) It is desirable that the Island of Singapore should not be included in that Malayan Union.

The particular paragraphs of the Secret Paper setting out in general terms the proposed constitutional scheme are reproduced here in extenso because I have endeavoured to keep the constitution, which I propose later in this Memorandum, within the general framework of the scheme set out in those paragraphs. The relevant paragraphs are:

"10. Within Malaya, conditions of dynastic pride and local particularism militate against the emergence within any foreseeable future of a union of the existing Malay States under a native Ruler; nor, for reasons explained above, would the emergence of a unified Malay monarchy for the whole province be acceptable to other non-Malay communities with substantial interests in the country. The British Crown alone provides the common link and loyalty which will draw the separate communities together and promote a sense of common interest and the development of common institutions. It is, therefore, necessary that, as a first step, the old situation in which His Majesty has no jurisdiction in the Malay States should be remedied. The legal view is that our present Treaties with the Malay Rulers are at present to be regarded as still operative (though for practical purposes in suspension owing to enemy occupation of the territory). A fresh treaty, therefore, with each Ruler should be concluded as soon as possible after re-occupation under which such jurisdiction would be ceded to His Majesty as would enable him to legislate for the States under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act, notwithstanding, in the case of Kedah and Perlis, the Clauses mentioned in paragraph 5 above. Thereafter it will be possible to proceed to create a new constitution for Malaya by Order-in-Council under statutory powers. This will involve fresh legislation in the United Kingdom Parliament in respect of the Straits Settlements.

11. Our constitutional scheme should be designed, first and foremost, to provide for a Union of all the Malay States and the settlements of Penang and Malacca. A central authority representing these States and Settlements should be created, and at its head there should be a Governor with an Executive and Legislative Council. The seat of Government of this Malayan Union would be conveniently at or near Kuala Lumpur, the capital of the present limited Federation. Subject to the new central authority, the several States and Settlements would be empowered to deal with such local affairs as may be devolved upon them by the central authority. These local authorities would be so constituted as to be representative of the principal communities and interests in the State or Settlement concerned."

===== Page 314 =====
12. It is desirable that the Island of Singapore should not be included in the present conception of the Malayan Union. Its inclusion might adversely affect the Malay attitude towards the proposals for the Union of the States, which is the first and foremost object of our policy and the necessary basis for any more extensive Union. The basis for a separate organisation for the Island of Singapore already existed in the Municipality of Singapore, but the appointment of a separate Governor or Lieutenant-Governor for the Settlement will be desirable. There will, of course, be many matters of administration, e.g., Posts and Telegraphs, on which the closest collaboration will be required between the authorities at Singapore and the rest of the Peninsula, but these can be the subject of particular agreements. Nevertheless, it should be made clear that His Majesty's Government has no desire to preclude or prejudice in any way the fusion of the two Administrations in a wider Union at any time should they both agree that such a course were desirable.

14. It is recognised that this programme will require the sacrifice by the State Rulers of some part of the authority and jurisdiction hitherto exercised by them. The interests of Malaya as a whole clearly require such a sacrifice. But there is no intention that the Rulers should lose their personal position as the natural leaders of their Malay people within their State territory. Indeed, the association of their territories in the Union will give the Rulers an opportunity to play their part in a wider sphere of affairs, for it is intended to devise means to associate the Rulers personally with the machinery of the central authority, possibly as an advisory body to the Governor in respect of Malay and Mohammedan affairs. In any event, our past obligations will require that the elaboration of the new constitutional arrangements should have regard to the political, economic and social interests of the Malay race.

In addition to the scheme set out in the above mentioned paragraphs, the Secretary of State for the Colonies, in a secret and personal letter to the Supreme Allied Commander, South East Asia Command, dated the 13th June 1944, expressed the view (inter alia) that the Sultans should derive from a central Malayan authority any temporal authority which they are to exercise in the post-war arrangement, and that they should not be given any grounds for believing that they will have derived authority in the constitution - apart from their personal prestige and dignity - either by reason of delegation from any external British authority or by reason of their own royal status.

I refer to this later because in the constitution suggested by me, the Sultans are granted what I regard as

===== Page 315 =====
Their fit and proper position with certain defined functions to be performed by them.

It appears clear from paragraph 11 of the Secret Paper quoted above, that in the proposed scheme the several States and Settlements, as they now stand, are to be retained together with their present boundaries. That is my interpretation of the words -

"Subject to the new central authority, the several States and Settlements would be empowered to deal with such local affairs as may be devolved upon them by the central authority. These local authorities would be so constituted as to be representative of the principal communities and interests in the State or Settlement concerned."

The retention of the States and Settlements, as they now stand, would be in keeping with British ideals and policy, namely to build on whatever fabric of government is found in territory ceded to, or coming under the protection of, Great Britain, by the molding of a new constitution upon the fabric of government already existing, rather than by the total destruction of whatever scheme of government is found, with a consequential complete rebuilding.

6. The basis for the creation of a Union and the establishment of its constitution is the negotiation of separate treaties with the Sultans of the four Federated and five Unfederated Malay States, whereby each Sultan will cede to His Majesty such jurisdiction as will enable His Majesty to legislate under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act for all the nine Malay States, thereby setting up, under an Order-in-Council, a central legislature with powers to legislate for all these nine States. All those States will be incorporated into a Malayan Union, together with the British Settlements of Penang and Malacca, which at present form part of the Straits Settlements.

Subject to the usual power of disallowance by His Majesty, there will be no restriction on the legislative powers of this central legislature. Legislation required to have the force

===== Page 316 =====
Of law throughout the whole of the Malayan Union will be enacted by this new central legislature instead of, as formerly, by the Federal Council in respect of the four Federated Malay States, and by the State Council for each Unfederated Malay State, and by the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements as regards the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

The negotiation of these new treaties (one treaty with each separate Sultan) creates difficulties, because in fact His Majesty's Government is requesting each Sultan to sign away many of his powers. This immediately brings to the forefront what position the Malay Sultans will have in the new constitution, and the whole success or failure of the negotiations for the execution of these new treaties hangs on this matter.
