As a statement of fact, it is true to say that under the present constitution the position in the Federated Malay States is that the Residents rule and the Sultans advise, whereas in each Unfederated Malay State the Sultan rules and the British Adviser advises.

In law, the Sultans in the Federated Malay States, if they wished, could prevent a law passed by the Federal Council coming into force by refusing to assent to it, and in each of the Unfederated Malay States the Sultan's assent is essential before any law passed by the State Council can have the force of law.

Clearly, in the Union, the central legislature must be supreme and the laws passed by it cannot depend for their force of law upon the assent of each one of the nine Sultans. Accordingly, in the new constitution, the Sultans as sovereign Rulers can have no place in the central legislature and the laws passed by that legislature must be assented to solely by the Governor.

===== Page 317 =====
The possibility had occurred to me of the new Central Legislature being bicameral in form, with the composition of the Second Chamber made up of the nine Sultans and the Governor. The difficulties of such a scheme are many. The main ones are to obtain suitable representation for the Settlements of Penang and Malacca; to devise ways and means of settling differences between the Lower and Upper Chambers; without the inclusion in the Second Chamber of the principal officers of Government, e.g. Chief Secretary, Attorney-General, and Financial Secretary, the business of the Second Chamber relating to legislation, finance, and matters of a technical nature would be very difficult of transaction.

Apart from these difficulties, a Second Chamber, composed of the Malay Rulers, would not be truly representative of the peoples of the Malay Peninsula, because the Sultans would be bound to look at problems mainly from the point of view of the Malays and also from the parochial view of their own States.

Further, the main functions of a Second Chamber are (a) to prevent the discharge of precipitate and ill-considered legislation by a single House, (b) to look on problems from a non-party aspect, and (c) to discuss at leisure matters of general and non-political importance.

In short, as Bagehot has stated, a Second Chamber "Besides independence to revise judicially and power to revise effectively, has leisure to revise intellectually." The Sultans, either individually or collectively, do not possess the necessary qualifications to perform effectively the real functions of a Second Chamber and on this account, and on account of the difficulties I have just mentioned, I am satisfied that any such Second Chamber is impracticable. In any case, it would be impolitic to attempt to find it a place within the scheme of a unified constitution for the Malay Peninsula.

===== Page 318 =====
But some place must be found for the Sultans in that constitution, because I consider they will not be satisfied with being informed, to quote from paragraph 14 of the Secret Paper, that their position is merely that of "natural leaders of their Malay people within their State territories."

A provision common to all the treaties between His Majesty's Government and the Sultans is that on matters of Mohammedan Religion and Malay Custom the Sultan is not bound to ask and accept the advice of the Resident or Adviser. These are matters which only affect the Malay community of the Malay States and, as regards the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, which are British territory, such provisions are not relevant. It is right and proper that on those matters the Governor in the Union should have the advice of the Sultans as a body. Accordingly, the new constitution should provide for the establishment of a Council composed of all the Sultans (the term "Malay Rulers" must be abandoned) which should be a deliberative, consultative and advisory, but not an executive, body, with the Governor, or in his absence the Officer Administering the Government, as its President. On account of the fact that the Yam Tuan of Negri Sembilan and the Raja of Perlis are not Sultans this Council should have a Malay title as it would not be politic to give it the title in English "Council of Sultans." The main functions of this Council should be:

(a) To advise the Governor on all matters affecting Mohammedan religion and Malay custom, before any such matter is discussed or dealt with by the Union Legislature, and the Governor should be bound to have such matters discussed in the Council of Sultans in the first instance.

(b) To advise the Governor on any other matter which the Governor decides ought to be discussed in the Council of Sultans.

===== Page 319 =====
(c) To discuss any matter proposed for discussion by any Sultan, provided the Governor approves its discussion.

To restrict the functions of this Council to those mentioned in (a) alone would not satisfy the Sultans, and I see no harm in including (b) and (c).

As the relationship between His Majesty's Government and each Sultan is bound by treaty, the following matters should be debarred from discussion in the Council:

(a) Treaties, rights, dignities and privileges of each Sultan and members of his family, and the actions of each individual Sultan.

(b) The internal affairs of any individual State or Settlement which are properly within the purview of a State or Settlement Council.

It should be stated clearly that the establishment of this Council shall not prejudice in any way the treaty relations (by this I mean the new treaty arrangements) between His Majesty's Government and each Sultan, nor should the right of direct correspondence and access between any Sultan and the Governor be in any way impaired.

Such a Council should meet at regular intervals and should be opened with ceremony in keeping with the prestige of the Sultans.

Complete records of all Council Meetings should be kept in the English and Malay languages.

Council Meetings should not be public, but communiques to the press should be issued after each Meeting.

If such a Council is established with due ceremony and with the intention that the Governor should look upon it as a permanent consultative and advisory body, this ought to go a long way towards appeasing the Sultans; especially those of the Unfederated Malay States, for the loss of many of their present powers. With regard to the loss of those powers, it

===== Page 320 =====
It is appropriate here to suggest that immediately on the re-occupation of Malaya, the policy of the Military Government should be to stress the fact that the immediate Government of Malaya is a British Military Administration and give the appearance that if the Sultans act in a reasonable manner, His Majesty's Government will restore to them such of their former rights, dignities, and privileges as are consonant with the proposed new constitution. This policy should tend to give the impression that it is the Sultans, rather than His Majesty's Government, who are seeking concessions.

If the new constitution is accepted by the Sultans, the Council of Sultans will form a valuable consultative and advisory body to which the Governor of the Central Government can look for advice, particularly on matters touching Mohammedan Religion and Malay Custom, because such advice will be collective rather than individual. In fact, each Sultan, as a member of this Council, will play his part in a wider sphere than hitherto. It should be pointed out to the Sultans that in the post-war world the policy, not only of Great Britain but also of other nations, will be to deal with affairs on a regional basis rather than by individual States.

The rules of business for the Council should be framed by the Governor after consultation with the Sultans, and no meeting of the Council should be held unless the Governor or the Officer Administering the Government can attend, as it would be impolitic to hold any meeting with the Senior Sultan presiding.

I now revert to the Secretary of State's letter to which reference has been made in paragraph 5 above immediately following the citation of the relevant paragraphs from the Secret Paper CMB(44)3.

As I read the relevant passage quoted from that letter, the new constitution set up by the King-in-Council under the

===== Page 321 =====
Powers contained under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act will contain no reference to the functions of the Sultans. I would point out that it is the nature of the new constitution which will interest the Sultans more than the new treaties, because the latter merely provide the machinery by means of which the new constitution can be made.

If the Sultans find that they have been allotted no place in the new constitution, then, in my view, they will not sign the new treaties. If they are told that an appropriate place will be allotted to them by the "central Malayan authority" when it has been established (there is no precise description of the central authority contemplated and it may mean the Legislative Council of the Union Government), equally they will not be satisfied. In fact, if the "central Malayan authority" contemplated is the Legislative Council it would mean that the Malay, Chinese, and Indian unofficial members of such Council would be able to air their views in public as to what position the Sultans should occupy. Further, any Malayan authority which gives powers to the Sultans could amend or revoke such powers, which would place the Sultans in the hands of that authority.

I cannot, myself, see the necessity for such a policy. The new constitution will take away from the Sultans most of their powers, so surely it should set out the position they are to occupy, and also specify, in general terms, the functions they are desired to perform. I am certain the Sultans would wish to be placed in their appropriate position in the new constitution by His Majesty the King-in-Council, after negotiations have taken place with each Sultan, rather than by any local Malayan authority. Also, any future change in their status and functions would then depend on the King-in-Council, after further discussions with the Sultans themselves, rather than their prestige and dignity being opened to discussion in local politics.

===== Page 322 =====
In an affair of this nature, the end to be achieved is the same, but it is the way of achieving it which is an all-important matter.

7. Paragraph 11 of the Secret Paper states that there should be a Governor at the head of the Union Government with an Executive and Legislative Council. As this means that the Union Government should be conducted and constituted in the same way as the government of a Colony, it is necessary to consider the composition of both the Executive and Legislative Councils.

(A) Executive Council.

Since the Government will be run on the same lines as the government of a Colony, I assume that, apart from the Governor, the three principal officers of government will be the Chief Secretary, the Attorney-General, and the Financial Secretary. I have used the title "Attorney-General" because the term "Legal Adviser", used in the Federated and Unfederated Malay States, will no longer be appropriate.

Before setting out what I consider should be the composition of the Executive Council, the status of the British officers who are to be the heads of States and Settlements must be discussed. Under the present constitution, the status of the British Resident is higher than that of any other executive officer of government in the Federated Malay States, and in each of the Unfederated Malay States, the status of the British Adviser (General Adviser in Johore) is more or less equivalent to that of a British Resident in the Federated Malay States.

With the establishment of the Union Government, there will no longer be the necessity to accord to the senior administrative officer in each State the same status as he held previously because his functions will be less important than those of many of the heads of departments of the Union Government and, in any case, he must be subordinate to the Chief Secretary. I suggest that the titles of the posts to be held

===== Page 323 =====
By the senior executive officers in the various States and Settlements, their titles should be changed from "British Resident" in the Federated Malay States, "British Adviser" ("General Adviser" in Johore) in the Unfederated Malay States, and "Resident Councillor" in the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, to "Resident Commissioner" in each State and Settlement. The term "State Commissioner" would not be appropriate because, in Johore, certain Malay officials are called by that title. It will not be possible to have the eleven Resident Commissioners as members of the Executive Council since their status will be less than that of the former British Residents, British Advisers, and Resident Councillors. In any case, their inclusion as members of the Executive Council would not be appropriate in the unified constitution.

There should be five official members of the Executive Council. Only the Chief Secretary, Attorney-General, and Financial Secretary should be ex-officio, leaving two other officials to be nominated by the Governor.

There should be an equal number of unofficial members of the Executive Council, but it would be preferable not to prescribe such unofficial representatives on a racial basis. In the first instance, however, it is desirable that there should be one European, one Malay, one Chinese, and one Indian unofficial member on the Executive Council, leaving the Governor free to nominate the most suitable unofficial to fill the remaining vacancy.

It is not necessary to lay down that the membership of the Executive Council should be restricted to members of the Legislative Council, though this is the ideal to be aimed at eventually.

The question arises whether membership of the Executive Council should be restricted to persons who are Malayan Union Citizens; having examined the provisions of the proposed Malayan Union Citizenship Directive, I do not think such restriction will create difficulties.

This will give an Executive Council consisting of the Governor and ten members.

===== Page 324 =====
(B) Legislative Council.

I assume that the unofficial members of the Legislative Council of the Union would be nominated because, in its present stage of development, the Malay Peninsula is unsuited to the electoral system.

Under the present constitution, the Federal Council of the Federated Malay States consists of:

High Commissioner - President.

Official Members:
- Resident of Perak.
- Resident of Selangor.
- Resident of Negri Sembilan.
- Resident of Pahang.
- Federal Secretary.
- Legal Adviser.
- Financial Secretary.
- Secretary for Chinese Affairs.
- Controller of Labour.
- General Manager, F.M.S. Railways.
- Adviser on Agriculture.
- Comptroller of Customs.
- Adviser on Medical Services.
- Adviser on Education.
- Additional member (Adviser on Public Works).

Unofficial Members:
- 5 Europeans.
- 4 Malays.
- 2 Chinese.
- 1 Indian.

Hence, in addition to the Governor, there are at present fifteen official and twelve unofficial members of the Federal Council.

Under the new constitution, unofficial representation on the Legislative Council should be on a population basis as far as possible. To this end, it is convenient to divide the Malay Peninsula into the following areas:

1. Kedah and Perlis.
2. Penang and Province Wellesley.
3. Perak.
4. Selangor and Pahang.
5. Negri Sembilan and Malacca.
6. Johore.
7. Kelantan and Trengganu.

===== Page 325 =====
The estimated population of these areas, by race, as on 30th June, 1941, is:

Area                           Malays   Europeans   Eurasians   Chinese    Indians   Others    Total

1. Kedah & Perlis              387,735  674         151        116,672   62,025   16,051    583,308
2. Penang & Province Wellesley 119,913  2,833       2,682      230,679   60,503   2,437     419,047
3. Perak                       335,385  4,113       1,590      450,197   196,056  5,350     992,691
4. Selangor & Pahang           281,236  5,606       2,831      413,632   210,730  9,317     923,352
5. Negri Sembilan & Malacca    217,912  2,029       3,346      217,931   87,552   3,326     532,096
6. Johore                     302,104  1,264       352        308,901   58,498   4,178     675,297
7. Kelantan & Trengganu         555,836  262         57         40,319    9,000    8,250     613,724

It is clear from the above figures that for the areas mentioned the nominated unofficials, in every case, will be Malays and Chinese; therefore some means must be evolved whereby adequate representation can be afforded to Europeans, Indians and Eurasians. This could be done by fixing the total number of unofficial members of the Legislative Council at twenty-one, out of which fourteen members would be nominated to represent the above-mentioned areas on a population basis. The remaining seven unofficial members would be nominated to represent important interests throughout the Union, thereby affording a suitable method of securing adequate representation for European, Indian and Eurasian interests.

There should be twenty-one official members of the Legislative Council, of whom five would be the official members of the Executive Council.

(C) Temporary Advisory Council.

When Military Administration ceases and the new Civil Government is established in the Malay Peninsula, it will be impossible for that Government to set up a Legislative Council immediately. To cover the interregnum between the end of the

===== Page 326 =====
Military Administration and the setting up of a Legislative Council: the new constitution should specifically provide for the establishment of an Advisory Council to the Governor, which would take the place temporarily of a Legislative Council. This Advisory Council should consist of an unspecified number of official and unofficial members. During that temporary period, an Executive Council, consisting of five official and five unofficial members, would be in existence and function as it would when the Civil Government is established.

It is probable that the Chief Civil Affairs Officer will set up, during the period of Military Administration, an Advisory Council on which there will be unofficial representation, thus affording an opportunity for earmarking unofficials who would be suitable to serve on the Governor's Advisory Council.

Nomenclature.

(D) Nomenclature.

Suitable terms must be thought of as to what the Union Government and its Legislature are to be called. In order to stress that it is a Union, the following would appear to be appropriate terms:

(a) Union of Malaya.
(b) Government of the Union of Malaya.
(c) Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya.

The use of the term "Malaya" is not strictly accurate because Malaya includes Singapore, which is excluded from the Union, but this minor inaccurate geographical expression should be condoned in favour of stressing that the object is the unification of Malaya.

8. Reference has already been made to State Councils in the Federated Malay States being reconstituted in 1932. Perak and Negri Sembilan adopted the bicameral system, whereas in Selangor and Pahang there was only one chamber.

Putting aside the bicameral system in Perak and Negri Sembilan, the general composition of a State Council in the Federated Malay States was that it was presided over by the Sultan, with the British Resident, Malay Chiefs, State officials,

===== Page 327 =====
Unofficial Europeans, Malays, Chinese and Indians, as members.

Under the new constitution, the Sultan must drop out, and the President of a State or Settlement Council must be the Resident Commissioner. It is difficult to say now what the actual membership of a State or Settlement Council should be, but the members of those Councils should be appointed by the Governor after consulting the Resident Commissioner. As regards the nomination of unofficial members, some consideration should be paid to the racial population of each State and Settlement, but I would not make this the only guide.

In view of the fact that the Sultans will have nothing to do with the State Councils, it is advisable that an Advisory Council to the Sultan should be established in each State with appropriate functions and that such Council should be presided over by the Sultan. Its members should be appointed by the Sultan subject to the approval of the Governor.

The functions of that Advisory Council should be:

(a) To advise the Sultan on all matters affecting Mohammedan religion and Malay custom.

(b) To advise the Sultan on any other matter which the Resident Commissioner, with the approval of the Governor, refers to this Advisory Council.

As regards the functions of the State and Settlement Councils, paragraph 11 of the Secret Paper, quoted in paragraph 5 of this Memorandum, states:

"Subject to the new central authority, the several States and Settlements would be empowered to deal with such local affairs as may be devolved upon them by the central authority."

If by central authority is meant the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya, I beg to differ because in my view that would unnecessarily restrict the functions of a State or Settlement Council.

I suggest that each State and Settlement Council should be empowered to legislate.

===== Page 328 =====
(a) On all matters which, in the opinion of the Governor-in-Council, are of a merely local or private nature in the State or Settlement.

(For instance, the Malayan Collieries Enactment, enacted by the State Council of Selangor, deals solely with the Malayan Collieries Ltd. building a private railway inside the State of Selangor.)

Before the Governor-in-Council decides whether a matter requiring legislation should be enacted by a State or Settlement Council, he must first be satisfied by a certificate signed by the Attorney-General that it is a fit and proper matter for legislation by such a Council.

Having so decided, the Clerk to Executive Council will sign a certificate to that effect.

(b) On all subjects in respect of which the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya by law delegates the power of making laws to a State or Settlement Council.

(Laws made by a State or Settlement Council under this provision would be in the nature of rules or regulations etc. made under Ordinances passed by the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya. I use the word "Ordinance" because I consider that it is the appropriate term to use for laws passed by the Legislative Council. The present word "Enactment" is more applicable to the legislative acts of a local body, and accordingly I suggest that the laws passed by State or Settlement Council should be termed "Enactments".)

As regards other business of the State and Settlement Councils, I suggest that they should be empowered to discuss questions of a purely local nature. Provision can be made in their constitutions restricting discussions on other business to matters of a local nature. Such restrictions can be achieved by a provision

===== Page 329 =====
Analogous to the usual clause contained in Instructions under the Sign Manual and Signet issued to a Governor, in which it is laid down that the Governor alone is entitled to submit questions to Executive Council. If Resident Commissioners are given similar powers, there should be no danger of discussions in State and Settlement Councils extending to subjects which extend beyond local matters.

9. A re-organisation of the Courts will be necessary consequent on the formation of a Union. Before discussing what form this re-organisation should take, it is desirable to set out briefly the present constitution of the Supreme Courts throughout Malaya.

The Supreme Court of the Colony is constituted by the Courts Ordinance (Cap. 10) and exercises within the Colony the same jurisdiction and authority as are held by His Majesty's High Court of Justice in England. Its Judges are appointed by His Majesty by Letters Patent under the Public Seal of the Colony. Hence those Judges are His Majesty's Judges functioning in the King's Courts.

The Supreme Court of the Federated Malay States is constituted by the Courts Enactment (Cap. 2) and comprises the Court of Appeal and the Courts of a Judge. The Court of a Judge has full criminal and civil jurisdiction. The Chief Justice and the other Judges are appointed jointly by the Rulers of the Federated Malay States and hence they are the Sultans' Judges and not Judges of His Majesty.

Under the Straits Settlements Courts Ordinance the Judges of the Federated Malay States are declared ex officio Judges of the Supreme Court of the Colony, and a similar law makes Judges of the Colony ex officio Judges of the Supreme Court of the Federated Malay States.

As regards appeals from the Supreme Court, in civil and criminal matters the Colony and the Federated Malay States each has its own Court of Appeal consisting of three or more Judges.

===== Page 330 =====
In practice, it is generally made up of the two Chief Justices and a puisne Judge.

I do not propose to go into the constitution of all the Courts of the Unfederated Malay States. I merely set out what is the position in the State of Johore under its Courts Enactment (Cap. 54). There, the Supreme Court is made up of the Court of Appeal and the Court of a Judge. The Judge stationed in Johore is appointed by the Sultan, and a provision of the Johore Courts Enactment makes the two Chief Justices and all the Judges of both the Colony and the Federated Malay States ex officio Judges of the State of Johore. As regards the Johore Court of Appeal, it is composed of two or more Judges and, in practice, the Chief Justice and one other Judge of the Colony sit.

In 1932, correspondence took place between the Governor and the Colonial Office regarding the establishment of a Supreme Court of Malaya, consisting of a Malayan Court of Appeal and a High Court of Justice for the Colony and for each of the Federated Malay States, and such other Malay States as desired to join. It was suggested that the Judges should consist of a Chief Justice (to be styled "Chief Justice of Malaya"), Appeal Judges and puisne Judges. At the time this proposal was made, one of the main objects was to create a stronger Court of Appeal since at that time the Chief Justice of the Colony was not ex officio a Judge of the Federated Malay States, with the result that in the Federated Malay States the Court of Appeal was either composed of the Chief Justice of the Federated Malay States and two Puisne Judges or, if the appeal was against a decision of the Chief Justice himself, it was heard before three puisne Judges. This state of affairs was quickly rectified by altering the law of the Federated Malay States in order to make the Chief Justice of the Colony an ex officio Judge of the Supreme Court of the Federated Malay States.

===== Page 331 =====
There was much opposition to the suggested judicial re-organisation. It was opposed by the then Acting Chief Justice, Federated Malay States, and also by the Federated Malay States Bar Committee. I was in Malaya at the time and know that most of the opposition was due to the feeling that if this judicial re-organisation came about with a single Chief Justice stationed in Singapore, the people of Singapore would have the ear of the Chief Justice, and the members of the Bar of the Federated Malay States would come a very bad second.

In the correspondence between the Governor and the Colonial Office, much was made of the fact that in both East and West Africa, Courts of Appeal have been set up for the purpose of hearing appeals from politically and legally distinct High Courts or Supreme Courts. This is true on paper, but it is not true, in East Africa for instance, to say that the composition of the Court of Appeal is any stronger than it was in Malaya after the law was amended making the Chief Justice of the Colony an ex officio Judge of the Supreme Court of the Federated Malay States. After that amendment of the law, the Court of Appeal was invariably composed of the two Chief Justices and one puisne Judge. From my experience in East Africa, the Court of Appeal there is very often composed of two Chief Justices and a puisne Judge.

In view of the fact that the Island of Singapore and the rest of Malaya are to be two separate entities, with separate Governments, I see no useful purpose in having a Supreme Court for the whole of Malaya.

I realise that some of the executive departments in the new constitution will be pan-Malayan, but the reason for this is to keep policy uniform throughout Malaya on vital matters affecting the whole Union. The Court and its Judges are not concerned with any question of policy.

===== Page 332 =====
Accordingly, I suggest that there should be a separate High Court of the Union of Malaya with a Chief Justice at its head. The term "High Court" is used because it appears to be the custom to reserve the term "Supreme Court" for Crown Colonies. The Chief Justice will have sufficient work to do in reorganising the judicial system of all the Malay States and the two Settlements of Penang and Malacca, without having any responsibility for the Island of Singapore. In the first instance, a lot of his time will be taken up in travelling in order to get acquainted with local conditions, particularly in the Unfederated Malay States.

In addition to the Chief Justice, there must be a number of Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya stationed at various centres throughout the country. I need not go into the question of how many puisne Judges will be required or where they should be stationed.

In accordance with previous practice, the Chief Justice and the puisne Judges of the Supreme Court in Singapore should be declared ex officio Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya, and vice versa as regards the Chief Justice and puisne Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya. In practice, the Court of Appeal, whether sitting in the Union of Malaya or in Singapore, will invariably be composed of the two Chief Justices and one puisne Judge. I see no reason for creating a separate Court of Appeal which would mean the appointment of Appeal Judges with consequential extra expense.

This suggested constitution of the High Court of the Union of Malaya means that the judicial system of those Malay States which provide for Malay Judges and Appeal Courts composed of unqualified Malay Judges and a British Adviser (e.g. Perlis and Trengganu) must go. All these unqualified Judges must be replaced by qualified personnel and all appeals must go to the Court of Appeal of the Union of Malaya.

===== Page 333 =====
Death sentences in the Union of Malaya should be considered by the Governor-in-Council and not by the Malay Rulers.

I might say here, in order to give a fuller picture of this judicial system, I consider that the District Judge system in force in the Colony should be applied to the Union of Malaya, and that these appointments of District Judges should be made from the Colonial Legal Service. In fact, I think it is a matter for consideration whether District Courts should be given more extensive jurisdiction than they now possess in the Straits Settlements.

With regard to appeals from the Court of Appeal, at the present time appeals to the Privy Council are only provided for in the Colony, the Federated Malay States, and in the State of Johore. In the Colony, there is an appeal to the King-in-Council as of right by virtue of the Charter and the constitution of the Straits Settlements as a Crown Colony. The Malay States, being merely protected States, are not subject to Orders-in-Council except at the request of the Rulers thereof.

In 1906, the four Sultans of the Federated Malay States requested the King-in-Council to entertain appeals from the Federated Malay States Court of Appeal in civil matters and in pursuance of such request the Federated Malay States Order-in-Council, 1906, was promulgated giving effect to the wishes of the Sultans. That Order-in-Council was revoked and replaced by the Federated Malay States Appeal Order-in-Council in 1912 consequent upon the enactment of what is now section 33 of the Courts Enactment (Cap. 2) which reads as follows:

"From any judgment or order of the Court of Appeal in any civil matter an appeal may be made to His Britannic Majesty in Council subject to such rules and regulations as may from time to time be prescribed by order of His said Majesty in Council."

The same procedure was followed in Johore in 1921.

The new constitution should provide for appeals from the Court of Appeal of the Union of Malaya to the Privy Council.

===== Page 334 =====
10. Having dealt with the constitutional set up as regards the Government of the Union of Malaya, its Executive and Legislative Councils, the State and Settlement Councils and the Courts, it is now appropriate to discuss the question of the transfer to the Union Government of the property etc. belonging to the various States and Settlements.

As the constitution of the Union of Malaya is to be that of a Union and not a Federation, and since the local legislatures in the States and Settlements will be completely subordinate to the central government, and also because the Sultans will be no longer sovereign Rulers but will collectively form an Advisory Council to the Governor, I feel that there is no room for any compromise in the transference of State and Settlement property to the Union Government and in the latter assuming full liability for State and Settlement debts. It appears to me that if the new constitution is to be a Union let it be one in practice and not merely on paper. Accordingly I have been largely guided by the provisions of the South Africa Act, 1909, in making the following suggestions. I am well aware that the State of Johore is wealthy compared with the other Unfederated Malay States, and that in creating a Union the State of Johore will contribute into the common pool assets greater in extent than those made by other Malay States. Although it will be difficult to persuade the Sultan of Johore to see reason on this, it cannot be avoided, because to have an effective Union there can be no exceptions.

It is not possible in a general Memorandum of this kind to provide for every particular matter in the transfer of State and Settlement property to the Union Government, and I merely set out below the main subjects to which consideration must be given.

(a) Revenues.

Under the present system in the Federated Malay States certain revenues are appropriated to finance Federal Services and other revenues remain State. In the new constitution we are not, as stated above, endeavouring

===== Page 335 =====
To create a greater federation, the aim is a Union and therefore there is no necessity for deciding what revenues shall be retained by the various States and Settlements and what shall go to the Union Government. All revenues, from whatever source arising, over which the States had powers of appropriation at the time of the establishment of the Union must vest in the Union Government. The financial experts may consider it desirable to appoint a commission after the establishment of the Union, to enquire into the financial arrangements which should exist between the Union Government and the various States and Settlements.

(b) Transfer of State and Settlement property.

All cash, bankers' balances, securities for moneys etc. belonging to each State and Settlement at the establishment of the Union must be transferred to the Union Government. If, however, the balance of any funds raised at the establishment of the Union has been raised by law for any special purpose in any State or Settlement, the Union Government must appropriate such balance for the special purpose for which it was raised.

(c) State land, mines and minerals.

By section 8 of the Federated Malay States Land Code (Cap. 138) and by similar provisions in the land laws of the Unfederated Malay States, the entire property in, and control of, State land is vested solely in the Ruler of the State.

As regards the alienation of State land, each British Resident in the Federated Malay States is empowered to alienate it on behalf of the Ruler of the State, and in the Unfederated Malay States although by law the Sultan in Council is the authority having the power to alienate State land, in fact the Sultan in Council has delegated such power to other bodies and [POSSIBLY MISSING].

===== Page 336 =====
Persons. At one time, I thought it might be unnecessary to amend the law whereby State land is vested in the Ruler of the State, but having read and thought much about a unified constitution, I am of the opinion that all State land, public works, mines and minerals, and all property, whether moveable or immoveable, and all rights of whatever description belonging to the several States and Settlements must be transferred to the Union Government, which will take such property subject to any encumbrances thereon.

(d) Railways, Ports and Harbours.

With regard to the Railways, the Federated Malay States Government owns the entire railway system of Malaya, with the exception of the Johore State Railway, which is leased from Johore. The latest lease with Johore was executed on 30th November 1932, and provides that the Federated Malay States Government shall hold a lease for the Johore State Railway until 31st December 1953. Under that lease, the Federated Malay States Government pays to Johore a token sum of one dollar per year in advance and also a certain percentage of the net revenue on the working of the Federated Malay States Railway System. If there is a deficit on such a working, Johore gets nothing, but, on the other hand, Johore does not have to stand any proportionate share of any deficit.

Under the new constitution, the whole railway must be transferred to the Union Government, which means that all the capital sunk in the Railway by the Federated Malay States Government and also by the Johore Government accrues to the benefit of the Union Government.

In recent years, it cannot be said that Johore has been making much profit from their share in the

===== Page 337 =====
Federated Malay States Railway System, because, after 1931, the running of the railway has generally resulted in a deficit, and by the end of 1934 the Railway Renewals Reserve Fund had been depleted to such an extent that it only stood at 87,478,164.

Hence, as regards the transfer of the railway to the Union Government, it means a great sacrifice by the Federated Malay States Government, with a correspondingly lesser one by the Johore Government, and a resultant gain to the remainder of the States and Settlements forming the Union.

With the exception of the port and harbour property at Penang, Prai and Butterworth which is vested in the Penang Harbour Board by section 30(1)(c) of the Straits Settlements Ports Ordinance (Cap. 149), all ports and harbours in the various States and Settlements must be transferred to the Government of the Union of Malaya.

(e) State and Settlement debts.

The Union of Malaya Government having assumed ownership of all the property formerly belonging to the various States and Settlements must, in equity, assume liability for all their debts, and the interest thereon, as they stand at the time of the establishment of the Union.

(f) Financial arrangements between the Straits Settlements and the Government of the Union of Malaya.

Since Penang and Malacca are part of the Straits Settlements and under the new constitution they will be transferred from the jurisdiction of the Government of the Straits Settlements to that of the Government of the Union of Malaya, agreement must be reached as to what is to be the division of the assets and liabilities of these two Settlements.

===== Page 338 =====
Between the new Government of the Island of Singapore and the Government of the Union of Malaya, presumably this matter will be decided by agreement between the two Governments.

11. At the establishment of the Union, all the laws of each particular State and Settlement will be in force, and until the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya begins to function, there will not be in existence any Union Legislation. Accordingly, the constitution itself must provide that all the laws in force in the several States and Settlements at the establishment of the Union shall continue in force in the respective States and Settlements until repealed or amended by the Legislature of the Union of Malaya.

If the judicial system as suggested in paragraph 9 of this Memorandum is adopted, provision must be made for the pending cases and appeals being taken over by the new Courts from the old Courts.

As regards legislation after the establishment of the Union, one difficulty occurs to me. All the Malay States have not reached an equal stage of development, e.g., Trengganu, Kelantan and Perlis are behind Kedah and much behind the four Federated Malay States and Johore. In these backward States there is no legislation on several highly technical subjects, e.g., bills of exchange, and it will not be desirable to legislate on such subjects in these backward States for some time to come.

12. I have carried out a certain amount of research as to whether the Courts should be given express power to interpret the constitution or whether the granting of such express power is unnecessary.

I do not think there can be any doubt that the Courts must have power to interpret the constitution should the occasion arise.

===== Page 339 =====
In South Africa and Canada there is no law expressly conferring power on the Courts to interpret the constitution and yet numerous decided cases show that there is no doubt that the Courts in those two Dominions have assumed such jurisdiction.

On the other hand, in Australia, section 76 of the Commonwealth of Australia Constitution Act provides (inter alia) that Parliament may make laws conferring jurisdiction on the High Court on any matter arising under the constitution or involving its interpretation.

By section 30 of the Commonwealth Judiciary Act, 1903 - 1934, the High Court is given original jurisdiction in all matters arising under the constitution and involving its interpretation. Thus, in Australia the Federal High Court has been given express powers to interpret the constitution.

In the Colonies the most useful case I can find on this matter is Jerusalem - Jaffa District Governor v Suleiman Murra (1926) A.C. 321, in which it was held by the Privy Council that it was the right and duty of the Supreme Court of Palestine to consider whether an Ordinance was consistent with the terms of the Mandate.

I have not had time to go into this matter in detail but incline to the view that, for the avoidance of doubts, it is preferable to include express provision in the constitution empowering the Courts to interpret it, and express provision must be included to the effect that, in case of conflict, Ordinances of the Union of Malaya must prevail over Enactments of State and Settlement Councils.

13. Paragraph 15 of the Secret Paper states -

"The complete details of the application of this policy and in particular the detailed measures to be designed for the creation of a self-governing institution can only be worked out on the spot after re-occupation in the light of the prevailing conditions."

Knowing the mentality of the Sultans and the necessity for getting the new treaties executed as quickly as possible after the re-occupation of Malaya, I consider that the new

===== Page 340 =====
The constitution must be in complete draft form before we return to Malaya. The new treaties are merely the machinery by means of which a new constitution can be created, and it is the new constitution which will interest the Sultans. It would be fatal to let it appear that we had no cut and dried scheme for that constitution on our return. The less delay in negotiating the new treaties the better chance we have of the Sultans agreeing to them. They will not agree unless we are in a position to tell them exactly what our plans are regarding the new constitution. Therefore I urge that the new treaties and the new constitution should be in complete draft form before the re-occupation of Malaya.

Conclusion.

14. The constitution as outlined in this Memorandum was originally submitted to the Colonial Office on 15th November, 1944, as representing the views of the writer only.

Since that date discussions have taken place between representatives of the Colonial Office and of C.A. (M.P.U.). As a result of those discussions a few amendments of minor importance have been made in the Memorandum as originally drafted, and I think that the Memorandum as it now stands represents the considered views of those representatives of the Colonial Office and of C.A. (M.P.U.) who read the Memorandum in its original form and attended those discussions.

C.A. (M.P.U.), 38 Hyde Park Gate, LONDON, S.W.7.
H.C. WILLAN

8th March, 1945.

Colonial Office 1 - 20
Major-General Hone 21 - 40
Colonel Newboult 41
Colonel McKerron 42
Mr. O'Connor 43

Re draft Note (as yet unfinished) on the above mentioned Memorandum - I suggest paragraph 1 thereof should be redrafted to read -

"The War Cabinet has approved, for planning purposes, the policy of establishing after the war a Malayan Union which will consist of the nine Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, and that this shall be achieved by the negotiation of fresh agreements with the Rulers which shall cede full jurisdiction to His Majesty in their territories. ===== Page 345 =====
Dear Gent,

I will let you have in the course of a day or two a reply to the point you have put to me about securing jurisdiction for the Crown in the territories of Malay Rulers. I may say at once that there has been no occasion for requiring an Indian Ruler to cede comprehensive jurisdiction over an extensive area in very recent times. Ordinarily, cessions have been needed only to enable cantonments to be established, and there is express provision for this in our Treaty with Mysore, 1913, art. 8 (Aitchison Vol. IX page 309) which enables us to maintain a big Civil and Military Station at Bangalore.

A much more extensive cession of jurisdiction was obtained from Hyderabad in Berar, now incorporated in the Central Provinces, by way of a lease in perpetuity on payment of Rs. 25 lakhs p.a. by an Agreement of 1902 (Aitchison Vol. IX page 165), this being in liquidation of Hyderabad's debt to the British Government for maintenance of troops for the State's protection.

Similarly, other of the dominions of the Khan of Kolet which today form the greater part of the Province of British Baluchistan were made over to be administered by the British in full jurisdiction in a series of engagements (see Aitchison Vol. XI Baluchistan Nos.: XXVI and XXVIII) by way of a perpetual lease in return for money consideration.

Again

===== Page 346 =====
Again, there have been very numerous cessions of jurisdiction, criminal, civil, and when this was not forgotten, fiscal, over stretches of railway line constructed through State territory.

It would, I suppose, be formally contended in all these cases that the cessions were acquired by agreement, the Rulers accepting the advantages accruing to them from the arrangements. But in the background there always loomed the claim enunciated by Canning after the Mutiny to have established British paramountcy over all India, a claim, of course, resting on superior force.

I doubt if my more considered reflections will supply you with a more soothing formula, more closely associated with international law. After all, our jurisdiction in the China Treaty Ports and in Persia, etc., was acquired in much the same way.

I do not remember any case in which we have extruded a Ruler from the whole of the administration in the whole of his territories and have called this accession of jurisdiction. When that has happened, it has been called annexation!

Yours sincerely,

(Sgd.) P.J. Patrick.

My dear Patrick,

You have been so good as to allow me to bother you with the matter of Sir Harold MacMichael's treaty-making mission to the Malay States when the place is liberated. Can you help me further with your great experience of relations with Indian States, viz. His Majesty has no jurisdiction in any of the Malay States at present. The object of the new treaties with each of the Rulers will be the cession by the Ruler of full jurisdiction to His Majesty. If all or any of the Rulers flatly decline to sign a fresh treaty to this effect, what ultimate authority can we invoke to justify our de facto and de jure exercise of such jurisdiction? Clearly we can act in a drastic way, e.g. by annexation; also, I suppose we could, at any rate in cases where the throne of any State has become vacant during Japanese occupation by death of the Ruler, hold up any new appointment of Sultan until we can get what we want either from the principal candidate for the throne or the next best on the list.

But in your experience with any comparable Indian States in which His Majesty has any jurisdiction, can you give us any advice as to what authority we can rely upon to justify our going ahead with what we intend to do, regardless of a Ruler's refusal to sign the requisite agreement?

Yours sincerely,

(Sgd.) G.E.J. Gent.

P.J. Patrick, Esq., C.S.I.

===== Page 352 =====
Attached to this Note is a memorandum, prepared by the Malayan Planning Unit and revised in consultation with the Colonial Office, which contains detailed proposals for the framing of the post-war Malayan Constitution. The War Cabinet has approved, for planning purposes, the policy of establishing after the war a Malayan Union which will consist of the nine Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, and that this shall be achieved by the negotiation of fresh Agreements with the Rulers which shall cede full jurisdiction to His Majesty in their territories. The memorandum now submitted deals with the practical application of that policy. It is felt that each of the proposals contained in the memorandum is implicit in the policy itself, and is inherent in the very idea of a Malayan Union (as opposed to an extended Federation of Malay States).

2. The memorandum has been discussed in draft with Sir Harold MacMichael, and in consultation with him the following points have emerged, which may not be suitable for inclusion in the memorandum itself, but which it is nevertheless desirable to bring to the attention of the Secretary of State.

3. The cession of jurisdiction by each Ruler to His Majesty is accepted as a necessary step, but it is one which is capable of being misrepresented by ignorant, and still more by mischievous, people as a wanton breach of faith and an act of naked imperialism. If the negotiations are allowed to become "bogged" by argument and counter-argument the chances diminish of our policy being represented in the right light from the outset, and the opportunities for misrepresentation will increase.

===== Page 353 =====
This cardinal consideration leads to several conclusions.

4. The timing of the negotiations with the Malay Rulers is of the utmost importance. The missing of opportunities through inadequate forethought or insufficient boldness of outlook, leading inevitably to uncertainty of touch and consequent hesitation, would not only jeopardise the negotiations themselves, but would have other ill-effects which might be very far-reaching. Consequently, the work of elaborating the proposed new Constitution and drafting the necessary Instruments should be achieved as rapidly as possible. Once the present draft memorandum has been approved by the Secretary of State, with such alterations as he desires to make, the drafting of an Order in Council establishing the new Constitution should be begun immediately.

5. The actual Treaties with the Rulers will, of course, be very short, and will merely enshrine the cession of jurisdiction to His Majesty. They will not go into the consequential details of what His Majesty will do with the jurisdiction when acquired. Nevertheless, these consequences will be of the greatest interest to the Malay Rulers, and it may be assumed that they will fundamentally affect their willingness to sign the Treaties. While there is room for argument as to whether the actual draft Order in Council should be shown to them or not, it is important that we should be able to explain our proposals to them in a clear, comprehensive and convincing manner (and in a Malay language version).

6. Equally important, if we are to attain our objective of a smooth and rapid settlement, will be the method and procedure of handling individual Rulers both before and during the negotiations. It has been agreed with S.A.C., S.E.A that the Military authorities shall not be involved in the negotiations, which are to be left to Sir Harold MacMichael. Nevertheless, it is essential that the Military authorities should be thoroughly acquainted with the situation, and should avoid any action which may subsequently embarrass Sir Harold's

===== Page 354 =====
Task. In particular, they should not formally recognise any claimant in any of the nine States as being the legitimate Ruler of that State, even if he has held such a position uninterruptedly since before the Japanese occupation. This will mean considerable vigilance, since claimants may be expected to try and establish their position at the earliest possible moment and by all possible means. It will no doubt be necessary for the Military authorities to use the de facto authority of this or that Malay personage in each area in order to achieve their essential objectives of maintenance of security, prevention of disease and unrest, distribution of supplies, etc.; but it is essential that none of the Rulers should have been placed by the Military in a securely established position by the time when Sir Harold arrives in Malaya. In the first place, a recognised Malay Ruler who refused to sign a new Treaty might have to be deposed (an extremely inconvenient course, to say the least); and in the second place, if any of the Rulers feel that the ground is firm under their feet, they will unquestionably be more likely to adopt a non-cooperative, obstructive, and non-possumus attitude. They must realise that for their reinstatement their willingness to fall in with the policy of H.M.G. will be an indispensable condition. This does not mean, of course, that H.M.G. will be prepared to recognise as a Ruler of any of the States whoever is "prepared to sign on the dotted line". Such a course might mean the acceptance in some or all cases of the most worthless candidate and this again would open the door to reasonable criticism and be, indeed, a poor beginning to the history of the new Malaya. In the event of an otherwise worthy claimant being unwilling to follow the accepted policy of H.M.G., great efforts would doubtless be made to persuade him, before drastic steps were taken. But it must be recognised that in the last resort

===== Page 355 =====
Resort a "potential" Ruler who remained adamant might have to be displaced in favour of the best alternative candidate; and no Ruler should therefore be officially recognised before the negotiations are concluded.

7. For the rest, the Military authorities can be of great assistance to Sir Harold when he starts his work. For the reasons already given, there should be no unduly protracted delay in his arrival, after the liberation of the Peninsula. On the other hand, it would be a mistake if he were to appear on the scene immediately. There should be a brief period of, say, one month, during which the Military Administration would have time to assess the position in each of the States. They could then advise Sir Harold, on his arrival, as to the "candidate" in each State whose claims and conduct entitle him in their view to be the chosen instrument for the negotiations and the local throne. In many cases, the choice would probably be obvious. Sir Harold would then pursue his negotiations, proceeding in each case according to the established local custom. (The customs of the various States are being examined, and he will be provided with the facts before his departure). Thus, in some States it may be necessary for the new Treaty to be signed by certain of the State Chiefs as well as by the Ruler himself. The Ruler must be recognised before he actually signs the Treaty since the signature of the State Ruler is necessary; but he should be firmly committed to sign (perhaps by initialling a draft after obtaining whatever approval of Chiefs is required by the custom of his State) before recognition takes place.

8. Then again, if the negotiations are to result in that speedy settlement at which we should aim, it will be important that Sir Harold MacMichael should have plenipotentiary powers to negotiate on behalf of H.M.G. so that, having worked the Rulers (or "potential" Rulers) up to the point where they are ready to sign, he will be able to clinch the business then and there. If at that point, or

===== Page 356 =====
At an earlier stage in the proceedings, he has to take refuge in expressions of uncertainty or doubt on issues of vital moment, and to refer home various points raised, this will throw open the door to conditions, qualifications, and second thoughts on the part of the Rulers, as a result of which the opportunity of any (and certainly of a rapid) settlement may be lost.

9. Finally, Sir Harold has represented that his task will be greatly eased if he is able to say something reassuring to the Malays regarding the degree of support to be accorded to their own position vis-à-vis the Chinese. It is true that under the proposals for Malayan Union citizenship, every Malay will automatically achieve citizenship, while only a proportion of Chinese will do so. This is an argument which can be legitimately used to show that the Malays will not be deserted by H.M.G. under the new policy, although our plans embody a belated recognition and regularising of the reasonable rights of those non-Malays who have definitely chosen to make their homes in Malaya, and those plans would in fact go far to safeguard the Malay position in its political aspects to a greater extent than could be said of our policy in the past. The full strength of this argument, however, may not be apparent to those with whom it will be used, and Sir Harold hopes that it may be backed up by others.

10. For instance it is assumed that he will have every warrant for promising that particular attention will be given to the education of Malays and their training in the field of local government. He also suggests that it would be both justifiable and helpful if he could point to a forward policy of encouraging, or at least facilitating, the immigration of Malays from the Netherlands East Indies as a counterpoise to the immigration of Chinese and Indians. The Colonial Office is already in touch with the Foreign Office, with a view to an approach being made to the Dutch Government on this

===== Page 357 =====
Very point. If the Dutch response is favourable, it may be possible to go a long way towards meeting this point; though the need of avoiding any overt discrimination against Chinese and Indians will, it is fully recognised, have to be carefully watched.

11. Above all the question must be faced of the course to be followed if the Rulers, or any of them, flatly decline to sign new Treaties. For reasons suggested in paragraph 6 above it will not be practicable simply to refuse recognition of the recalcitrant party or parties and select more pliable instruments. It is to be assumed, on the other hand, that H.M.G. will not on this account jettison their policy in respect either of those States which refuse assent to it, or, in desperation, of all. What, then, are the ultimate sanctions applicable? It may be impossible at this stage to formulate procedure, step by step, with precision and exactitude, but for purpose of negotiation, it is necessary to succeed (and particularly so in dealing with an Eastern people) that the broad lines to be followed in such an eventuality should be known both to Sir Harold who must be sure of his ground and to the several Rulers and Chiefs who must be left in no doubt as to H.M.G.'s definite intention. If once it is clear beyond any doubt that the policy decided upon will be carried out, and doubt is at the same time removed as to the way in which this can and will be done, the issue is made a clear cut one and the chances of protracted obstruction are minimised.

12. H.M.G.'s decision on future policy for Malaya offers a bright hope for the efficiency, security and the promotion of self-government in that country, and it offers a safeguard for the political future of the Malays themselves since they will be the most numerous element in the citizenship. It is therefore imperative that a satisfactory answer should be found for the question of ultimate sanctions as, if left unsolved, it may wreck the possibility of achieving that policy.

13. The sanctions which it is possible to consider appear to be the following:

(a) annexation of any State whose ruler refuses to cede jurisdiction;

===== Page 358 =====
(b) refusal to recognise as Ruler any claimant who refuses to do so and the acceptance and recognition of the next best on the list of claimants who will agree to fall in with H.M.G's policy.

Of these, the first course can be expected to create severe local reactions amongst the Malays throughout the Peninsula, and to be adversely criticised by world opinion. The second would expose us to criticism inside and outside Malaya, though its degree might depend upon the extent to which the chosen ruler was merely a worthless puppet. This course might not be a complete solution if in any State there were no possible Malay candidate willing to accept our terms.

===== Page 359 =====
My dear Gent,

Reference my second draft of the Future Constitution for the Malay Peninsula, there are two matters which I promised to pursue further. They are:

(a) The question of the membership of Executive Council, Legislative Council and other local bodies being restricted to Malayan Union Citizens - see bottom of page 23.

(b) Whether the port and harbour property at Penang is vested by law in the Penang Harbour Board - see middle of page 37.

2. Re (a) - Paragraph 8 of the Malayan Union Citizenship Directive reads as follows:

"Save with the consent of the Governor

(a) no person who is not a Malayan Union citizen should be admitted to the Public Service, and

(b) the appointment of unofficial persons to be members of local councils and similar bodies should be confined to Malayan Union citizens."

As regards officials who are not Malayan Union citizens, they will already be members of the Public Service and paragraph 8(b) of the Directive will not apply to them, therefore they are adequately covered.

G.E.J.Gent, Esq., C.M.G., D.S.O., O.B.E., M.C.,
Colonial Office,
Downing Street, S.W.1.

P.T.O.

===== Page 360 =====
As regards unofficials, I do not anticipate any difficulty. Most of them whom the Governor desires to appoint to Executive Council, Legislative Council, etc., will be Malayan Union Citizens automatically by virtue of paragraph 2 of the Directive, i.e., they will have resided in Malaya for a period of 10 years out of the previous 15 years, the period of Japanese occupation being disregarded. As regards the few, they will be very few, who on account of insufficient residence in Malaya are not qualified automatically to become Malayan Union citizens, the Governor has full discretion under paragraph 8 of the Directive to consent to their appointment to Executive Council, Legislative Council, etc.

Paragraph 8(b) of the Malayan Union Citizenship Directive is merely a transitory provision which will become obsolete after the Citizenship Order in Council has been in force just over 5 years. I say that because I presume the policy will be not to appoint any unofficial to be a member of Executive Council, Legislative Council, etc., who is unwilling to apply for Malayan Union Citizenship under paragraph 3 of the Citizenship Directive.

Hence, in my view, there is no need to consider this matter further as no difficulty arises.

3. Re (b) - The relevant law on the subject is contained in the Straits Settlements Ports Ordinance (Cap 149), which by section 30(1)(c) vests the port and harbour property at Penang, Prai and Butterworth (the property is more particularly described on pages 114 and 115 of Volume IV of the revised edition of the Laws of the Straits Settlements) in the Penang Harbour Board.

On account of this the last five lines of sub-paragraph (d) of paragraph 10 on page 37 of my Memorandum should be amended to read as follows:

"With the exception of the port and harbour property at Penang, Prai and Butterworth which is vested in the Penang Harbour Board by section 30(1)(c) of the Straits Settlements Ports Ordinance (Cap 149), all ports and harbours in the various States and Settlements must be transferred to the Government of the Union of Malaya."

Yours sincerely,

[Signature]
