EXPLANATORY NOTES:

1. Underlined portions are amendments to Willan's original memorandum dated 15th November, 1944.

2. The page numbers of the redraft correspond, page by page, with the page numbers of the original draft, and they should be substituted in place of the corresponding pages of that original draft, thus giving a complete Second Draft.

3. ( ) denotes a deletion from the original memorandum.

===== Page 365 =====
FUTURE CONSTITUTION FOR MALAY PENINSULA.

1. In the Far Cabinet Secret Paper CMB(AA) dated 14th January, 1944, the Secretary of State for the Colonies sets out, in general terms, the future constitutional policy for British Colonial Territories in South East Asia. It is clear that as regards Malaya the aim is -

(a) A separate Government for Singapore Island.

(b) The Union of the four Federated Malay States, the five Unfederated Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

2. Before discussing a new constitutional policy for Malaya it is as well to state briefly what is the present constitution.

Malaya is divided into three parts -

(a) The Straits Settlements, a Crown Colony, for which His Majesty is able to legislate under the Straits Settlements Act, 1866, and the British Settlements Act, 1897.

(b) The four Malay States of Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan and Pahang over which His Majesty has no powers under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. These States formed themselves into a Federation in 1896, and in 1909 a Federal Council was established which enacted legislation having the force of law throughout the Federation.

(c) The five Unfederated Malay States of Johore, Kedah, Kelantan, Trengganu and Perlis. Each of these States is independent and His Majesty has no powers under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act over any of them. Each such State has its own legislature, namely the Ruler in State Council.

All the nine Malay States are bound to Great Britain by treaties which date from 1874, in the case of Perak, to 1930, in the case of Perlis. The provisions of these treaties follow the same general lines, namely that each Malay Ruler shall accept a British officer whose advice shall be asked

===== Page 366 =====
In short, as Bagehot has stated, a Second Chamber "Besides independence to revise judicially and power to revise effectively, has leisure to revise intellectually." The Sultans, either individually or collectively, do not possess the necessary qualifications to perform effectively the real functions of a Second Chamber and on this account, and on account of the difficulties I have just mentioned, I am satisfied that any such Second Chamber is impracticable. In any case, it would be impolitic to attempt to find it a place within the scheme of a unified constitution for the Malay Peninsula.

But, some place must be found for the Sultans in that constitution, because I consider they will not be satisfied with being informed, to quote from paragraph 14 of the Secret Paper, that their position is merely that of "natural leaders of their Malay people within their State territories."

A provision common to all the treaties between His Majesty's Government and the Sultans is that on matters of Malay Religion and Custom the Sultan is not bound to ask and accept the advice of the Resident or Adviser. These are matters which only affect the Malay community of the Malay States and, as regards the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, which are British territory, such provisions are not relevant. It is right and proper that on those matters the Governor in the Union Government should have the advice of the Sultans as a body. Accordingly the new constitution should provide for the establishment of a Council composed of all the Sultans (the term "Malay Rulers" must be abandoned) which should be a deliberative, consultative and advisory, but not an executive, body, with the Governor, or in his absence the Officer Administering the Government, as its President. On account of the fact that the Yang Tuan of Negri Sembilan and the Raja of Perlis are not Sultans this Council should have a Malay title as it would not be politic to give it the title in English "Council of Sultans." The main functions of this Council should be --

(a) To advise the Governor on all matters affecting Mohammedan religion and Malay custom, before any such matter is discussed or dealt with by the Union.

===== Page 367 =====
Legislature, and the Governor should be bound to have such matters discussed in the Council of Sultans in the first instance.

(b) To advise the Governor on any other matter which the Governor decides ought to be discussed in the Council of Sultans.

(c) To discuss any matter proposed for discussion by any Sultan, provided the Governor approves its discussion.

To restrict the functions of this Council to those mentioned in (a) alone would not satisfy the Sultans, and I see no harm in including (b) and (c).

As the relationship between His Majesty's Government and each Sultan is bound by treaty, the following matters should be debarred from discussion in the Council:

(a) Treaties, rights, dignities and privileges of each Sultan and members of his family, and the actions of each individual Sultan.

(b) The internal affairs of any individual State or Settlement which are properly within the purview of a State or Settlement Council.

It should be stated clearly that the establishment of this Council shall not prejudice in any way the treaty relations (by this I mean the new treaty arrangements) between His Majesty's Government and each Sultan, nor should the right of direct correspondence and access between any Sultan and the Governor be in any way impaired.

Such a Council should meet at regular intervals and should be opened with ceremony in keeping with the prestige of the Sultans.

Complete records of all Council Meetings should be kept in the English and Malay languages.

Council Meetings should not be public, but communiques to the press should be issued after each Meeting.

If such a Council is established with due ceremony and with the intention that the Governor should look upon it as a permanent consultative and advisory body, this ought to go a long way towards

===== Page 368 =====
Appeasing the Sultans, especially those of the Unfederated Malay States, for the loss of many of their present powers. With regard to the loss of those powers, it is appropriate here to suggest that immediately on the re-occupation of Malaya, the policy of the Military Government should be to stress the fact that the immediate Government of Malaya is a British Military Administration and give the appearance that if the Sultans act in a reasonable manner, His Majesty's Government will restore to them such of their former rights, dignities, and privileges as are consonant with the proposed new constitution. This policy should tend to give the impression that it is the Sultans, rather than His Majesty's Government, who are seeking concessions.

If the new constitution is accepted by the Sultans, the Council of Sultans will form a valuable consultative and advisory body to which the Governor and the Central Government can look for advice, particularly on matters touching Malay Religion and Custom, because such advice will be collective rather than individual. In fact, each Sultan, as a member of this Council, will play his part in a wider sphere than hitherto. It should be pointed out to the Sultans that in the post-war world the policy, not only of Great Britain but also of other nations, will be to deal with affairs on a regional basis rather than by individual States.

The rules of business for the Council should be framed by the Governor after consultation with the Sultans, and no meeting of the Council should be held unless the Governor or the Officer Administering the Government can attend, as it would be impolitic to hold any meeting with the Senior Sultan presiding.

I now revert to the Secretary of State's letter to which reference has been made at the beginning of this paragraph immediately following the citation of the relevant paragraphs from the Secret Paper CMB(44)3.

===== Page 369 =====
Government and, in any case, he must be subordinate to the Chief Secretary. I suggest that the titles of the posts to be held by the senior executive officers in the various States and Settlements should be changed from "British Resident" in the Federated Malay States, "British Adviser" ("General Adviser" in Johore) in the Unfederated Malay States, and "Resident Councillor" in the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, to "Resident Commissioner" in each State and Settlement. The term "State Commissioner" would not be appropriate because in Johore certain Malay officials are called by that title. It will not be possible to have the eleven Resident Commissioners as members of Executive Council since their status will be less than that of the former British Residents, British Advisers and Resident Councillors. In any case, their inclusion as members of Executive Council would not be appropriate in the unified constitution.

There should be five official members of Executive Council. Only the Chief Secretary, Attorney-General and Financial Secretary should be ex-officio, leaving two other officials to be nominated by the Governor.

There should be an equal number of unofficial members of Executive Council but it would be preferable not to prescribe such unofficial representatives on a racial basis. In the first instance, however, it is desirable that there should be one European, one Malay, one Chinese and one Indian unofficial member on Executive Council, leaving the Governor free to nominate the most suitable unofficial to fill the remaining vacancy.

It is not necessary to lay down that the membership of Executive Council should be restricted to members of Legislative Council, though this is the ideal to be aimed at eventually.

The question arises whether membership of Executive Council should be restricted to persons who are Malayan Union Citizens. This may create difficulties in the early stages on records Europeans, and this matter requires further consideration in relation to the Malayan Union Citizenship Directive.

This will give an Executive Council consisting of the Governor and ten members.

===== Page 370 =====
(B) Legislative Council

I assume that the unofficial members of the Legislative Council of the Union would be nominated because, in its present stage of development, the Malay Peninsula is unsuited to the electoral system.

Under the present constitution the Federal Council of the Federated Malay States consists of:

High Commissioner - President.
Official Members:
Resident of Perak.
Resident of Selangor.
Resident of Negri Sembilan.
Resident of Pahang.
Federal Secretary.
Legal Adviser.
Financial Secretary.
Secretary for Chinese Affairs.
Controller of Labour.
General Manager, F.N.S. Railways.
Adviser on Agriculture.
Comptroller of Customs.
Adviser on Medical Services.
Adviser on Education.
Additional member (Adviser on Public Works).

Unofficial Members:
5 Europeans.
4 Malays.
2 Chinese.
1 Indian.

Hence, in addition to the Governor, there are at present fifteen official and twelve unofficial members of the Federal Council.

Under the new constitution unofficial representation on Legislative Council should be on a population basis as far as possible. To this end it is convenient to divide the Malay Peninsula into the following areas:

1. Kedah and Perlis.
2. Penang and Province Wellesley.
3. Perak.
4. Selangor and Pahang.
5. Negri Sembilan and Malacca.
6. Johore.
7. Kelantan and Trengganu.

===== Page 371 =====
The estimated population of these areas, by race, as on the 30th June, 1941, is:

1. Kedah & Perlis: 387,735 674 151 116,672 62,025 16,051 583,308
2. Penang & Province Wellesley: 119,913 2,833 2,682 230,679 60,903 2,437 419,047
3. Perak: 335,385 4,115 1,590 450,197 196,056 5,350 992,691
4. Selangor & Pahang: 281,256 5,606 2,911 413,632 210,730 9,317 923,352
5. Negri Sembilan & Malacca: 217,912 2,029 3,346 217,931 87,552 3,326 532,096
6. Johore: 302,104 1,264 352 308,901 53,498 4,170 675,297
7. Kelantan & Trengganu: 555,836 262 57 40,319 9,000 8,250 613,724

It is clear from the above figures that for the areas mentioned the nominated unofficials, in every case, will be Malayan and Chinese, therefore some means must be evolved whereby adequate representation can be afforded to Europeans, Indians and Eurasians. This could be done by fixing the total number of unofficial members of Legislative Council at twenty-one, out of which fourteen members would be nominated to represent the above-mentioned areas on a population basis. The remaining seven unofficial members would be nominated to represent important interests throughout the Union, thereby affording a suitable method of securing adequate representation for European, Indian and Eurasian interests.

There should be twenty-one official members of the Legislative Council, of whom five would be the official members of the Executive Council.

(0) Temporary Advisory Council.

When Military Administration ceases and the new Civil Government is established in the Malay Peninsula, it will be impossible for that Government to set up a Legislative Council immediately. To cover the interregnum between the end of the Military Administration and the setting up of a Legislative Council, the new constitution should specifically provide for the establishment of an Advisory Council to the Governor which would take its place.

===== Page 372 =====
Temporarily of a Legislative Council. This Advisory Council should consist of an unspecified number of official and unofficial members. During that temporary period, the Executive Council, consisting of five official and five unofficial members, would be in existence and function as it would when the Civil Government is established.

It is probable that the Chief Civil Affairs Officer will set up, during the period of Military Administration, an Advisory Council on which there will be unofficial representation, thus affording an opportunity for enmarking unofficials who would be suitable to serve on the Governor's Advisory Council.

Nomenclature.

(D) Nomenclature.

Suitable terms must be thought of as to what the Union Government and its Legislature are to be called. In order to stress that it is a Union the following would appear to be appropriate terms -

(a) Union of Malaya.
(b) Government of the Union of Malaya.
(c) Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya.

The use of the term "Malaya" is not strictly accurate because Malaya includes Singapore, which is excluded from the Union, but this minor inaccurate geographical expression should be condoned in favour of stressing that the object is the unification of Malaya.

8. Reference has already been made to State Council in the Federated Malay States being reconstituted in 1932. Perak and Negri Sembilan adopted the bi-cameral system, whereas in Selangor and Pahang there was only one chamber.

Putting aside the bi-cameral system in Perak and Negri Sembilan, the general composition of a State Council in the Federated Malay States was that it was presided over by the Sultan, with the British Resident, Malay Chiefs, State officials, unofficial Europeans, Malays, Chinese and Indians as members.

Under the new constitution the Sultan must drop out and the President of a State or Settlement Council must be the Resident Commissioner. It is difficult to say now what the actual membership of a State or Settlement Council should be, but the members of those Councils should be appointed by the Governor on the advice of the Resident Commissioner. As regards the nomination of unofficial members some consideration should be paid to the racial population of each State and Settlement, but I would not make this the only guide.

===== Page 373 =====
In view of the fact that the Sultans will have nothing to do with the State Councils, it is advisable that an Advisory Council to the Sultan should be established in each State with appropriate functions and that such Council should be presided over by the Sultan.

The functions of that Advisory Council should be:

(a) To advise the Sultan on all matters affecting Mohammedan religion and Malay custom.

(b) To advise the Sultan on any other matter which the Resident Commissioner, with the approval of the Governor, refers to this Advisory Council.

As regards the functions of the State and Settlement Councils, paragraph 11 of the Secret Paper, quoted in paragraph 5 of this Memorandum, states:

"Subject to the new central authority, the several States and Settlements would be empowered to deal with such local affairs as may be devolved upon them by the central authority."

If by central authority is meant the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya, I beg to differ because in my view that would unnecessarily restrict the functions of a State or Settlement Council.

I suggest that each State and Settlement Council should be empowered to legislate:

(a) On all matters, which, in the opinion of the Governor-in-Council, are of a merely local or private nature in the State or Settlement.

(For instance, the Malayan Collieries enactment, enacted by the State Council of Selangor, deals solely with the Malayan Collieries Ltd, building a private railway inside the State of Selangor.)

Before the Governor-in-Council decides whether a matter requiring legislation should be enacted by a State or Settlement Council, he must first be satisfied by a certificate signed by the Attorney-General that it is a fit and proper matter for legislation by such a Council.

Having so decided, the Clerk to Executive Council will sign a certificate to that effect.

(b) On all subjects in respect of which the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya by law delegates the power of making laws to a State or Settlement Council.

(Laws made by a State or Settlement Council under this provision would be in the nature of rules or regulations etc. made under Ordinances passed by the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya. I use the word "Ordinance" because I consider that it is the appropriate term to use for laws passed by the Legislative Council. The present word "Enactment" is more applicable to the legislative acts of a local body, and accordingly, I suggest that the laws passed by State or Settlement Council should be termed "Enactments".)

===== Page 374 =====
As regards other business of the State and Settlement Councils, I suggest that they should be empowered to discuss questions of a purely local nature. Provision can be made in their constitutions restricting discussions on other business to matters of a local nature. Such restrictions can be achieved by a provision analogous to the usual clause contained in Instructions under the Sign Manual and Signet issued to a Governor, in which it is laid down that the Governor alone is entitled to submit questions to Executive Council. If Resident Commissioners are given similar powers, there should be no danger of discussions in State and Settlement Councils extending to subjects which extend beyond local matters.

9. A re-organisation of the Courts will be necessary consequent on the formation of a Union. Before discussing what form this re-organisation should take, it is desirable to set out briefly the present constitution of the Supreme Courts throughout Malaysia.

The Supreme Court of the Colony is constituted by the Courts Ordinance (Cap. 10) and exercises within the Colony the same jurisdiction and authority as are held by His Majesty's High Court of Justice in England. Its Judges are appointed by His Majesty by Letters Patent under the Public Seal of the Colony. Hence those Judges are His Majesty's Judges functioning in the King's Courts.

The Supreme Court of the Federated Malay States is constituted by the Courts Enactment (Cap. 2) and comprises the Court of Appeal and the Courts of a Judge. The Court

===== Page 375 =====
In view of the fact that the Island of Singapore and the rest of Malaya are to be two separate entities, with separate Governments, I see no useful purpose in having a Supreme Court for the whole of Malaya.

I realize that some of the executive departments in the new constitution will be pan-Malayan, but the reason for this is to keep policy uniform throughout Malaya on vital matters affecting the whole Union. The Court and its Judges are not concerned with any question of policy.

Accordingly, I suggest that there should be a separate High Court of the Union of Malaya with a Chief Justice at its head. The term "High Court" is used because it appears to be the custom to reserve the term "Supreme Court" for Crown Colonies. The Chief Justice will have sufficient work to do in re-organizing the judicial system of all the Malay States and the two Settlements of Penang and Malacca, without having any responsibility for the Island of Singapore. In the first instance, a lot of his time will be taken up in travelling in order to get acquainted with local conditions, particularly in the Unfederated Malay States.

In addition to the Chief Justice, there must be a number of Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya stationed at various centres throughout the country. I need not go into the question of how many puisne Judges will be required or where they should be stationed.

In accordance with previous practice, the Chief Justice and the puisne Judges of the Supreme Court in Singapore should be declared ex officio Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya, and vice versa as regards the Chief Justice and puisne Judges of the Supreme Court of the Union of Malaya. In practice, the Court of Appeal, whether sitting in the Union of Malaya or in Singapore, will invariably be composed of the two Chief Justices and one puisne Judge. I see no reason for creating a separate Court of Appeal which would mean the appointment of Appeal Judges with consequential extra expense.

===== Page 376 =====
This suggested constitution of the Supreme Court of the Union of Malaya means that the judicial system of these Malay States, which provide for Malay Judges and Appeal Courts composed of unqualified Malay Judges and a British Adviser (e.g., Perlis and Trengganu), must go. All these unqualified Judges must be replaced by qualified personnel, and all appeals must go to the Court of Appeal of the Union of Malaya.

Death sentences in the Union of Malaya should be considered by the Governor-in-Council and not by the Malay Rulers.

I might say here, in order to give a fuller picture of this judicial system, I consider that the District Judge system in force in the Colony should be applied to the Union of Malaya, and that these appointments of District Judges should be made from the Colonial Legal Service. In fact, I think it is a matter for consideration whether District Courts should be given more extensive jurisdiction than they now possess in the Straits Settlements.

With regard to appeals from the Court of Appeal, at the present time, appeals to the Privy Council are only provided for in the Colony, the Federated Malay States, and in the State of Johore. In the Colony, there is the appeal to the King-in-Council as of right by virtue of the Charter and the constitution of the Straits Settlements as a Crown Colony. The Malay States, being merely protected States, are not subject to Orders-in-Council except at the request of the Rulers thereof.

In 1906 the four Sultans of the Federated Malay States requested the King-in-Council to entertain appeals from the Federated Malay States Court of Appeal in civil matters, and in pursuance of such request the Federated Malay States Order-in-Council, 1906, was promulgated giving effect to the wishes of the Sultans. That Order-in-Council was revoked and replaced by the Federated Malay States Appeal Order-in-Council in 1912 consequent upon the enactment of what is now section 33 of the Courts Enactment (Cap. 2) which reads as follows:

===== Page 377 =====
From any judgment or order of the Court of Appeal in any civil matter, an appeal may be made to His Britannic Majesty in Council, subject to such rules and regulations as may from time to time be prescribed by order of His said Majesty in Council.

The same procedure was followed in Johore in 1921.

The new constitution should provide for appeals from the Court of Appeal of the Union of Malay to the Privy Council.

10. Having dealt with the constitutional set up as regards the Government of the Union of Malay, its Executive and Legislative Councils, the State and Settlement Councils and the Courts, it is now appropriate to discuss the question of the transfer to the Union Government of the property etc. belonging to the various States and Settlements.

As the constitution of the Union of Malay is to be that of a Union and not a Federation, and since the local legislatures in the State and Settlement will be completely subordinate to the central government, and also because the Sultans will be no longer Malay Sultans but will collectively form an Advisory Council to the Governor, I feel that there is no room for any compromises in the transference of State and Settlement property to the Union Government and in the latter assuming full liability for State and Settlement debts. It appears to me that if the new constitution is to be a Union let it be one in practice and not merely on paper.

Accordingly, I have been largely guided by the provisions of the South Africa Act, 1909, in making the following suggestions. I am well aware that the State of Johore is wealthy compared with the other Unfederated Malay States, and that in creating a Union the State of Johore will contribute into the common pool assets greater in extent than those made by other Malay States. Although it will be difficult to persuade the Sultan of Johore to see reason on this, it cannot be

===== Page 378 =====
avoided, because to have an effective Union there can be no exceptions.

It is not possible in a general Memorandum of this kind to provide for every particular matter in the transfer of State and Settlement property to the Union Government, and I merely set out below the main subjects to which consideration must be given.

(a) Revenues.

Under the present system in the Federated Malay States certain revenues are appropriated to finance Federal Services and other revenues remain State. In the new constitution we are not, as stated above, endeavouring to create a greater federation, the aim is a Union and therefore there is no necessity for deciding what revenues shall be retained by the various States and Settlements and what shall go to the Union Government. All revenues, from whatever source arising, over which the States had powers of appropriation at the time of the establishment of the Union must vest in the Union Government. The financial experts may consider it desirable to appoint a commission after the establishment of the Union, to enquire into the financial arrangements which should exist between the Union Government and the various States and Settlements.

(b) Transfer of State and Settlement property.

All cash, bankers' balances, securities for moneys etc. belonging to each State and Settlement at the establishment of the Union must be transferred.

===== Page 379 =====
Referred to the Union Government. If, however, the balance of any funds raised at the establishment of the Union has been by law raised for any special purpose in any State or Settlement, the Union Government must appropriate such balance for the special purpose for which it was raised.

(c) State land, mines and minerals.

By section 8 of the Federated Malay States Land Code (Cap. 138) and by similar provisions in the land laws of the Unfederated Malay States, the entire property in, and control of, State land is vested solely in the Ruler of the State.

As regards the alienation of State land, each British Resident in the Federated Malay States is empowered to alienate it on behalf of the Ruler of the State, and in the Unfederated Malay States although by law the Sultan in Council is the authority having the power to alienate State land, in fact the Sultan in Council has delegated such power to other bodies and persons. At one time I thought it might be unnecessary to amend the law

===== Page 380 =====
Whereby State land is vested in the Ruler of the State, but having read and thought much about a unified constitution, I am of the opinion that all State land, public works, mines and minerals, and all property, whether movable or immovable, and all rights of whatever description belonging to the several States and Settlements must be transferred to the Union Government which will take such property subject to any encumbrances thereon.

(d) Railways, Ports and Harbours.

With regard to the Railways, the Federated Malay States Government owns the entire railway system of Malaya, with the exception of the Johore State Railway which is leased from Johore. The latest lease with Johore was executed on 30th November, 1932, and provides that the Federated Malay States Government shall hold a lease for the Johore State Railway until the 31st December, 1953. Under that lease the Federated Malay States Government pays to Johore a token sum of one dollar per year in advance and also a certain percentage of the net revenue on the working of the Federated Malay States Railway System. If there is a deficit on such a working Johore gets nothing, but, on the other hand, Johore does not have to stand any proportionate share of such deficit.

Under the new constitution the whole railway must be transferred to the Union Government which means that all the capital sunk in the Railway by the Federated Malay States Government and also by the Johore Government accrues to the benefit of the Union Government.

===== Page 381 =====
In recent years, it cannot be said that Johore has been making much profit from their share in the Federated Malay States Railway System, because after 1931, the running of the railway has generally resulted in a deficit, and by the end of 1934, the Railway Reserve Fund had been depleted to such an extent that it only stood at $7,478,164.

Hence, as regards the transfer of the railway to the Union Government, it means a great sacrifice by the Federated Malay States Government, with a correspondingly lesser one by the Johore Government, and a resultant gain to the remainder of the States and Settlements forming the Union.

Subject to examination of the law as to whether the port and harbour property at Penang is vested in the Penang Harbour Board, all ports and harbours in the various States and Settlements must be transferred to the Government of the Union of Malaya.

(e) State and Settlement debts.

The Union of Malaya Government, having assumed ownership of all the property formerly belonging to the various States and Settlements, must, in equity, assume liability for all their debts, and the interest thereon, as they stand at the time of the establishment of the Union.

(f) Financial arrangements between the Straits Settlements and the Government of the Union of Malaya.

Since Penang and Malacca are part of the Straits Settlements and under the new constitution they will be transferred from the jurisdiction of the Government of the Straits Settlements to that of the Government of the Union of Malaya, agreement must be reached as to what is to be the division of the assets and liabilities of these two Settlements between the new Government of the Island of Singapore and the Government of the Union of Malaya.

===== Page 382 =====
Presumably this matter will be decided by agreement between the two Governments.

11. At the establishment of the Union, all the laws of each particular State and Settlement will be in force and until the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya begins to function, there will not be in existence any Union Legislation. Accordingly, the constitution itself must provide that all the laws in force in the several States and Settlements at the establishment of the Union shall continue in force in the respective States and Settlements until repealed or amended by the Legislature of the Union of Malaya.

If the judicial system as suggested in paragraph 9 of this Memorandum is adopted, provision must be made for the pending cases and appeals being taken over by the new Courts from the old Courts.

As regards legislation after the establishment of the Union, one difficulty occurs to me. All the Malay States have not reached an equal stage of development, e.g. Trengganu, Kelentan, and Perlis are behind Kedah and much behind the four Federated Malay States and Johore. In these backward States, there is no legislation on several highly technical subjects, e.g. bills of exchange, and it will not be desirable to legislate on such subjects in these backward States for some time to come.

Interpretation of Constitution.

12. I have carried out a certain amount of research as to whether the Courts should be given express power to interpret the constitution or whether the granting of such express power is unnecessary.

I do not think there can be any doubt that the Courts must have power to interpret the constitution should the occasion arise.

===== Page 383 =====
In South Africa and Canada there is no law expressly conferring power on the Courts to interpret the constitution and yet numerous decided cases show that there is no doubt that the Courts in those two Dominions have assumed such jurisdiction.

On the other hand, in Australia, section 76 of the Commonwealth of Australia Constitution Act provides (inter alia) that Parliament may make laws conferring jurisdiction on the High Court on any matter arising under the constitution or involving its interpretation.

By section 30 of the Commonwealth Judiciary Act, 1905–1934, the High Court is given original jurisdiction in all matters arising under the constitution and involving its interpretation. Thus, in Australia the Federal High Court has been given express powers to interpret the constitution.

In the Colonies the most useful case I can find on this matter is Jerusalem–Jaffa District Governor v Sulciman Marra (1926) A.C. 321, in which it was held by the Privy Council that it was the right and duty of the Supreme Court of Palestine to consider whether an Ordinance was consistent with the terms of the Mandate.

I have not had time to go into this matter in detail but incline to the view that, for the avoidance of doubts, it is preferable to include express provision in the constitution empowering the Courts to interpret it, and express provision must be included to the effect that, in any case of conflict, Ordinances of the Union of Malaya must prevail over enactments of State and Settlement Councils.

13. Paragraph 15 of the Secret Paper states –

"The complete details of the application of this policy and in particular the detailed measures to be designed for the creation of a self-governing institution can only be worked out on the spot after re-occupation in the light of the prevailing conditions."

Knowing the mentality of the Sultans and the necessity for getting the new treaties executed as quickly as possible after the re-occupation of

===== Page 385 =====
FUTURE CONSTITUTION FOR MALAY PENINSULA.

1. In the War Cabinet Secret Paper CMB(44) dated 14th January, 1944, the Secretary of State for the Colonies sets out, in general terms, the future constitutional policy for British Colonial Territories in South East Asia. It is clear that as regards Malaya the aim is -

(a) A separate Government for Singapore Island.

(b) The Union of the Four Federated Malay States, the five Unfederated Malay States and the Settlements of Penang and Malacca.

2. Before discussing a new constitutional policy for Malaya it is as well to state briefly what is the present constitution. Malaya is divided into three parts -

(a) The Straits Settlements, a Crown Colony, for which His Majesty is able to legislate under the Straits Settlements Act, 1866, and the British Settlements Act, 1887.

(b) The four Malay States of Perak, Selangor, Negri Sembilan and Pahang over which His Majesty has no powers under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act. These States formed themselves into a Federation in 1896, and in 1909 a Federal Council was established which enacted legislation having the force of law throughout the Federation.

(c) The five Unfederated Malay States of Johore, Kedah, Kelantan, Trengganu and Perlis. Each of these States is independent and His Majesty has no powers under the Foreign Jurisdiction Act over any of them. Each such State has its own legislature, namely the Ruler in State Council.

All the nine Malay States are bound to Great Britain by treaties which date from 1874, in the case of Perak, to 1930, in the case of Perlis. The provisions of these treaties follow the same general lines, namely that each Malay Ruler shall accept a British officer whose advice shall be asked

===== Page 386 =====
In short, as Bagehot has stated, a Second Chamber "Besides independence to revise judicially and power to revise effectively, has leisure to revise intellectually." The Sultans, either individually or collectively, do not possess the necessary qualifications to perform effectively the real functions of a Second Chamber and on this account, and on account of the difficulties I have just mentioned, I am satisfied that any such Second Chamber is impracticable. In any case, it would be impolitic to attempt to find it a place within the scheme of a unified constitution for the Malay Peninsula.

But, some place must be found for the Sultans in that constitution, because I consider they will not be satisfied with being informed, to quote from paragraph 14 of the Secret Paper, that their position is merely that of "natural leaders of their Malay people within their State territories."

A provision common to all the treaties between His Majesty's Government and the Sultans is that on matters of Malay Religion and Custom the Sultan is not bound to ask and accept the advice of the Resident or Adviser. These are matters which only affect the Malay community of the Malay States and, as regards the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, which are British territory, such provisions are not relevant. It is right and proper that on those matters the Governor in the Union Government should have the advice of the Sultans as a body. Accordingly the new constitution should provide for the establishment of a Council composed of all the Sultans (the term "Malay Rulers" must be abandoned) which should be a deliberative, consultative and advisory, but not an executive, body, with the Governor, or in his absence the Officer Administering the Government, as its President. On account of the fact that the Yang Tuan of Negri Sembilan and the Raja of Perlis are not Sultans this Council should have a Malay title as it would not be politic to give it the title in English "Council of Sultans". The main functions of this Council should be -

(a) To advise the Governor on all matters affecting Mohammedan religion and Malay custom, before any such matter is discussed or dealt with by the Union

===== Page 387 =====
Legislature, and the Governor should be bound to have such matters discussed in the Council of Sultans in the first instance.

(b) To advise the Governor on any other matter which the Governor decides ought to be discussed in the Council of Sultans.

(c) To discuss any matter proposed for discussion by any Sultan, provided the Governor approves its discussion.

To restrict the functions of this Council to those mentioned in (a) alone would not satisfy the Sultans, and I see no harm in including (b) and (c).

As the relationship between His Majesty's Government and each Sultan is bound by treaty, the following matters should be debarred from discussion in the Council:

(a) Treaties, rights, dignities and privileges of each Sultan and members of his family, and the actions of each individual Sultan.

(b) The internal affairs of any individual State or Settlement which are properly within the purview of a State or Settlement Council.

It should be stated clearly that the establishment of this Council shall not prejudice in any way the treaty relations (by this I mean the new treaty arrangements) between His Majesty's Government and each Sultan, nor should the right of direct correspondence and access between any Sultan and the Governor be in any way impaired.

Such a Council should meet at regular intervals and should be opened with ceremony in keeping with the prestige of the Sultans.

Complete records of all Council Meetings should be kept in the English and Malay languages.

Council Meetings should not be public, but communiques to the press should be issued after each Meeting.

If such a Council is established with due ceremony and with the intention that the Governor should look upon it as a permanent consultative and advisory body, this ought to go a long way towards

===== Page 388 =====
Appeasing the Sultans, especially those of the Unfederated Malay States, for the loss of many of their present powers. With regard to the loss of those powers, it is appropriate here to suggest that immediately on the re-occupation of Malaya, the policy of the Military Government should be to stress the fact that the immediate Government of Malaya is a British Military Administration and give the appearance that if the Sultans act in a reasonable manner, His Majesty's Government will restore to them such of their former rights, dignities and privileges as are consonant with the proposed new constitution. This policy should tend to give the impression that it is the Sultans, rather than His Majesty's Government, who are seeking concessions.

If the new constitution is accepted by the Sultans, the Council of Sultans will form a valuable consultative and advisory body to which the Governor and the Central Government can look for advice, particularly on matters touching Malay Religion and Custom, because such advice will be collective rather than individual. In fact each Sultan, as a member of this Council, will play his part in a wider sphere than hitherto. It should be pointed out to the Sultans that in the post war world the policy, not only of Great Britain but also of other nations, will be to deal with affairs on a regional basis rather than by individual States.

The rules of business for the Council should be framed by the Governor after consultation with the Sultans, and no meeting of the Council should be held unless the Governor or the Officer Administering the Government can attend, as it would be impolitic to hold any meeting with the Senior Sultan presiding.

I now revert to the Secretary of State's letter to which reference has been made at the beginning of this paragraph immediately following the citation of the relevant paragraphs from the Secret Paper CMB(44)3.

===== Page 389 =====
Government and, in any case, he must be subordinate to the Chief Secretary. I suggest that the titles of the posts to be held by the senior executive officers in the various States and Settlements should be changed from "British Resident", in the Federated Malay States, "British Adviser" ("General Adviser" in Johore) in the Unfederated Malay States, and "Resident Councillor" in the Settlements of Penang and Malacca, to "Resident Commissioner" in each State and Settlement. The term "State Commissioner" would not be appropriate because in Johore certain Malay officials are called by that title. It will not be possible to have the eleven Resident Commissioners as members of Executive Council since their status will be less than that of the former British Residents, British Advisers and Resident Councillors. In any case their inclusion as members of Executive Council would not be appropriate in the unified constitution.

There should be five official members of Executive Council. Only the Chief Secretary, Attorney-General and Financial Secretary should be ex-officio, leaving two other officials to be nominated by the Governor.

There should be an equal number of unofficial members of Executive Council but it would be preferable not to prescribe such unofficial representatives on a racial basis. In the first instance, however, it is desirable that there should be one European, one Malay, one Chinese and one Indian unofficial member on Executive Council, leaving the Governor free to nominate the most suitable unofficial to fill the remaining vacancy.

It is not necessary to lay down that the membership of Executive Council should be restricted to members of Legislative Council, though this is the ideal to be aimed at eventually.

The question arises whether membership of Executive Council should be restricted to persons who are Malayan Union Citizens. This may create difficulties in the early stages as regards Europeans, and this matter requires further consideration in relation to the Malayan Union Citizenship Directive.

This will give an Executive Council consisting of the Governor and ten members.

===== Page 390 =====
(B) Legislative Council

I assume that the unofficial members of the Legislative Council of the Union would be nominated because, in its present stage of development, the Malay Peninsula is unsuited to the electoral system.

Under the present constitution, the Federal Council of the Federated Malay States consists of:

High Commissioner - President.

Official Members:
- Resident of Perak
- Resident of Selangor
- Resident of Negri Sembilan
- Resident of Pahang
- Federal Secretary
- Legal Adviser
- Financial Secretary
- Secretary for Chinese Affairs
- Controller of Labour
- General Manager, F.M.S. Railways
- Adviser on Agriculture
- Comptroller of Customs
- Adviser on Medical Services
- Adviser on Education
- Additional member (Adviser on Public Works)

Unofficial Members:
- 5 Europeans
- 4 Malays
- 2 Chinese
- 1 Indian

Hence, in addition to the Governor, there are at present fifteen official and twelve unofficial members of the Federal Council.

Under the new constitution, unofficial representation on the Legislative Council should be on a population basis as far as possible. To this end, it is convenient to divide the Malay Peninsula into the following areas:

1. Kedah and Perlis
2. Penang and Province Wellesley
3. Perak
4. Selangor and Pahang
5. Negri Sembilan and Malacca
6. Johore
7. Kelantan and Trengganu

===== Page 391 =====
The estimated population of these areas, by race, as on the 30th June, 1941, is:

| Area                         | Malays | Europeans | Eurasians | Chinese  | Indians | Others | Total   |
|------------------------------|--------|-----------|-----------|----------|---------|--------|---------|
| 1. Kedah & Perlis             | 387,735| 674       | 151       | 116,672  | 62,025  | 16,051 | 583,308 |
| 2. Penang & Province Wellesley|119,913| 2,853     | 2,682     | 230,679  | 60,503  | 2,437  | 419,047 |
| 3. Perak                     | 335,385| 4,113     | 1,990     | 450,197  | 196,056 | 5,350  | 992,691 |
| 4. Selangor & Pahang         | 281,236| 5,606     | 2,831     | 413,632  | 210,730 | 9,317  | 923,352 |
| 5. Negri Sembilan & Malacca  | 217,912| 2,029     | 3,346     | 217,931  | 87,552  | 3,326  | 532,096 |
| 6. Johore                   | 302,104| 1,264     | 352       | 308,901  | 58,498  | 4,178  | 673,297 |
| 7. Kelantan & Trengganu      | 555,836| 262       | 57        | 40,319   | 9,000   | 8,250  | 613,724 |

It is clear from the above figures that for the areas mentioned the nominated unofficials, in every case, will be Malays and Chinese, therefore some means must be evolved whereby adequate representation can be afforded to Europeans, Indians and Eurasians. This could be done by fixing the total number of unofficial members of Legislative Council at twenty-one, out of which fourteen members would be nominated to represent the above mentioned areas on a population basis. The remaining seven unofficial members would be nominated to represent important interests throughout the Union, thereby affording a suitable method of securing adequate representation for European, Indian and Eurasian interests.

There should be twenty-one official members of Legislative Council of whom five would be the official members of Executive Council.

(0) Temporary Advisory Council.

When Military Administration ceases and the new Civil Government is established in the Malay Peninsula it will be impossible for that Government to set up a Legislative Council immediately. To cover the interregnum between the end of the Military Administration and the setting up of a Legislative Council the new constitution should specifically provide for the establishment of an Advisory Council to the Governor which would take the place

===== Page 392 =====
Temporarily of a Legislative Council. This Advisory Council should consist of an unspecified number of official and unofficial members. During that temporary period, the Executive Council, consisting of five official and five unofficial members, would be in existence and function as it would when the Civil Government is established.

It is probable that the Chief Civil Affairs Officer will set up, during the period of Military Administration, an Advisory Council on which there will be unofficial representation, thus affording an opportunity for sarmanding unofficials who would be suitable to serve on the Governor's Advisory Council.

Nomenclature.

(B) Nomenclature.

Suitable terms must be thought of as to what the Union Government and its Legislature are to be called. In order to stress that it is a Union the following would appear to be appropriate terms:
(a) Union of Malaya.
(b) Government of the Union of Malaya.
(c) Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya.

The use of the term "Malaya" is not strictly accurate because Malaya includes Singapore, which is excluded from the Union, but this minor inaccurate geographical expression should be condoned in favour of stressing that the object is the unification of Malaya.

8. Reference has already been made to State Councils in the Federated Malay States being reconstituted in 1932. Perak and Negri Sembilan adopted the bicameral system, whereas in Selangor and Pahang there was only one chamber.

Putting aside the bicameral system in Perak and Negri Sembilan, the general composition of a State Council in the Federated Malay States was that it was presided over by the Sultan, with the British Resident, Malay Chiefs, State officials, unofficial Europeans, Malays, Chinese and Indians, as members.

Under the new constitution the Sultan must drop out and the President of a State or Settlement Council must be the Resident Commissioner. It is difficult to say now what the actual membership of a State or Settlement Council should be, but the members of those Councils should be appointed by the Governor on the advice of the Resident Commissioner. As regards the nomination of unofficial members some consideration should be paid to the racial population of each State and Settlement, but I would not make this the only guide.

===== Page 393 =====
In view of the fact that the Sultans will have nothing to do with the State Councils, it is advisable that an Advisory Council to the Sultan should be established in each State with appropriate functions and that such Council should be presided over by the Sultan.

The functions of that Advisory Council should be:

(a) To advise the Sultan on all matters affecting Mohammedan religion and Malay custom.

(b) To advise the Sultan on any other matter which the Resident Commissioner, with the approval of the Governor, refers to this Advisory Council.

As regards the functions of the State and Settlement Councils, paragraph 11 of the Secret Paper, quoted in paragraph 5 of this Memorandum, states:

"Subject to the new central authority, the several States and Settlements would be empowered to deal with such local affairs as may be devolved upon them by the central authority."

If by central authority is meant the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya, I beg to differ because in my view that would unnecessarily restrict the functions of a State or Settlement Council.

I suggest that each State and Settlement Council should be empowered to legislate:

(a) On all matters which, in the opinion of the Governor-in-Council, are of a merely local or private nature in the State or Settlement.

(For instance, the Malayan Collieries Enactment, enacted by the State Council of Selangor, deals solely with the Malayan Collieries Ltd. building a private railway inside the State of Selangor.)

Before the Governor-in-Council decides whether a matter requiring legislation should be enacted by a State or Settlement Council, he must first be satisfied by a certificate signed by the Attorney-General that it is a fit and proper matter for legislation by such a Council.

Having so decided, the Clerk to Executive Council will sign a certificate to that effect.

(b) On all subjects in respect of which the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya by law delegates the power of making laws to a State or Settlement Council.

(Laws made by a State or Settlement Council under this provision would be in the nature of rules or regulations etc. made under Ordinances passed by the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya. I use the word "Ordinance" because I consider that it is the appropriate term to use for laws passed by the Legislative Council. The present word "Enactment" is more applicable to the legislative acts of a local body, and accordingly I suggest that the laws passed by State or Settlement Council should be termed "Enactments".)

===== Page 394 =====
As regards other business of the State and Settlement Councils, I suggest that they should be empowered to discuss questions of a purely local nature. Provision can be made in their constitutions restricting discussions on other business to matters of a local nature. Such restrictions can be achieved by a provision analogous to the usual clause contained in Instructions under the Sign Manual and Signet issued to a Governor, in which it is laid down that the Governor alone is entitled to submit questions to Executive Council. If Resident Commissioners are given similar powers, there should be no danger of discussions in State and Settlement Councils extending to subjects which extend beyond local matters.

9. A re-organisation of the Courts will be necessary consequent on the formation of a Union. Before discussing what form this re-organisation should take, it is desirable to set out briefly the present constitution of the Supreme Courts throughout Malaya.

The Supreme Court of the Colony is constituted by the Courts Ordinance (Cap. 10) and exercises within the Colony the same jurisdiction and authority as are held by His Majesty's High Court of Justice in England. Its Judges are appointed by His Majesty by Letters Patent under the Public Seal of the Colony. Hence those Judges are His Majesty's Judges functioning in the King's Courts.

The Supreme Court of the Federated Malay States is constituted by the Courts Enactment (Cap. 2) and comprises the Court of Appeal and the Courts of a Judge. The Court

===== Page 395 =====
In view of the fact that the Island of Singapore and the rest of Malaya are to be two separate entities, with separate Governments, I see no useful purpose in having a Supreme Court for the whole of Malaya.

I realise that some of the executive departments in the new constitution will be pan-Malayan, but the reason for this is to keep policy uniform throughout Malaya on vital matters affecting the whole Union. The Court and its Judges are not concerned with any question of policy.

Accordingly I suggest that there should be a separate High Court of the Union of Malaya with a Chief Justice at its head. The term "High Court" is used because it appears to be the custom to reserve the term "Supreme Court" for Crown Colonies. The Chief Justice will have sufficient work to do in re-organising the judicial system of all the Malay States and the two Settlements of Penang and Malacca, without having any responsibility for the Island of Singapore. In the first instance, a lot of his time will be taken up in travelling in order to get acquainted with local conditions, particularly in the Unfederated Malay States.

In addition to the Chief Justice there must be a number of Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya stationed at various centres throughout the country. I need not go into the question of how many puisne Judges will be required or where they should be stationed.

In accordance with previous practice the Chief Justice and the puisne Judges of the Supreme Court in Singapore should be declared ex officio Judges of the High Court of the Union of Malaya, and vice versa as regards the Chief Justice and puisne Judges of the Supreme Court of the Union of Malaya. In practice the Court of Appeal, whether sitting in the Union of Malaya or in Singapore, will invariably be composed of the two Chief Justices and one puisne Judge. I see no reason for creating a separate Court of Appeal which would mean the appointment of Appeal Judges with consequential extra expense.

===== Page 396 =====
This suggested constitution of the Supreme Court of the Union of Malaya means that the judicial system of those Malay States which provide for Malay Judges and Appeal Courts composed of unqualified Malay Judges and a British Adviser (e.g., Perlis and Trenggam) must go. All these unqualified Judges must be replaced by qualified personnel, and all appeals must go to the Court of Appeal of the Union of Malaya.

Death sentences in the Union of Malaya should be considered by the Governor-in-Council and not by the Malay Rulers.

I might say here, in order to give a fuller picture of this judicial system, I consider that the District Judge system in force in the Colony should be applied to the Union of Malaya, and that these appointments of District Judges should be made from the Colonial Legal Service. In fact, I think it is a matter for consideration whether District Courts should be given more extensive jurisdiction than they now possess in the Straits Settlements.

With regard to appeals from the Court of Appeal, at the present time, appeals to the Privy Council are only provided for in the Colony, the Federated Malay States, and in the State of Johore. In the Colony, there is the appeal to the King-in-Council as of right by virtue of the Charter and the constitution of the Straits Settlements as a Crown Colony. The Malay States being merely protected States are not subject to Orders-in-Council except at the request of the Rulers thereof.

In 1906, the four Sultans of the Federated Malay States requested the King-in-Council to entertain appeals from the Federated Malay States Court of Appeal in civil matters, and in pursuance of such request, the Federated Malay States Order-in-Council, 1906, was promulgated giving effect to the wishes of the Sultans. That Order-in-Council was revoked and replaced by the Federated Malay States Appeal Order-in-Council in 1912 consequent upon the enactment of what is now section 33 of the Courts Instrument (Cap. 2) which reads as follows:

===== Page 397 =====
From any judgment or order of the Court of Appeal in any civil matter, an appeal may be made to His Britannic Majesty in Council, subject to such rules and regulations as may from time to time be prescribed by order of His said Majesty in Council.

The same procedure was followed in Johore in 1921.

The new constitution should provide for appeals from the Court of Appeal of the Union of Malaya to the Privy Council.

10. Having dealt with the constitutional setup as regards the Government of the Union of Malaya, its Executive and Legislative Councils, the State and Settlement Councils, and the Courts, it is now appropriate to discuss the question of the transfer to the Union Government of the property, etc., belonging to the various States and Settlements.

As the constitution of the Union of Malaya is to be that of a Union and not a Federation, and since the local legislatures in the State and Settlement will be completely subordinate to the central government, and also because the Sultans will be no longer Malay Rulers but will collectively form an Advisory Council to the Governor, I feel that there is no room for any compromises in the transfer of State and Settlement property to the Union Government and in the latter assuming full liability for State and Settlement debts. It appears to me that if the new constitution is to be a Union, let it be one in practice and not merely on paper.

Accordingly, I have been largely guided by the provisions of the South Africa Act, 1909, in making the following suggestions. I am well aware that the State of Johore is wealthy compared with the other unfederated Malay States, and that in creating a Union, the State of Johore will contribute into the common pool assets greater in extent than those made by other Malay States. Although it will be difficult to persuade the Sultan of Johore to see reason on this, it cannot be

===== Page 398 =====
avoided, because to have an effective Union there can be no exceptions.

It is not possible in a general Memorandum of this kind to provide for every particular matter in the transfer of State and Settlement property to the Union Government, and I merely set out below the main subjects to which consideration must be given.

(a) Revenues.

Under the present system in the Federated Malay States certain revenues are appropriated to finance Federal Services and other revenues remain State. In the new constitution we are not, as stated above, endeavouring to create a greater federation, the aim is a Union and therefore there is no necessity for deciding what revenues shall be retained by the various States and Settlements and what shall go to the Union Government. All revenues, from whatever source arising, over which the States had powers of appropriation at the time of the establishment of the Union must vest in the Union Government. The financial experts may consider it desirable to appoint a commission after the establishment of the Union, to enquire into the financial arrangements which should exist between the Union Government and the various States and Settlements.

(b) Transfer of State and Settlement property.

All cash, bankers' balances, securities for moneys etc. belonging to each State and Settlement at the establishment of the Union must be transferred

===== Page 399 =====
Referred to the Union Government. If, however, the balance of any funds raised at the establishment of the Union has been by law raised for any special purpose in any State or Settlement, the Union Government must appropriate such balance for the special purpose for which it was raised.

(c) State land, mines and minerals.

By section 8 of the Federated Malay States Land Code (Cap. 138) and by similar provisions in the land laws of the Unfederated Malay States, the entire property in, and control of, State land is vested solely in the Ruler of the State.

As regards the alienation of State land, each British Resident in the Federated Malay States is empowered to alienate it on behalf of the Ruler of the State, and in the Unfederated Malay States although by law the Sultan in Council is the authority having the power to alienate State land, in fact the Sultan in Council has delegated such power to other bodies and persons. At one time I thought it might be unnecessary to amend the law

===== Page 400 =====
Whereby State land is vested in the Ruler of the State, but having read and thought much about a unified constitution, I am of the opinion that all State land, public works, mines and minerals, and all property, whether moveable or immovable, and all rights of whatever description belonging to the several States and Settlements must be transferred to the Union Government which will take such property subject to any encumbrances thereon.

(d) Railways, Ports and Harbours.

With regard to the Railways, the Federated Malay States Government owns the entire railway system of Malaya, with the exception of the Johore State Railway which is leased from Johore. The latest lease with Johore was executed on 30th November, 1932, and provides that the Federated Malay States Government shall hold a lease for the Johore State Railway until the 31st December, 1953. Under that lease, the Federated Malay States Government pays to Johore a token sum of one dollar per year in advance and also a certain percentage of the net revenue on the working of the Federated Malay States Railway System. If there is a deficit on such a working, Johore gets nothing, but, on the other hand, Johore does not have to stand any proportionate share of such deficit.

Under the new constitution, the whole railway must be transferred to the Union Government which means that all the capital sunk in the Railway by the Federated Malay States Government and also by the Johore Government accrues to the benefit of the Union Government.

===== Page 401 =====
In recent years, it cannot be said that Johore has been making much profit from their share in the Federated Malay States Railway System, because after 1931, the running of the railway has generally resulted in a deficit, and by the end of 1934 the Railway Renewals Reserve Fund had been depleted to such an extent that it only stood at 87,478,164.

Hence, as regards the transfer of the railway to the Union Government, it means a great sacrifice by the Federated Malay States Government, with a correspondingly lesser one by the Johore Government, and a resultant gain to the remainder of the States and Settlements forming the Union.

Subject to examination of the law as to whether the port and harbour property at Penang is vested in the Penang Harbour Board, all ports and harbours in the various States and Settlements must be transferred to the Government of the Union of Malaya.

(e) State and Settlement debts.

The Union of Malaya Government, having assumed ownership of all the property formerly belonging to the various States and Settlements, must, in equity, assume liability for all their debts and the interest thereon, as they stand at the time of the establishment of the Union.

(f) Financial arrangements between the Straits Settlements and the Government of the Union of Malaya.

Since Penang and Malacca are part of the Straits Settlements and under the new constitution they will be transferred from the jurisdiction of the Government of the Straits Settlements to that of the Government of the Union of Malaya, agreement must be reached as to what is to be the division of the assets and liabilities of these two Settlements between the new Government of the Island of Singapore and the Government of the Union of Malaya.

===== Page 402 =====
Presumably this matter will be decided by agreement between the two Governments.

11. At the establishment of the Union all the laws of each particular State and Settlement will be in force and until the Legislative Council of the Union of Malaya begins to function there will not be in existence any Union Legislation. Accordingly, the constitution itself must provide that all the laws in force in the several States and Settlements at the establishment of the Union shall continue in force in the respective States and Settlements until repealed or amended by the Legislature of the Union of Malaya.

If the judicial system as suggested in paragraph 9 of this Memorandum is adopted, provision must be made for the pending cases and appeals being taken over by the new Courts from the old Courts.

As regards legislation after the establishment of the Union, one difficulty occurs to me. All the Malay States have not reached an equal stage of development, e.g., Trengganu, Kelantan, and Perlis are behind Kedah and much behind the four Federated Malay States and Johore. In these backward States, there is no legislation on several highly technical subjects, e.g., bills of exchange, and it will not be desirable to legislate on such subjects in these backward States for some time to come.

(Interpretation of Constitution.)

12. I have carried out a certain amount of research as to whether the Courts should be given express power to interpret the constitution or whether the granting of such express power is unnecessary.

I do not think there can be any doubt that the Courts must have power to interpret the constitution should the occasion arise.

===== Page 403 =====
In South Africa and Canada, there is no law expressly conferring power on the Courts to interpret the constitution, and yet numerous decided cases show that there is no doubt that the Courts in those two Dominions have assumed such jurisdiction.

On the other hand, in Australia, section 76 of the Commonwealth of Australia Constitution Act provides (inter alia) that Parliament may make laws conferring jurisdiction on the High Court on any matter arising under the constitution or involving its interpretation.

By section 30 of the Commonwealth Judiciary Act, 1903-1934, the High Court is given original jurisdiction in all matters arising under the constitution and involving its interpretation. Thus, in Australia, the Federal High Court has been given express powers to interpret the constitution.

In the Colonies, the most useful case I can find on this matter is Jerusalem - Jaffa District Governor v Suleiman Murra (1926) A.C. 321, in which it was held by the Privy Council that it was the right and duty of the Supreme Court of Palestine to consider whether an Ordinance was consistent with the terms of the Mandate.

I have not had time to go into this matter in detail but incline to the view that, for the avoidance of doubts, it is preferable to include express provision in the constitution empowering the Courts to interpret it, and express provision must be included to the effect that, in any case of conflict, Ordinances of the Union of Malaya must prevail over Enactments of State and Settlement Councils.

13. Paragraph 15 of the Secret Paper states:

"The complete details of the application of this policy and in particular the detailed measures to be designed for the creation of a self-governing institution can only be worked out on the spot after re-occupation in the light of the prevailing conditions."

Knowing the mentality of the Sultans and the necessity for getting the new treaties executed as quickly as possible after the re-occupation of

===== Page 404 =====
The War Office,

TOP SECRET.

TO: G.E.J. Gent Esq. C.M.G. O.B.E. M.C.

Subject: Force 136 - Malaya.

Herewith 1 copy of a note on Force 136 compiled by DGCAO (M) Rear Headquarters SAGSEA.

Tel: CAL: 5685/453.

===== Page 410 =====
FORCE 136.

On 29.5.45 Lt.Col. Johnston-Smith of Force 136 brought a Mr. Stewart (a businessman with long residence in Burma) to see me. Mr. Stewart has been connected with the activities of Force 136 in Burma for some time. He described to me the activities of this Force in Burma and also the consequential results in those parts of Burma which have been liberated.

2. The Burmese, under British Officers, are very anti-Japanese but, at the same time, they are not pro-British. They have a strong Burma nationalistic outlook and their motto is "Burma for the Burmese". Mr. Stewart stressed that they have been very loyal to their British Officers because they well realise that it is more dangerous for a Britisher in that Force than it is for a Burmese. He went on to say that in his view, if these Burmese members of Force 136 are handled tactfully and sympathetically by CAS(B) and later by the Civil Government of Burma, they would not cause any trouble. If, on the other hand, their aspirations towards Burmese independence is turned down flat and they do not receive tactful and sympathetic treatment they might easily cause a lot of trouble.

3. Having heard Mr. Stewart, I considered the problem in Malaya.

The members of Force 136 in Malaya are exclusively Chinese with British Officers. These Chinese have faced dangers and privations in Malaya in their efforts to sabotage the Japanese war effort. They can have no idea, like the Burmese, of endeavouring to obtain Chinese independence in Malaya, because it is not their country. But in the light of their poor political status in Malaya in the past they will at least expect some betterment of their political position as a reward for their efforts against the Japanese.

If when I wrote my Memorandum on the Future Constitution for the Malay Peninsula I had known as much about Force 136 as I know now I would have included the activities of its Chinese members as an added reason for the creation of a Union.

4. If the proposals for a Malayan Union and the consequential granting of a Malayan Citizenship are implemented they will be a complete answer to any demands which these Chinese members of Force 136 may make.

1 June 45.

Brigadier
DCCA0(M)
Rear Hdqrs Sacsea.
